10058{_}785{_}A nocturn, a round of Psalms, was said on Saturday night by the vigilants preparing for the Sunday services.
10058{_}786{_}Before the eighth century two other short nocturns were added.
10058{_}787{_}This addition, which was copied from the monastic practice, built up the three nocturn form of office and became the model and form of the office for saints.
10058{_}788{_}"There is good reason for believing that originally the Divine Office formed part of the Mass.
10058{_}789{_}The _synaxis_, for which the early Christians assembled by night, consisted of the 'breaking of bread,' preceded by the singing of psalms and hymns, litanies and collects, readings, homilies, invocations and canticles.
10058{_}790{_}This was the whole official liturgical prayer, apart, of course, from private prayer" (Dom Cabrol, _Day Hours of the Church_, Introduction, p. xvi).
10058{_}791{_}One of the chief objects of Pope Pius X. in his reform was the restoration of the liturgical importance of the Sunday office, the office of the Lord's Day, and, therefore, in its own right, superior to the saints' feasts by which it had been displaced from its special office, psalms and lessons.
10058{_}792{_}And this could only be effected by a change in the rules of occurrence, and in Title IV.
10058{_}793{_}(_De Festorum occurentia_, etc., section 2) we find the new rule for restoring Sunday offices to their proper liturgical rights.
10058{_}794{_}In Title IV., sect, 1 (see Breviary, Additiones and Variationes) there is no change in the old rubric.
10058{_}795{_}The eight Sundays of the first class exclude every other feast.
10058{_}796{_}And the Sundays of the second class only give place to a double of the first class and then are commemorated at Lauds, Vespers and Mass, and have the ninth lesson in Matins.
10058{_}797{_}But section 2 (_Dominicis minoribus_)... goes to the root of the matter of the new change in the rules for Sunday's liturgical office.
10058{_}798{_}The ordinary Sundays ranked as semi-doubles and hence their Mass and Office was superseded by the Mass and Office of some occurring feast.
10058{_}799{_}The length of the Sunday office, in the breviaries until lately in use, made many hearts rejoice over the occurring feast.
10058{_}800{_}But the almost total omission of the ancient and beautiful Sunday Masses was a misfortune and, in a sense, an unbecoming practice, which broke away from ancient liturgical rule and tradition.
10058{_}801{_}The abbreviation of the Sunday office in the new breviaries and the rule laid down in Title IV., sect.
10058{_}802{_}2, restore Sunday's office and Sunday's Mass to their old and proper dignity.
10058{_}803{_}The general rule laid down is that on Sundays throughout the year the proper office of the Sunday shall always be said.
10058{_}804{_}The exceptions are (1) Feasts of our Lord and their octaves, (2) Doubles of the first class, (3) Doubles of the second class.
10058{_}805{_}On these days the office will be the office of the feast, with commemoration in Lauds, Vespers and Mass.
10058{_}806{_}Henceforth Sundays are divided into:  (1) Sundays of the first class, which exclude all feasts;  (2) Sundays of the second class, which exclude all feasts save doubles of the first class;  (3) The ordinary Sundays, which exclude all but doubles of the first or second class, feasts of our Lord, and their octave days.
10058{_}807{_}The date of Easter is the pivot of Calendar construction.
10058{_}808{_}Before Easter come the Sundays of Lent and Quinquagesima, Sexagesima, Septuagesima Sundays.
10058{_}809{_}Septuagesima cannot fall earlier than the eighteenth day of January, nor later than the twenty-second day of February.
10058{_}810{_}Hence, in some years there are fewer "Sundays after the Epiphany" than in others, owing to the dates of Easter and Septuagesima.
10058{_}811{_}The smaller the number of Sundays after Epiphany the greater is the number of Sundays after Pentecost.
10058{_}812{_}If the number of Sundays after Pentecost be twenty-five, the twenty-fourth Sunday will have the office of the sixth Sunday after Epiphany.
10058{_}813{_}If there be twenty-six Sundays after Pentecost, the twenty-fourth Sunday will have the office of the fifth after Epiphany, and the twenty-fifth will have that of the fifth Sunday; the twenty-sixth will be the sixth Sunday's office.
10058{_}814{_}It should be remembered that the Sunday called the twenty-fourth after Pentecost is _always_ celebrated immediately before the first Sunday of Advent, even though it should not be even the twenty-third Sunday after Pentecost.
10058{_}815{_}TITLE V.--FERIAL OFFICE.
10058{_}816{_}_Etymology and different signification_ of the word _Feria_.
10058{_}817{_}The word is derived probably from the Latin _feriari_ (to rest).
10058{_}818{_}Among the Romans, the idea of a day of rest and a holy day was intimately united and received the name of _feria_.
10058{_}819{_}But it was amongst the Hebrews that the day set apart for the worship of God received the most distinctive character as day of rest (_Heortology_, p. 2).
10058{_}820{_}Hence the early Christians called the days of the week _feriae_.
10058{_}821{_}Why did the Church adopt the word _feriae_?
10058{_}822{_}She wished to mark the day of the week and not to name them by their pagan name (_e.g., dies lunae_) nor by their Jewish names (_e.g., prima sabbati_), which should be a sort of recognition of the dead and dying synagogue.
10058{_}823{_}Hence she adopted the word _feria_, to denote the Christian rest in the Lord, the Christian peace and the abstinence from all sin, and that each and every day should be consecrated to God.
10058{_}824{_}The Christian use of the word is found in Origen (185-254) and was fully established in the time of Tertullian.
10058{_}825{_}In the time of Amalare (circa 830) the ferial office had taken a well-defined form, Matins having twelve psalms and six antiphons.
10058{_}826{_}In Lauds of every _feria_ were recited the psalms, _Miserere; Deus, Deus meus; Deus misereatur nostri_; a canticle drawn from a prophet and varying each day of the week (_e.g., Confitebor_, Isaias xii., for Monday's Lauds; _Ego dixi_, Isaias xxxviii., for Tuesday's Lauds, etc., and the two psalms _Laudate_ (148, 150) and the _Cantate_, psalm 149).
10058{_}827{_}In the small hours the Sunday psalms without antiphons were recited.
10058{_}828{_}Vespers had daily, fixed psalms.
10058{_}829{_}At each hour the _Kyrie Eleison_ and ferial _prayers_ were said on bended knees and the hours terminated--as do the hours of Holy Week still--with _Pater Noster and Miserere_.
10058{_}830{_}Ferias are divided into three classes, major ferias, privileged ferias and non-privileged.
10058{_}831{_}Ash Wednesday and the three last days of Holy Week are the major ferias which are privileged and exclude all feasts (_vide_ Tit.
10058{_}832{_}II., sec.
10058{_}833{_}2).
10058{_}834{_}Non-privileged feriae are the feriae of Lent and Advent, Quarter Tense or Ember days and Rogation Monday.
10058{_}835{_}They take precedence of simple feasts only.
10058{_}836{_}In the ferial office nine psalms are said, and not twelve, as in the old order of the Breviary.
10058{_}837{_}The psalms found arranged in the new Breviary for three nocturns are to be said with nine antiphons up to the versicle of third nocturn--the versicle of the first and second being omitted (Tit.
10058{_}838{_}I., sec.
10058{_}839{_}7).
10058{_}840{_}Hence the psalms are to be said straight through (_sine interuptione_) omitting in the first two nocturns, the versicle and response, Pater Noster, absolutions and all pertaining to the lessons.
10058{_}841{_}This simplifies things and makes the ferial office shorter than the office of feasts.
10058{_}842{_}TITLE VI.--THE OFFICE OF VIGILS.
10058{_}843{_}_Etymology, nature and synonyms_.
10058{_}844{_}The word _vigil_ is from the Latin _vigilare, to keep awake, to watch_, because in old times the night before any great event, religious or worldly, was spent in watching.
10058{_}845{_}Thus, the night prior to ordination to the priesthood, the night prior to a great battle, was spent in watching before the altar.
10058{_}846{_}Hence, the word vigil came to mean the prayers said during the time of watching or waking, preparatory to the great event.
10058{_}847{_}It signified, too, the fast accompanying the watching, and lastly it came to mean the liturgical office of Mass and Breviary fixed for the time of vigilance.
10058{_}848{_}In the Roman Church it was sometimes called the nocturn or night office.
10058{_}849{_}The Greeks call the vigil _profesta_, the time before the feast.
10058{_}850{_}The custom existed among the pagans, almost universally, before the time of Christ.
10058{_}851{_}The Jews practised this ancient night prayer, as the scripture in several places shows, _"in noctibus extollite manus vestras in sancta"_ (Psalm 133).
10058{_}852{_}Our Saviour sanctified this use by His example, and the early Christians were, on account of these night assemblies, the objects of fear and dread, of admiration and of hatred.
10058{_}853{_}Organised vigils lasted till the thirteenth century in some countries, but owing to abuses and discord they became not a source of edification, but the occasion and cause of grave scandals, and were forbidden gradually and universally.
10058{_}854{_}The Church now retains for the faithful one congregational vigil, the vigil of Christmas.
10058{_}855{_}Formerly, it was customary to observe a fast on a day or night of a vigil, but that custom was suppressed sometimes, or fell into disuse.
10058{_}856{_}Vigil fasts are now few.
10058{_}857{_}Almost the only relic of the vigil now remaining is the Mass and Office.
10058{_}858{_}When were vigils held?
10058{_}859{_}In the early ages they were held only on Saturday nights and on nights preceding great solemnities or the festivals of the Martyrs.
10058{_}860{_}The early converts, if they had been pagans, knew few or no prayer formulae, and very little of the psalms was learned by them even in their Christian practice.
10058{_}861{_}But Jews who became Christians knew psalms and hymns and prayers.
10058{_}862{_}So that in the early Christian vigils, there was no attempt made at reciting the Divine Office, and the custom of such recitation was not introduced until about 220 A.D. and was not obligatory (Duchesne, _Christian Worship_, Chap.
10058{_}863{_}VIII.).
10058{_}864{_}It is difficult to speak with certainty about the hour of beginning or the hour of ending these vigil services.
10058{_}865{_}Some think that the first nocturn was said about 9 p.m. Lauds was said before sunrise and hence was called _Laudes-matutinae_.
10058{_}866{_}But "after the middle of the ninth century, we gather from contemporary documents, that the office of vigils was, as a whole, regularly constituted and well known" (Baudot, p.64).
10058{_}867{_}These vigils were held in cenacles or upper rooms of houses.
10058{_}868{_}During the days of persecution these meetings were not infrequent and were held secretly in crypts, catacombs, private houses and at martyrs' tombs.
10058{_}869{_}In times of peace they were held everywhere, in churches, monasteries, castles.
10058{_}870{_}Vigils are divided into two classes, major and minor; major vigils are the vigils of Christmas, Epiphany and Pentecost, and they are called privileged vigils and are celebrated as semi-doubles.
10058{_}871{_}The vigils of Christmas and Pentecost are privileged vigils of the first class.
10058{_}872{_}The vigil of Epiphany is a privileged vigil of the second class.
10058{_}873{_}All others are minor or non-privileged vigils.
10058{_}874{_}TITLE VII.-OCTAVES.
10058{_}875{_}_Etymology and nature_.
10058{_}876{_}The word "octave" is from the Latin _octavus_ (eighth) because, in the early ages of Christianity, the Church celebrated the eighth day only after the celebration of the feast itself; not until the twelfth century was the custom of a commemoration on each of the eight days introduced.
10058{_}877{_}We have, probably, an example of this still in our Breviaries.
10058{_}878{_}The feast of St. Agnes is celebrated on 21st January and on 28th it is mentioned at Vespers and Lauds only, and the name in old Roman service books is _Octavo, S. Agnetis_.
10058{_}879{_}The origin of the octave is Jewish.
10058{_}880{_}We read in the Old Testament that God ordered that the Feasts of Pasch and Pentecost should be celebrated for eight days.
10058{_}881{_}So, too, the Feast of Tabernacles lasted for eight days, the first and eighth days being days of special celebration and devotion.
10058{_}882{_}The Christian Church adopted the method of showing great honour and glory to the principal festivals of the Christian year, to the great saints, the patrons of countries, dioceses, etc.
10058{_}883{_}But just as the calendar became overcrowded with saints' offices, which excluded almost entirely the Sunday and ferial offices, so, too, the additions of octaves created confusion and further tended to the exclusion of the old liturgical use of the Psalter and the supplanting of the Sunday and ferial offices.
10058{_}884{_}Hence, in the _Motu Proprio Abhinc duos annos_, the octaves of the calendar are divided into three great classes, privileged, common and simple.
10058{_}885{_}Privileged octaves are further divided into three _orders_.
10058{_}886{_}Those of the first order are the octaves of Easter and Pentecost; the octaves of Epiphany and Corpus Christi belong to the second order, and the octaves of the Nativity and Ascension belong to the third.
10058{_}887{_}The Christmas octave admits feasts of saints, but the octaves of Epiphany, Easter and Pentecost do not admit any feasts (Tit.
10058{_}888{_}V., sec, 3).
10058{_}889{_}A day within an octave has a right to first Vespers, and the antiphon and response should be from first Vespers (S.C.R., June, 1905).
10058{_}890{_}But the feast of the day falling within octave has a right to first and second Vespers.
10058{_}891{_}The exceptions are, when at second Vespers of St. Thomas, the office of the octave of the Nativity to be observed on 30th December has to be commemorated again, in octaves like octaves of Epiphany when each day has its proper antiphon at the _Magnificat_, and again on and July in second Vespers of Visitation the office of St. Peter and Paul is to be commemorated.
10058{_}892{_}In octaves the suffrages of saints and the Athanasian Creed are not said.
10058{_}893{_}When feasts of the Universal Church, which are celebrated with an octave are perpetually transferred to the next day, because of a perpetual impediment, according to the rubrics, the octave day is not therefore perpetually transferred but ought to be kept as in the Universal Church on its own day.
10058{_}894{_}TITLE VIII.--OFFICE OF THE BLESSED VIRGIN FOR SATURDAY.
10058{_}895{_}"_In omnibus Sabbatis per annum entra Adventum et Quadragesimam, ac nisi Quatuor Tempora aut Vigiliae ocurrant_," etc.
10058{_}896{_}In all Saturdays throughout the year, except on the Saturdays of Advent, Lent, Ember Days or occurring Vigils, or unless a feast of nine lessons has to be said on the Saturday, then it is laid down in the rubrics that the Office of the Blessed Virgin should always be said with the rite of a simple office.
10058{_}897{_}The rubrics of the New Psalter (Title I., sec.
10058{_}898{_}6) direct, "_In officio Sanctae Mariae in Sabbato et in festis simplicibus sic officium persolvendum est; ad matutinum, Invitatorium et hymnus dicuntur de eodem officio vel de iisdem Festis; Psalmi cum suis antiphonis et versu de Feria occurente I. et II.
10058{_}899{_}Lectis de Feria cum Responsoriis Propriis vel de Communi.
10058{_}900{_}III.
10058{_}901{_}vero lectio de officio vel Festo duabus lectionibus in unum junctis si quando duae pro Festo habeatur, ad reliquas autem Horas omnia dicuntur, prouti supra num.
10058{_}902{_}5 in Festis Duplicibus expositum est_."
10058{_}903{_}In the Office of the Blessed Virgin for Saturdays (Decree S.C.R., 26th January.
10058{_}904{_}1916) the antiphons and Psalms at Matins, Lauds and small Hours are to be said from the Saturday and from the _capitulum_ onwards all is to be taken from the office of the Blessed Virgin.
10058{_}905{_}This office is not to be confounded with the _officium parvum Beatae Mariae._ The office _de Sabbato_ is obligatory throughout the Church.
10058{_}906{_}The _officium parvum_ was only for choir use, an addition to the office of the day.
10058{_}907{_}Saturday, dedicated to the Blessed Virgin, is of great antiquity, as the mention of it in the works of St. Peter Damien, St. Bernard and Pope Benedict XIII.
10058{_}908{_}shows, but as to the time of its origin or a history of its growth, little seems to be known.
10058{_}909{_}At first the cult consisted in various and voluntary prayers and practices.
10058{_}910{_}About the middle of the fourteenth century an office was composed for recital on Saturdays as dedicated to the Mother of God.
10058{_}911{_}The office in our Breviaries was composed by St. Pius V, (1566-1572).
10058{_}912{_}TITLE IX,--COMMEMORATIONS.
10058{_}913{_}The rules laid down in the general rubrics of the Breviary for commemorations were never very simple, and when we read the changes brought about in _De ratione Divini officii recitandi juxta novum Psalteri ordinem_, Titles II., III., IV., V., VI., with' the decrees of the Congregation (January, 1912), and subsequently (_Abhinc duos Annos_) everyone must fear to tread the maze with certainty and must often fall back gratefully on the labours of the compilers of the _Ordo_ which he follows.
10058{_}914{_}Or, perhaps, doubts may be dispelled by _The New Psalter_ (Burton and Myers) published in 1912.
10058{_}915{_}The chapter on the Calendar in that book is worth study, but needs now additions and corrections, owing to the issue of more recent decrees.
10058{_}916{_}In the study of commemorations and translations of feasts there are two words which have a special meaning and which, being often used in calendar working, deserve a special note.
10058{_}917{_}They are "occurrence" and "concurrence."
10058{_}918{_}_Occurrence_ is the conjunction of two or more offices, which fall on the same day.
10058{_}919{_}It may be accidental when two movable feasts are concerned or when a movable feast falls on a day which has a fixed office; or it may be perpetual, when a fixed office falls on a day which already has a fixed office.
10058{_}920{_}The Church does not ask the recitation of a double or a triple office.
10058{_}921{_}She, by her fixed rules, prefers one out of the two of the "occurring" offices, transfers if possible the others, or at least commemorates them by an antiphon, versicle and prayer, and sometimes by a ninth lesson at Matins.
10058{_}922{_}_Concurrence_ is the conjunction of two offices, which succeed one another, so that a question arises as to which feast the Vespers belong to; whether to the feast of the day or to the feast of the following day, or whether the psalms should be of the feast and the remaining part of the Vespers should be as the _Ordo_ so often notes (_a cap.
10058{_}923{_}de seq._), from the _capitulum_ the office is taken from the following feast.
10058{_}924{_}The new rubrics contain five titles which make certain modifications in the rules hitherto observed.
10058{_}925{_}We thus obtain a ready made division of the subject:--  (1) Of the precedence of Feasts (Title II.).
10058{_}926{_}(2) Of the accidental occurrence of feasts and their translation (Title     III.).
10058{_}927{_}(3) Of the perpetual occurrence of feasts and their transfer (Title V.).
10058{_}928{_}(4) Of the occurrence of feasts (Title V.).
10058{_}929{_}(5) Of the commemorations (Title VI.)
10058{_}930{_}(Myers and Burton, _op.
10058{_}931{_}cit._).
10058{_}932{_}The new rubrics without the aid of any commentator give pretty clear notions of the laws of precedence, occurrence and commemoration.
10058{_}933{_}For students in college these rules are expounded in detail with additions, changes, exceptions.
10058{_}934{_}But for priests, long past the student stage, it is difficult to undo the fixed liturgy lore of their student and early priest life; and the need of such a book as _The New Psalter and its Uses_ is, for those interested, a necessity.
10058{_}935{_}Even since the publication of that book, changes have been made.
10058{_}936{_}For example, doubles, major or minor and semi-doubles, which were perpetually excluded on their own day were transferred to some fixed day.
10058{_}937{_}This is given in _The New Psalter and its Uses_.
10058{_}938{_}But this has now been changed.
10058{_}939{_}In the case of feasts of the universal Church, no translation is allowed now.
10058{_}940{_}But feasts proper to a nation, diocese, order, institute or particular church may still be transferred to a fixed day, if perpetually impeded on their own day.
10058{_}941{_}Another example of necessary changes in that excellent book is in the last paragraph of page 136 (see Decree S.C.R., June, 1912).
10058{_}942{_}The works of compilers and liturgists need constant revision to keep pace with new decisions and decrees.
10058{_}943{_}In making commemorations, the order of the commemoration as laid down in the _Ordo_ should be followed.
10058{_}944{_}Elements of a commemoration are the Antiphon of the _Benedictus_ or the _Magnificat_ with versicle and response.
10058{_}945{_}These antiphons are considered most excellent, preceding as they do the Gospel canticles (St. Luke I.).
10058{_}946{_}The antiphon, versicle and prayer of the commemoration at an hour should never be repetitions of others said in the same hour.
10058{_}947{_}Thus, if in the office of a confessor pontiff having the prayer _Da quaesumus_, another confessor pontiff's feast, commemorated in the same hour, should not have the same prayer.
10058{_}948{_}About the prayer, or, as it is called, the collect, the following should be noted: first, the commemoration is omitted if the prayer of the office which is being recited and the prayer of the feast to be commemorated have the same object.
10058{_}949{_}Thus, a feast of the Blessed Virgin, falling within the octave of the Assumption, should not be commemorated.
10058{_}950{_}Second, where a commemoration for a saint or saints of title similar to that of the saints whose office is being said, is to be made, the Congregation of Rites (5th May, 1736) arranged that not even the versicles and response be repeated and that the following order be observed:--  IN VESPERS--  1st Com.
10058{_}951{_}made by Antiphon and Versicle of Lauds.
10058{_}952{_}2nd Com.
10058{_}953{_}made by Antiphon of second Vesper and Ver.
10058{_}954{_}of II.
10058{_}955{_}Nocturn.
10058{_}956{_}3rd Com.
10058{_}957{_}made by Antiphon of I. Noct.
10058{_}958{_}and Vers.
10058{_}959{_}of III.
10058{_}960{_}Nocturn.
10058{_}961{_}IN LAUDS--  1st Com.
10058{_}962{_}made by Antiphon and Vers.
10058{_}963{_}from first Vesp.
10058{_}964{_}2nd Com.
10058{_}965{_}made by Antiphon I.
10058{_}966{_}Noct., and Ver., III.
10058{_}967{_}Noct.
10058{_}968{_}3rd Com.
10058{_}969{_}made by Antiphon II.
10058{_}970{_}Vesp., Vers., II.
10058{_}971{_}Noct.
10058{_}972{_}If it should happen in commemorating a day within an octave that the versicle from the common had already been taken for the office, then the rule is "_Sumenda est in laudibus antiphona de secundis Vesperis; et pro secundis Vesperis antiphona de laudibus in utroque tamen casu cum v. de primis Vesperis_" (S.C.R., 18th Dec., 1779).
10058{_}973{_}In the above given form of making commemorations it may be noted that the second commemoration in Lauds is made up from the versicles and response of Matins and not from second Vespers, so as to avoid repeating in Lauds what was said at Vespers (Cavalieri).
10058{_}974{_}As regards prayers in the office the reminder that the same formula must not be repeated in the same hour may be supplemented.
10058{_}975{_}Because, prayers having all words identical, save one single word, are not considered in liturgy as different prayers (_e.g., Accendamur exemplis; instruamur exemplis_, Feast of St. Philip and St. James, Feasts of several martyrs).
10058{_}976{_}So, too, prayers which have the same form of petition (e.g., the prayers on feast of St. Joseph and on feast of St. Mathew), are not considered as different and must not be repeated in the same hour.
10058{_}977{_}But where the petition is different, even though all the remainder of the prayers are similar in wording, they may be repeated in the same hour.
10058{_}978{_}But what is to be done in offices where a commemoration prayer and the prayer of the office is from the common?
10058{_}979{_}What must be done where the feast is the feast of a Doctor and a commemoration of a Doctor is to be made?
10058{_}980{_}What is to be done when the office of the feast is of a virgin not a martyr, and a commemoration of a virgin not a martyr is to be made?
10058{_}981{_}In the first case the prayer from the office of a confessor or Pontiff should be said, adding to it the title of Doctor.
10058{_}982{_}In the other case, the prayer _Indulgentiam_, omitting the word _martyr_, is to be said.
10058{_}983{_}The origin of these commemorations was, that the Popes in removing the solemn celebrations of certain feasts of Apostles and Martyrs, which were formerly of precept, provided that their _cultus_ should not be forgotten, and that their commemoration in the office should remind priests and the faithful of those servants of God, whom the Church wishes ever to honour.
10058{_}984{_}I have said the order given for commemoration in the _Ordo_ should be followed; but not to follow this order does not exceed a venial sin.
10058{_}985{_}Even the deliberate omission of a commemoration in Lauds or Vespers is not a violation of a grave precept.
10058{_}986{_}TITLE X.--THE TRANSLATION OF FEASTS.
10058{_}987{_}When several offices fall on the same day, only one office, the one of highest rank or most important, is said.
10058{_}988{_}The others are transferred or commemorated.
10058{_}989{_}The last section dealt with commemorations, and now we come to the difficult question of the translation of feasts.
10058{_}990{_}Title X. of the general rubrics must be read in connection with the Apostolic Constitution, _Divino Afflatu_ (1911) and with the _Abhinc duos Annos_ (1913).
10058{_}991{_}Translation of a feast may mean the removal of a feast from an impeded day to a day which is free.
10058{_}992{_}Thus a feast of higher rank may fall on a feast day of a saint whose feast is of lower rank; the latter may then be transferred.
10058{_}993{_}Transference is either perpetual or accidental and temporary.
10058{_}994{_}The former applies to feasts which are always impeded by the meeting with a feast of higher rite on their fixed days.
10058{_}995{_}A feast which would fall on 6th January would suffer perpetual translation.
10058{_}996{_}This translation bears different names in rubrics, decrees and liturgical writings--_translatio ad diem, fixam, translatio ad diem assignatam, mutatio, etc._ Accidental translation means occasional transference, a transfer in one year and not in another.
10058{_}997{_}Title II., section i, of the _Divino Afflatu_ gives the characters of preferential rank which are to be considered in occurrence, concurrence or translation of feasts, _Ritus altior, ratio primarii aut secundarii, Dignitas Personalis, solemnitas externa_.
10058{_}998{_}Although in the General Rubrics of the Breviary, the title _De Festorum praestantia_ is not found, the four principles, (1)gradation of rite, (2)classification as a primary or secondary feast, (3)personal dignity, (4)external solemnity, are mentioned in the sixth section of Title X., _De Translatione Festorum_, and the degrees of personal dignity are added in the second section of Title XL, _de commemorationibus_.
10058{_}999{_}Before 1897 precedence, and hence transference, was settled first by the rank of the rite (Double major, etc.
10058{_}1000{_}); then, too, between two feasts of the same rite, transference was settled by dignity and finally by solemnity.
10058{_}1001{_}But in 1897 the Sacred Congregation of Rites indicated two further notes to be observed in the weighing of claims for transference, (1)the classification into primary and secondary feasts, (2)the distinction between fixed and movable feasts.
10058{_}1002{_}This latter distinction--between fixed and movable feasts--has been suppressed by the new legislation and some changes made in the others.
10058{_}1003{_}I.
10058{_}1004{_}_Gradation of Feasts_ makes a distinction between doubles, semi-doubles and simples, and distinguishes the various kinds of doubles.
10058{_}1005{_}The order of procedure will be--(1)Doubles of the first class, (2)doubles of the second class, (3)greater doubles, (4)doubles, (5)semi-doubles, (6)simples.
10058{_}1006{_}But as the section shows (Tit.
10058{_}1007{_}II., sec.
10058{_}1008{_}i) this is subject to the privileges of certain Sundays, ferias, and octave days or even days within an octave.
10058{_}1009{_}And hence, an ordinary Sunday, though!
10058{_}1010{_}only a semi-double, will take precedence of a double; and an octave day, though only a double, takes precedence of a greater double.
10058{_}1011{_}II.
10058{_}1012{_}Classification as a primary or a secondary feast.
10058{_}1013{_}Tables of classification are to be found in the prefatory part of the new Breviary, under the headings _Tres Tabellae_.
10058{_}1014{_}They give a revised list of feasts with their rank and rites.
10058{_}1015{_}Some feasts are reduced from primary to secondary rank (e.g., Feast of the Dolours); and the tables give a new division of primary and secondary doubles and semi-doubles.
10058{_}1016{_}III.
10058{_}1017{_}Thirdly, the order of precedence among feasts will be determined by the dignity of the person who is the special object of the office that is to be recited.
10058{_}1018{_}Hence, in the order set down in General Rubrics (Title XI, _De Concurrentia officii_, sec.
10058{_}1019{_}2) all feasts of our Lord, other things being equal, take precedence of the feasts of our Lady.
10058{_}1020{_}And then, in order, come the festivals of the angels, of St. John the Baptist, of St. Joseph, of the Apostles and other saints.
10058{_}1021{_}Amongst the saints who are honoured as martyrs, confessors or virgins there is no precedence as to personal dignity.
10058{_}1022{_}IV.
10058{_}1023{_}Lastly, there is the note of "external solemnity," which may give precedence to one or two feasts, which are equal in the above-mentioned matters--i.e., in Gradation I., Classification II., Precedence III.
10058{_}1024{_}But the main point is that only doubles of first and second class have the right, as a rule, of transference.
10058{_}1025{_}Transference is now rather rare.
10058{_}1026{_}"From these rules it will be seen that in cases of concurrence, occurrence, perpetual transfer or translation, precedence between two feasts will first be decided by gradation of rite, a double of the first class being preferred to one of the second, and so on.
10058{_}1027{_}If the feasts are of equal rank recourse must be had to the second test, the distinction between primary and secondary feasts.
10058{_}1028{_}If both happen to be primary, or both are secondary, then precedence will be granted to the feast which has the greater personal dignity.
10058{_}1029{_}And if both feasts should have the same dignity, then the fact of external solemnity would confer precedence" (_The New Psalter and its Uses_, p. 79).
10058{_}1030{_}For practical help, a look at the first of the _Duae Tabellae_ is a guide to find out which office is to be said, if more than one feast occur on the same day.
10058{_}1031{_}Before discussing new offices it may be well to remember that votive offices of all kinds, including the votive offices conceded by the decree of July, 1883, are abolished.
10058{_}1032{_}These offices were drastic innovations, introduced to get rid of the very long psalm arrangement of the ferial office.
10058{_}1033{_}The new distribution of the psalms got rid of the onus, and votive offices are no longer given in the Breviary.
10058{_}1034{_}TITLE XL--CONCURRENCE.
10058{_}1035{_}_Concurrence_ is the conjunction of two offices which succeed each other, so that the question arises to which of the two are the Vespers of the day to be assigned.
10058{_}1036{_}The origin of this conjunction of feasts was by some old writers traced to the Mosaic law in which the festivals, began in the evening, and they quote "from evening until evening you shall celebrate your sabbaths" (_Leviticus_, xxii.
10058{_}1037{_}32).
10058{_}1038{_}The effect of concurrence may be that the whole vespers may belong to the feast of the day or may be said entirely from, the feast of the following day; or it may be that the psalms and antiphons belong to the preceding festival and the rest of the office be from the succeeding feast.
10058{_}1039{_}The General Rubrics, Title XI, must be read now in conjunction with Titles IV., V., and VI.
10058{_}1040{_}of the _Additiones et Variationes ad norman Bullae "Divino Afflatu"_.
10058{_}1041{_}The rules for concurrence are given in Table III.
10058{_}1042{_}of the _Tres Tabellae_ inserted in the new Breviary (S.C.R., 23 January, 1912).
10058{_}1043{_}These tables supersede the tables given in the old editions of the Breviary.
10058{_}1044{_}The first of these two tables shows which office is to be said, if more than one feast occur on the same day, whether perpetually or accidentally.
10058{_}1045{_}The second table is a guide to concurrence--_i.e._, whether the first vespers of the following feast is to be said entirely without reference to the preceding feast, or if second vespers of the preceding feast is to be said entire, without reference to the following; or, again, first vespers of the following with commemoration of the preceding, or second vespers of the preceding with commemoration of the following, or vespers of the more noble feast with commemoration of the other--any of these may be the liturgical order to follow, and the _Tabella_ makes things clear.
10058{_}1046{_}The "tables" are to be used thus:--Opening the Breviary at the _I Tabella, "Si occurrat eodem die,"_ first find the number marked in that square in which the two feasts in question meet, and then read the direction printed, in column on same page to left-hand side, bearing the same number.
10058{_}1047{_}For example: the question is about the occurrence of a Sunday of the first class and a Double of the first class.
10058{_}1048{_}_Double of the first class_ stands first word of page, and _Sunday of first class_ will be found in column beneath the rows of figures.
10058{_}1049{_}Now the square in which straight lines drawn from _double of first class_ and _Sunday of first class_ meet bears the number 6, and reference to number 6 in column of directions found on same page gives the rule, "_Officium de 2, Translatio de I_," that is, the office must be of the Sunday of first class and the double of the first class must be transferred according to the rubrics.
10058{_}1050{_}When in these brief directive notes, (1-8), mention is made of the "first or the preceding," the reference is made to feast or office printed in the upper part of the Table, e.g., Double of first class.
10058{_}1051{_}Reference to "the second" or "following" refers to feast printed in the lower section of the Table.
10058{_}1052{_}Where _O_ stands in a square in the _Tabella_ it signifies that there can be no occurrence or concurrence between feasts whose "lines" meet in that square.
10058{_}1053{_}These two tables are very ingeniously arranged.
10058{_}1054{_}The lists, given in the Breviary following these tables, give the lists of greater Sundays and Ferias, privileged vigils, doubles of first and second class and greater doubles, and tell whether feasts are primary or secondary.
10058{_}1055{_}TITLE XII.--THE ARRANGEMENT OF THE OFFICE ACCORDING    TO THE RUBRICS GIVEN ABOVE.
10058{_}1056{_}If any one wish from the rubrics given in the Breviary to arrange the office, he can see in the calendar and in the tables of movable feasts which office he is to say on the following day.
10058{_}1057{_}And when he has found out the feast he determines, from the rules given, the vespers and the other hours.
10058{_}1058{_}If the office be the office of an excepted feast, the whole office is said from the feast as it is in the Proper or Common of saints; but the psalms of Lauds and the hours are taken from the Sunday psalms, as they stand in the new Psaltery, At Prime the psalm _Deus in nomine_ is said in place of _Confitemini_.
10058{_}1059{_}Compline is said from the Sunday psalms.
10058{_}1060{_}If the office be the ordinary non-excepted office it is recited according to the rule laid down in the new rubrics.
10058{_}1061{_}Tit.
10058{_}1062{_}I., n. 5,:--  "_Ad matut, invit.
10058{_}1063{_}Hymnus, Lectiones II.
10058{_}1064{_}et III.
10058{_}1065{_}nocturni ac responsoria 2 et 3 nocturnorum propria vel de communi; antiphonae vero, psalmi et versus trium nocturnorum necnon Lestiones I. Nocturni cum suis Responsoriis de feria occurrente...."_  _"Ad Laudes et ad Vesperas ant.
10058{_}1066{_}cum Psalm.
10058{_}1067{_}de Feria; Capit.
10058{_}1068{_}Hym.
10058{_}1069{_}Vers.
10058{_}1070{_}et Antiph.
10058{_}1071{_}ad Benedictus vel ad magnificat cum oratione aut in Proprio aut de Communi ad Horas minores et Complet.
10058{_}1072{_}aut cum Psalm semper dicitur de occurrente Feria.
10058{_}1073{_}Ad Primam pro Lectione breve legitur capit.
10058{_}1074{_}Nonae ex Proprio, vel de Communi.
10058{_}1075{_}Ad Tertiam, sextam et Nonam, capit.
10058{_}1076{_}Respons.
10058{_}1077{_}breve et orat.
10058{_}1078{_}pariter sumuntur vel ex Proprio vel de Communi_."
10058{_}1079{_}(Matins and the other hours are treated of in another section.)
10058{_}1080{_}PART II.
10058{_}1081{_}RULES FROM MORAL AND ASCETIC THEOLOGY FOR THE RECITATION   OF THE BREVIARY.
10058{_}1082{_}MORAL THEOLOGY GIVES THE RULES AND LAWS, WHICH MUST BE FOLLOWED FOR THE VALID AND LICIT RECITATION OF THE HOURS.
10058{_}1083{_}ASCETIC THEOLOGY EXPLAINS THE MEANS, WHICH ARE TO BE USED IN THEIR FERVENT RECITATION.
10058{_}1084{_}CHAPTER I.
10058{_}1085{_}MORAL AND ASCETIC THEOLOGY.
10058{_}1086{_}Q.
10058{_}1087{_}Who are bound to recite the Divine Office?
10058{_}1088{_}R. 1.
10058{_}1089{_}Religious, that is, all those who have made       Religious Profession, in the Canonical       sense, and who are bound to Choir recitation       (Canon 610, Juris Canonici).
10058{_}1090{_}2.
10058{_}1091{_}Clerics in Holy Orders (Canon 135, Codex).
10058{_}1092{_}3.
10058{_}1093{_}Beneficed Clergy.
10058{_}1094{_}Who are Beneficed Clergy?
10058{_}1095{_}Beneficed Clergy are those who hold a Canonically erected benefice.
10058{_}1096{_}Canon 1409 of the _Codex Juris Canonici_ defines an ecclesiastical benefice to be a "Juridical entity constituted or erected by competent ecclesiastical authority, consisting of a sacred office and the right of receiving revenues from endowments attached to the office."
10058{_}1097{_}Hence under this Canon, as previously three conditions are required for a benefice, first, a sacred office, second, the right of receiving revenues from endowment attached to that office, third, erection by ecclesiastical authority.
10058{_}1098{_}There never was any doubt in the many discussions on this subject, that the work and care of a parish is a sacred office, and that parish priests hold such an office.
10058{_}1099{_}But the second condition mentioned above received different interpretations.
10058{_}1100{_}Some held that it implied a certain amount of ecclesiastical property set aside, from the revenues of which the holder of the benefice would derive his income.
10058{_}1101{_}Hence the revenues of parish priests in these Kingdoms, arising from certain and voluntary offerings of the faithful, were not fixed revenues, did not fulfil the conditions of "endowment," and parishes must not be regarded as benefices.
10058{_}1102{_}This opinion is no longer tenable.
10058{_}1103{_}Canon 1410 says:--"The endowment of a Benefice is constituted either by property, the ownership of which pertains to the Juridical entity itself, or by certain and obligatory payments of any family or moral personality, or by certain and voluntary offerings of the faithful which appertain to the rector of the benefice, or, as they are called stole fees, within the limits of diocesan taxation or legitimate custom, or choral distributions, exclusive of a third part of the same, if all the revenues of the benefice consist of choral distributions."
10058{_}1104{_}This Canon seems to make it clear that the second condition is fulfilled in all the parishes of these Kingdoms, since to the sacred office is attached the right of receiving revenue from the certain and voluntary offerings of the faithful or from stole fees or from both.
10058{_}1105{_}The third condition, erection by ecclesiastical authority, is qualified by Canon 1418 which prescribes that benefices should be erected by a legitimate document defining the place of the benefice, its endowment and the duties and rights of the person appointed.
10058{_}1106{_}This law has not an invalidating clause, hence it is not now necessary nor ever was it necessary to have such a written document.
10058{_}1107{_}A valid appointment was and can be made without any writing.
10058{_}1108{_}Where these three conditions are fulfilled there is a benefice, true, real, and canonical.
10058{_}1109{_}Normally parishes are benefices.
10058{_}1110{_}(See _Irish Ecclesiastical Record_, Vol.
10058{_}1111{_}XIV., No.
10058{_}1112{_}623; and _Irish Theological Quarterly_, October, 1917, p.
10058{_}1113{_}209.)
10058{_}1114{_}Every cleric in holy orders is bound under pain of mortal sin to recite daily the Divine Office.
10058{_}1115{_}No General Council, no Pope, has made such a law, but the old-established custom has grown, until it has the force of a law (Bened.
10058{_}1116{_}XIV., _Instructio Coptharum_).
10058{_}1117{_}Authors are not agreed as to the date of the first traces of this old custom.
10058{_}1118{_}Billuart quotes the text of the fourth Council of Carthage to prove that it existed in the fourth century, _Clericus, qui absque corpusculi sui inequalitate vigiliis deest, stipendiis privatus, excommunicatur_.
10058{_}1119{_}Gavantus can find traces of it only as late as the sixth century.
10058{_}1120{_}Several decrees of provincial councils regarding this custom are quoted by writers on liturgy.
10058{_}1121{_}However, the matter is clearly and definitely dealt with by the General Council of Lateran (1213) and by the Bulls, _Quod a nobis_ and _Ex proximo_, of Pope Pius V. (1571).
10058{_}1122{_}This Pope expressly states that wilful omission of the Divine Office is a grave sin--"_grave peccatum intelligat se commissise_."
10058{_}1123{_}The obligation of reciting the office binds those in Holy Orders, even though they may be excommunicated, suspended, degraded or imprisoned.
10058{_}1124{_}The obligation binds for the first time when subdeaconship has been conferred.
10058{_}1125{_}Subdeacons are bound to recite "the hour" in the office of the day, corresponding to the time of their ordination.
10058{_}1126{_}If the ordination is finished before nine o'clock, the sub-deacon is bound to begin his recitation with Terce.
10058{_}1127{_}If the ordination is held between nine o'clock and mid-day the recitation begins with Sext.
10058{_}1128{_}The question is discussed by theologians if the recitation of Terce or Sext may be lawfully and validly made before the ordination.
10058{_}1129{_}Some authors deny that it may be justly and lawfully done, while others, with some probability, affirm that before ordination the debt may be paid in advance.
10058{_}1130{_}Are priests bound to follow the Proper in their own diocese?
10058{_}1131{_}They are, if it has been approved by the Sacred Congregation of Rites (S.R.C., 4597-4746).
10058{_}1132{_}But a priest travelling (_peregrinus_) should recite the office according to the calendar of the church to which he is attached regularly, but the obligation of following the calendar of his home church was not binding by a grave precept.
10058{_}1133{_}A reply of the Sacred Congregation of Rites (Nov., 1831) arranged (1) that beneficed clergy are always bound to recite the office of their own proper church or diocese; (2) that simple priests may read either the office as arranged for the place they tarry in or travel in, or the office of their own home diocese; (3) for unattached priests (_vagi_) it is the wiser order to follow the office as laid down in their own diocese.
10058{_}1134{_}Must every holder of a benefice read the Divine Office?
10058{_}1135{_}Every holder is bound, under pain of mortal sin, to recite the Divine Office daily, if the benefice be an ecclesiastical benefice fulfilling the conditions named above.
10058{_}1136{_}The omission of the recital of the Divine Office by a beneficed person is a grave sin against the virtue of religion and a grave sin against the virtue of justice.
10058{_}1137{_}For the Church imposes on the beneficiary the duty of the Office recital, on condition that he may not take the fruits of his benefice if he do not recite the Office.
10058{_}1138{_}What sin is committed by the omission of a notable part of the daily office?
10058{_}1139{_}He who wilfully omits a notable part of the daily Divine Office commits a mortal sin.
10058{_}1140{_}A notable part of the Divine Office for any day is held by some theologians to be the omission of one psalm in one of the small hours, or a corresponding quantity of matter in lessons, responses, etc.
10058{_}1141{_}They hold that such wilful omission is a grave sin.
10058{_}1142{_}Other theologians hold--and their opinion is the more common and the more probable one--that, although one psalm is a notable part of a small hour, in relation to the whole office it is not a notable part, and its omission is not a grave matter.
10058{_}1143{_}These theologians hold that the wilful omission of an entire small hour or equivalent matter (e.g., Sext, or the third nocturn of Matins) is an omission of a notable part and cannot be excused from grave sin.
10058{_}1144{_}The omission of the entire office of a day, the seven canonical hours, is held by some theologians to carry the guilt of seven mortal sins.
10058{_}1145{_}Because, there is a different precept for each hour and the omission of each hour violates a precept.
10058{_}1146{_}The Salamenticenses think this opinion probable.
10058{_}1147{_}The more common and the more correct opinion is that by such omission only one sin is committed.
10058{_}1148{_}And the theologians who hold this opinion say that the recitation of the canonical hours is imposed under one precept only, and hence there is only one obligation embracing the seven hours.
10058{_}1149{_}This is the opinion of St. Alphonsus (n. 148) who quotes several authors (including Lessius, Sanchez and St. Antoninus) in support.
10058{_}1150{_}If a person in Holy Orders omit several hours with a retractation, or a moral interruption in his sinful intentions, he may commit several mortal sins, because all the omissions, which in themselves are grave matter, may become independent of each other by the interruption and renewal of the intention (St. Alphonsus, n. 148).
10058{_}1151{_}What must a person do who has a doubt that he has omitted something in his recitation of the office?
10058{_}1152{_}Is he bound to make assurance doubly sure by reciting the part of which he doubts?
10058{_}1153{_}If the doubt be a positive doubt, that is, if he have good reason to believe that he has recited it, he is not bound to anything further regarding the part in question.
10058{_}1154{_}For instance, if a priest remembers having started the recitation of a lesson, and in a short time finds himself at the end of it, and cannot be sure if he have recited it, the presumption is in favour of the priest and of the recitation, because it is his custom to recite completely whatever part he commences.
10058{_}1155{_}He has, thus, moral certainty that he has satisfied the precept, and it is not necessary to repeat it; if the necessity for repetition be admitted in such a case, a fruitful source of scruples is opened up.
10058{_}1156{_}On the other hand, if the doubt be negative--that is to say, if a person has no reasonable motive for believing that he has recited the full office or the full hour, he is bound to recite the part omitted, because in such a doubt, the precept of recitation is, as the theologians say, "in possession."
10058{_}1157{_}(St. Alphonsus, n. 150).
10058{_}1158{_}It is not allowed to change anything nor to add anything to the daily office without permission.
10058{_}1159{_}The Sacred Congregation of Rites (10 June, 1690, n. 3222) replied to a query, that in saints' offices nothing is to be added and nothing is to be changed, and this reply applies to all sorts of offices, old and new.
10058{_}1160{_}THE ORDER TO BE OBSERVED IN RECITING THE DIVINE OFFICE.
10058{_}1161{_}In reciting the Divine Office two points of order are to be noted: (1) the order or arrangement of offices, (2) the order or arrangement of Hours.
10058{_}1162{_}The order of offices indicates which office is to be said on each day as laid down in the calendar.
10058{_}1163{_}The order of the Hours points out which of the seven hours should be recited, firstly, secondly, etc., Matins, Lauds, Prime, Terce, etc.
10058{_}1164{_}It is of obligation to observe both orders.
10058{_}1165{_}But is it a sin to change wilfully the order of the office?
10058{_}1166{_}It is not, if there be a reasonable cause for the change.
10058{_}1167{_}For instance, if a priest cannot say the office proper to his diocese on a certain day, but says some other approved office, the change is not a sin.
10058{_}1168{_}But if a priest, _ex industria_, substitute one office for another, it is _per se_ a venial sin; but if an office be said which is very much shorter than the calendar office, or if this changing or substituting be so frequent as to disturb gravely the good order of the year's offices, the sin may be (and, according to some authors, is) a mortal sin.
10058{_}1169{_}It is asked whether a person fulfils his debt to the Church if he has recited by mistake an office other than the one assigned in the calendar of the day.
10058{_}1170{_}Theologians teach that such a recitation fulfils the debt.
10058{_}1171{_}The Church does not wish to impose a second recitation, and her axiom "_officium pro officio valet_" holds, provided always that the order of the psalms as laid down in the new psaltery is followed.
10058{_}1172{_}This order is necessary always for validity.
10058{_}1173{_}However, if the substituted office be very much shorter than the omitted office, it is advised to equalise them by reciting the psalms of Matins, This is a counsel and was not laid down by theologians as an obligation.
10058{_}1174{_}An office thus omitted is not to be transferred to another day (S.C.R., June 17th, 1673).
10058{_}1175{_}The office may be omitted altogether for that year.
10058{_}1176{_}If there be leisure the omitted office should be recited.
10058{_}1177{_}This practice is in conformity with the spirit of the liturgy and with the right order of the calendar.
10058{_}1178{_}The Sacred Congregation of Rites, questioned on this matter, replied _sic debere fieri_, such should be done.
10058{_}1179{_}If a priest recites by mistake one day's office for another (e.g., the Tuesday office on a Monday) he is bound to recite Tuesday's office on Tuesday (St. Alphonsus).
10058{_}1180{_}If, however, after a portion of the office has been read, it is noticed that a mistake has been made in reading the calendar or the _Ordo_, and that the office partly recited is not the office of the current day, what is to be done?
10058{_}1181{_}If the priest has without fault made the mistake of reciting some office not ascribed to the current day, he is not bound to repeat the part already recited (e.g., Matins); it is sufficient, valid and lawful to follow the correct office in the following Hours.
10058{_}1182{_}The priest reciting is not bound to repeat even part of an hour, if he finds out his mistake during the recitation of even a small hour.
10058{_}1183{_}And he may finish the psalm or hymn or prayer which he was reciting when he discovered his mistake, and he may then take up the correct office at the part or hour at which he leaves off, or he may finish the Hour at which he was engaged.
10058{_}1184{_}The former solution of the difficulty seems the better, as it more accurately agrees with the maxim, _error corrigatur ubi apprehenditur_.
10058{_}1185{_}If the error in the selecting of the office has been wilful, say, through gross carelessness, and is the fault of the priest who changes a notable part of a canonical Hour, he is obliged--the more probable opinion teaches--to repeat the full Hour, and this obligation binds under pain of venial sin--_i.e._, the obligation to recite the office in the prescribed manner.
10058{_}1186{_}What is a person bound to do who forgets part of an Hour--is he obliged to repeat the full Hour?
10058{_}1187{_}He is bound to recite the part forgotten only, unless the mistake be made through gross carelessness, and unless it be a considerable part (e.g., two nocturns); in that case he is bound under pain of venial sin to repeat the full Hour.
10058{_}1188{_}If a person say the same Hour (e.g., Terce) twice, may he compensate for extra labour by the omission of an equivalent part (e.g., None)?
10058{_}1189{_}Such omission is unlawful; he must recite all the Hours without omission (Scavini, 391).
10058{_}1190{_}Is there an obligation to repeat the Hours in the order fixed in the Breviary?
10058{_}1191{_}Yes, there is such an obligation.
10058{_}1192{_}And a person may sin venially by the inversion of the Hours, The obligation binds _sub veniali_ only.
10058{_}1193{_}The inversion does not mean any grave breach of order, which is fixed by a secondary precept and as a circumstance of light importance.
10058{_}1194{_}If the whole office be recited, the substance of the office--which is the main and primary matter--is safeguarded.
10058{_}1195{_}Several authors argued that any inversion of the Canonical Hours, if frequent, is a mortal sin, but the opinion which says that the inversion of the Hours is only a venial sin is the more probable (St. Alph.
10058{_}1196{_}169; Gury, 77; Lehmkuhl II., 621).
10058{_}1197{_}Which causes justify an inversion of the Hours?
10058{_}1198{_}Any reasonable cause justifies this inversion.
10058{_}1199{_}Thus, if a friend invite a priest to joint recitation of an Hour, and the priest have not the preceding canonical Hours recited, he is justified in accepting the invitation and in inverting the order of the Hours.
10058{_}1200{_}Or if a person have a Diurnal only at hand, he may read the day Hours, although he have not Matins for the day read.
10058{_}1201{_}Again, a priest may not have the lessons for Matins at hand, but he may recite the psalms for Matins, Lauds, and add the lessons at Matins when they are to hand (Gury, n. 78; St.
10058{_}1202{_}Alph., n. 170).
10058{_}1203{_}Is it a sin to say Matins for following day before finishing office of current day?
10058{_}1204{_}Some theologians answer affirmatively, because the office of the current day should be complete before another office is begun.
10058{_}1205{_}Others hold that such recitation is both valid and licit, as the office of one day and its obligation have no bond with the office of another day, and that any reasonable cause exempts from all sin or fault (Gury, n. 79).
10058{_}1206{_}Not to recite the commemorations in the prescribed order set out in the _Ordo_ is held by some theologians to be a venial sin, as they hold that the rubric is preceptive; others hold that it is not any sin, as they say that the rubric is directive.
10058{_}1207{_}ARTICLE III.--TIME OF RECITATION.
10058{_}1208{_}The time fixed for the recitation of the entire office of the day is from midnight to the midnight following, and anyone bound to recite the Divine Office does not sin gravely if he has recited carefully the entire office of the day between these limits of time; because, within these limits, the substance of the obligation binding to time is fulfilled.
10058{_}1209{_}Of course, it is lawful in virtue of a privilege granted by the Church to recite on the previous evening Matins and Lauds for the following day.
10058{_}1210{_}In the recitation the times fixed by the Church for each hour should be observed.
10058{_}1211{_}But the non-recital at those fixed times is never a mortal sin and is rarely a venial sin, unless their postponement or anticipation is without cause.
10058{_}1212{_}When may a priest begin the recitation of Matins and Lauds for the following day?
10058{_}1213{_}There were two different replies given to this question.
10058{_}1214{_}One opinion stated that it was lawful to begin Matins and Lauds after 2 o'clock, p.m., and this could be lawfully done every day in the year, and in every land.
10058{_}1215{_}Another opinion--and St. Alphonsus calls it _sententia verior_--denies that such a course is lawful.
10058{_}1216{_}The old French Breviaries gave a _horarium_ arranging the hour of anticipation of Matins and Lauds, so that no one should, through temerity or ignorance, begin the anticipation before the sun had passed half way in its course between mid-day and sunset.
10058{_}1217{_}On January 20th the time to begin the anticipation of hours was 2.15 p.m., but on June 8th the anticipation was not to begin till 4 p.m.  Nowadays, the first opinion is held almost universally.
10058{_}1218{_}The principal _internal_ argument for this opinion is the teaching that the anticipation may begin from the public hour of first vespers, and these may be recited publicly according to present-day custom at 2 p.m.
10058{_}1219{_}Therefore, this time, 2 o'clock p.m., is the beginning of the ecclesiastical day, and can be taken as the time for private anticipation of Matins and Lauds.
10058{_}1220{_}The _external_ argument in favour of this opinion is the authority of theologians.
10058{_}1221{_}In 1905, the Sacred Congregation of Rites was asked the question "_Utrum in privata recitatione Matutinum sequentis diei incipi possit, 2da pomeriddiane_?".
10058{_}1222{_}The reply was, "_Consulantur probati auctores_" (_Acta Sanctae Sedis_ XXXVII., p. 712).
10058{_}1223{_}Now many approved authors (e.g., Lehmkuhl, II., 793; Ballerini-Palmieri, IV.
10058{_}1224{_}515; Slater I., p. 609) hold that it is lawful, privately, to anticipate Matins and Lauds at 2 o'clock, p.m. Lehmkuhl, who previously favoured a stricter view, was compelled, in the latest editions of his _Moral Theology_, to say of this opinion which allows anticipation to begin at 2 o'clock, p.m.: "_Quae sententia hodie a multis usque gravissimis viris tenetur et observatur, ut, spectata consuetudine, extrinseca saltem probabilitas negari nequit_."
10058{_}1225{_}We conclude, accordingly, that always and everywhere the private anticipation of Matins and Lauds may begin at 2 p.m. (_cf.
10058{_}1226{_}Irish Ecclesiastical Record_, Fifth Series, Vol.
10058{_}1227{_}I., No.
10058{_}1228{_}541).
10058{_}1229{_}Doubts have arisen in connection with time changes made by various States in Europe.
10058{_}1230{_}The various schemes of new time, of daylight saving, of co-ordinations of time, uniformity of time all through certain States, have given rise to doubts and queries regarding the time for fulfilling the precept of the office and also regarding the time for lawful anticipation of Matins and Lauds.
10058{_}1231{_}These doubts were solved several years ago, and now there is no longer any difficulty or anxiety over "true time," "new time," "legal time," in relation to matters ecclesiastical.
10058{_}1232{_}In reply to queries, Dr. M. J. O'Donnell, in the _Irish Ecclesiastical Record_ (Vol.
10058{_}1233{_}III., p. 582), explains clearly this time difficulty and its solution by the Congregation of the Council on 22nd July, 1893.
10058{_}1234{_}The Bishop of Trier explained to the Congregation of the Council that owing to the State legislation in the German Empire all public clocks should register the same time, and that this meant that in his diocese the legal computation differs by half an hour from the mean time.
10058{_}1235{_}"May clerics follow the legal time in reciting the Divine Office?"
10058{_}1236{_}was the bishop's question.
10058{_}1237{_}The Congregation of the Council answered by a simple affirmative.
10058{_}1238{_}In 1892, Greenwich time was introduced for State purposes into all railway, postal, and Government offices in Holland.
10058{_}1239{_}The query was put to the Congregation of the Inquisition if the clergy and people might, for the purpose of fast and other ecclesiastical obligations, follow the new time, or were they obliged to retain the true time?
10058{_}1240{_}The reply was "_affirmative ad primam: negative ad secundam partem_."
10058{_}1241{_}"In a word, the constant Roman answer has been 'Do as you please'; so far as the approval of the legal time is concerned it confirms the conclusion of the editor of the _Acta_ (xxxii-251) that in computing time the Church follows the rule that regulates all business concerns in different localities....  "In the meantime, taking into account the conventional character of 'time' and the liberal principles of Rome in the past, we have no doubt that everyone, priest or layman, is fully justified in following the new time if he feels so inclined."
10058{_}1242{_}(See _Codex Juris.
10058{_}1243{_}Canon._, Can.
10058{_}1244{_}33).
10058{_}1245{_}Are priests bound to recite Matins and Lauds before Mass?
10058{_}1246{_}The first sentence of the _Ritus servandus in celebratione Missae_ in the Missal contains the clause "_saltem Malutino cum Laudibus absoluto_," The word _saltem_ indicates that the Church notifies the minimum and expects a further hour, Prime or even others of the small hours, to be finished before Mass.
10058{_}1247{_}But theologians hold that there is no grave obligation for such prior-to-Mass recital, and that any reasonable cause excuses from the obligation (Lehmkuhl II., 628).
10058{_}1248{_}In connection with this matter a very instructive and devotional essay in the _Irish Ecclesiastical Record_ (Fourth Series XXXI., n. 533) by Father M. Russell, S.J., is well worth reading.
10058{_}1249{_}It is entitled "A Neglected Adverb"; the adverb being _saltem_, from the clause quoted.
10058{_}1250{_}At what times should the small hours be recited?
10058{_}1251{_}Prime may be, and, probably with more appropriateness, should be used as morning prayer and said before Mass.
10058{_}1252{_}Terce and Sext may be said before mid-day, or Sext and None may be said after mid-day.
10058{_}1253{_}Vespers should be said after mid-day.
10058{_}1254{_}Compline was the night prayer of the monks, who probably instituted the hour.
10058{_}1255{_}It should be borne in mind that the substance of the law of recitation is fulfilled if the whole office of the day be recited before midnight, and that the obligation for entire and complete recitation is grave; while the recitation of the hours at set hours of the day is a light obligation.
10058{_}1256{_}ARTICLE IV.-OF THE PLACE OF RECITATION AND THE ATTITUDE    IN RECITING THE OFFICE.
10058{_}1257{_}Where should the Divine Office be recited?
10058{_}1258{_}The Divine Office should be recited in the place intended and set apart by the Church for that purpose--viz., in the choir or in the Church (Con.
10058{_}1259{_}Trid., sess.
10058{_}1260{_}24).
10058{_}1261{_}Canons and religious are bound to recite their office in choir; of course, this refers to Canons in residence at a cathedral for daily service, and to religious in the strict application of the term.
10058{_}1262{_}The Divine Office may be recited by priests anywhere, in the church, in a dwelling-house, walking, in the fields, etc.
10058{_}1263{_}In reciting the office a priest should observe an attitude in harmony with the great work in which he is engaged, prayer to God.
10058{_}1264{_}Hence, his attitude should be becoming, on his knees, standing, sitting, walking, but not sprawling or lying.
10058{_}1265{_}The rubrics which prescribe kneeling, sitting, standing, apply to choir recitation only.
10058{_}1266{_}But writers recommend that in private recitation these directions should not be altogether omitted, and they say that the practice of these rubrics of kneeling, bowing, standing, etc., is laudable and an aid to devout recital.
10058{_}1267{_}ARTICLE V.--PRONUNCIATION OF THE WORDS.
10058{_}1268{_}What kind of pronunciation is to be attended to in the recitation of the Divine Office?
10058{_}1269{_}The pronunciation should be vocal--that is, there should be some sound, _aliquis sonitus verborum_, as St. Alphonsus writes (n. 162).
10058{_}1270{_}Hence, to read the Breviary merely mentally or with the eyes only, does not satisfy the obligation.
10058{_}1271{_}[A] Although the reader may not hear the sound produced, he must be careful to form with his lips every syllable.
10058{_}1272{_}This must be done, not necessarily in a throaty way.
10058{_}1273{_}The formation of the words clearly with the lips suffices.
10058{_}1274{_}But writers on this point emphasise the importance of audible recitation as a preventive of slurred, mutilated Latinity, which often leads to careless, or even invalid recitation.
10058{_}1275{_}They note, too, that the reading with the eye merely, is a habit which readers bring from the reading of other books to their reading of the Breviary.
10058{_}1276{_}German authors dwell at length on the fact that many priests, very early in their career, contract the habit of faulty vocalisation of liturgy, and that they never seem to notice their fault, or at least never seem to attempt an amendment.
10058{_}1277{_}These authors attribute the defect to sub-voce recitation and recommend audible recitation, long and frequent audible recitation, to all priests reading their hours.
10058{_}1278{_}[Footnote A: The privilege of mental recitation was granted to the Friar Minor by Pope Leo X. and Pius V., but it is probable that the privilege was withdrawn by Pope Gregory XV.
10058{_}1279{_}in 1622, in his letter _Romanus Pontifex_; and Urban VIII., 1635, withdrew all privileges granted _vivae vocis oraculo_.
10058{_}1280{_}The text of the document granting the privilege is obscurely worded.
10058{_}1281{_}Still, several theologians of repute maintain that the privilege still exists and extends to the whole office.
10058{_}1282{_}This is taught by the Salaraenticenses, _De hor.
10058{_}1283{_}can.
10058{_}1284{_}cap._ 3, n. 55; Tamburini, Rodriguez, etc., others opposed this view of the privilege existing after Pope Urban's letter _Alias_.
10058{_}1285{_}This privilege extends to secular priests who are Franciscan tertiaries, if it exists at all.]
10058{_}1286{_}Can a priest fulfil his obligation by reciting the office with a companion?
10058{_}1287{_}Yes, he can, for such recitation is the Church's ideal; and the priest who says his part (alternate verses, etc.
10058{_}1288{_}), as in choir, fulfils his obligation, even when his companion is a layman or an inattentive person.
10058{_}1289{_}In such recitation a priest should be careful (1) that his recitation be of alternate verses, (2) that the verse recitation be successive and not simultaneous, (3) that the verses, etc., chanted by one companion (or by one choir) be heard by the other companion or choir.
10058{_}1290{_}There is no necessity for a priest at such recitation to say one verse in a loud voice and to say his companion's verses in a low, inaudible voice.
10058{_}1291{_}Some priests do this with distressing results.
10058{_}1292{_}Imperfect vocal recitation often leads to doubts and scruples in old age when remedies either cannot be applied or prove useless.
10058{_}1293{_}Those who recite the office in choir are bound by the rubrics concerning kneeling, sitting, standing, etc.
10058{_}1294{_}Secondly, they are bound to observe the rules of the liturgy, especially the rule as to the stop in each verse at the asterisk mark.
10058{_}1295{_}Thirdly, they are bound to recite clearly and distinctly; but even if they cannot hear distinctly the alternate choir, or even if they recite in a low voice, they fulfil the obligation of recitation; and canons are bound at Cathedral offices to sing and chant or to lose their manual distributions and the fruits of their prebends.
10058{_}1296{_}If a person reciting his office with a companion or in a choir does not understand the words recited by his companion or by the choir, he is not bound at the end to repeat the part which he did not understand, because such a person has the intention of offering prayer and praise to God, and that intention suffices.
10058{_}1297{_}Moreover, the Church's precept of reciting the office should he interpreted benignly, otherwise it must give rise to many scruples; for, companions in recitation, then, always, should be anxious as to the duty of repetition or the non-fulfilled duty of complete recitation.
10058{_}1298{_}Pronunciation of the words of the office should be _integral_.
10058{_}1299{_}That is, the words and syllables are to be repeated fully without mutilation or abbreviation.
10058{_}1300{_}Hence, if mutilation of the words occur to such an extent that the sense or meaning of the words is notably changed, mortal sin may be committed.
10058{_}1301{_}But if the mutilation be small in quantity there is only a venial sin committed, and often no sin at all may be committed, as the mutilation of words or syllables may be quite involuntary, or may be done inadvertently, or may arise from an inveterate habit very difficult to correct, and in the attempt to cure it time and patience may have been spent (St.
10058{_}1302{_}Alph., 164-165).
10058{_}1303{_}This bad habit, if it extend over a large portion of the recitation and destroy notably the sense of the words, may bind _sub gravi_ to repetition, as this fault or habit affects the very substance of recitation.
10058{_}1304{_}Priests seldom are bound to such a repetition, as the mutilation is not destructive to the sense of a notable part of the office and hence does not affect the substance of the obligation to vocal recital.
10058{_}1305{_}St. Alphonsus holds (n. 165), that the obligation is fulfilled as long as the meaning is not destroyed, _quando servatur aliqua significatio verborum_.
10058{_}1306{_}Pronunciation should be _continuous_.
10058{_}1307{_}That is, the recitation of each hour should be continuous, non-interrupted, and every notable stoppage or break in the recitation of a canonical hour is a venial sin, if there be no excusing cause for such an interruption.
10058{_}1308{_}Any reasonable cause for interruption (e.g., to obey a bell call, to see a parishioner who calls, to hear a confession) excuses from all fault (St.
10058{_}1309{_}Alph., n. 168).
10058{_}1310{_}If the recital of the office for any canonical hour be interrupted, should the whole hour be repeated?
10058{_}1311{_}Some theologians say that it should be repeated.
10058{_}1312{_}But the more probable opinion denies that there is any such obligation; it holds that the union of the prayers prescribed by the Church is not broken, as each psalm, each lesson, each prayer, has a complete signification and they are united sufficiently in one round of prayer by the intention formed of continuing the Hour, or even by the actual continuation.
10058{_}1313{_}Gury states that a priest interrupting the office between the verses of a psalm is not bound to repeat the entire psalm on resuming the recitation, as he says each verse has its own signification.
10058{_}1314{_}May Matins be said separately from Lauds without any excusing cause?
10058{_}1315{_}Yes, for it was the practice of the early Church to say these parts of the liturgy at times separated by intervals.
10058{_}1316{_}But if Matins be said separately, without Lauds following immediately.
10058{_}1317{_}_Pater Noster_ with Dominus Vobiscum and the prayer of the day should be said at the end of the _Te Deum_, If Lauds follow Matins immediately the _Pater_ and _Ave_ should not be said, for the Congregation (same decree) says "_Laudes incohandas ut in Psalterio_," but in the Psalter the _Pater_ and _Ave_ are not assigned for the beginning of Lauds.
10058{_}1318{_}A notable time may elapse between the nocturns of Matins without any excusing cause.
10058{_}1319{_}In the early Church intervals occurred between each nocturn.
10058{_}1320{_}Some authors state that an interval of three hours between two nocturns is quite lawful, even when there be no cause for the delay.
10058{_}1321{_}With a reasonable cause the interval may last as long as the excusing cause requires.
10058{_}1322{_}ARTICLE VI.--INTENTION AND ATTENTION.
10058{_}1323{_}The valid recitation of the Divine Office requires that the priest should have in his mind an intention of praying, for the Divine Office is a true and real prayer, not a mere vocal exercise.
10058{_}1324{_}Hence, a priest reading his office as a mere study or as a means of remembering the words of the psalms does not validly recite his office (St.
10058{_}1325{_}Alph., n. 176).
10058{_}1326{_}Now, what sort of intention is best and what sort of intention is necessary?
10058{_}1327{_}An actual, explicit intention which states expressly when the Breviary is opened, "I intend to pray," is the best intention.
10058{_}1328{_}The devout recital of the prayer "_Aperi Domine_" expresses well the best form of the actual, explicit intentions of those reciting the office.
10058{_}1329{_}But such an express, actual intention is not necessary; a virtual intention, which finds expression in the opening of the Breviary to recite the office, suffices.
10058{_}1330{_}The mere opening of the book, the finding out of the office, the arrangement of the book markers, are ample evidence of the existence of a virtual intention quite sufficient for the valid recitation of the office.
10058{_}1331{_}St. Alphonsus writes, "_Imo puto semper adesse exercite, intentionem actualem implendi officium_" (n. 176).
10058{_}1332{_}This question of intention gives great trouble to the timid and scrupulous, whose doubts and difficulties seem hard to solve.
10058{_}1333{_}The common sense and common practice in everyday affairs seem to desert some people when they prepare to read the canonical hours.
10058{_}1334{_}For, who has not seen the nervous, pious, anxious cleric, stupidly labouring to acquire even a sufficient intention before beginning his hours?
10058{_}1335{_}Attention in reading the hours is a much more discussed and much more difficult mental effort.
10058{_}1336{_}It means the application of the mind to the thing in which we are engaged.
10058{_}1337{_}When we listen to a conversation or when we write a letter the mind is fixed and attentive to the matter spoken or written.
10058{_}1338{_}Intention is an act of the will; attention is an act of the understanding.
10058{_}1339{_}Attention may be either external or internal.
10058{_}1340{_}External attention is attention of such a kind that it excludes every exterior action physically incompatible with the recitation of the office--e.g., to write or type a letter, to listen attentively to those conversing, are acts incompatible with the simultaneous recitation of the office.
10058{_}1341{_}But walking, poking a fire, looking for the lessons, whilst reciting from memory all the time, are not incompatible with the external attention required in office recital; because such acts do not require mental effort which could count as a serious disturbing element.
10058{_}1342{_}However, in this matter of external attention no rule can be formulated for all Breviary readers; for what may lightly disturb and distract one reader may have no effect on another, and yet may seriously disturb the recitation of another (St.
10058{_}1343{_}Alph., n. 176).
10058{_}1344{_}External attention is necessary for the valid recitation of the office.
10058{_}1345{_}Internal attention is application or advertence of the mind.
10058{_}1346{_}Is such internal attention, such deliberate application or mental advertence necessary for the valid recitation of the office?
10058{_}1347{_}There are two opinions on this matter, two replies to the question.
10058{_}1348{_}According to one opinion, and this is the more common and the more probable one, internal attention is required for the valid recitation of the Hours.
10058{_}1349{_}1.
10058{_}1350{_}Because the Divine Office is a prayer, but there can be no true or real prayer without internal attention, for prayer is defined as an elevation of the soul to God, but if there be no internal attention, there is no elevation of the soul to God, and no prayer.
10058{_}1351{_}2.
10058{_}1352{_}Our Lord complained of those who had external attention at prayer, but lacked internal attention or advertence, "This people honour me with their lips, but their heart is far from me" (St. Matt.
10058{_}1353{_}xv.).
10058{_}1354{_}3.
10058{_}1355{_}The Church appears to demand internal attention at prayer, for although she has not given any positive precept dealing with this kind of attention, she does the same thing when she commands that the recitation of the Divine Office take the form of prayer for God's honour, and this recitation of words cannot be true prayer without internal attention.
10058{_}1356{_}4.
10058{_}1357{_}The Council of Trent seems to exact this attention when it wishes that the Divine Office be said reverently, distinctly and devoutly, reverenter, distincte, devote.
10058{_}1358{_}5.
10058{_}1359{_}If no internal attention be required in reciting the Hours, it is difficult to see how voluntary distractions are forbidden by Divine Law.
10058{_}1360{_}This is the opinion held by Cajetan (1496-1534), Sa (1530-1596), Azor (1539-1603), Sanchez (1550-1610), Roncaglai (1677-1737), Concina (1687-1756), and St. Alphonsus, the great Doctor of prayer (1696-1787).
10058{_}1361{_}According to the other opinion, external attention suffices always and ever to satisfy substantially the obligation of reading the office and for the avoidance of mortal sin which invalid recitation entails.
10058{_}1362{_}For,  (1) To pray is to speak to God, to trust in Him, to manifest to Him the wishes and wants of the soul; but this can be done by a person who has voluntary distractions of mind, just as a man can read to his king an address, setting forth the thanks and requests of his subjects, although the reader's mind is far from dwelling on the words or the meaning of the sentences before his eyes.
10058{_}1363{_}But he is careful to read all the words in a clear, intelligible manner.
