10058{_}1364{_}Now the theologians who maintain this opinion say that, _a fortiori_, this method of reading the Hours should be valid; for, in the reading the priest acts principally in the name of the Church, as her minister, and offers up prayers to God in her name, and they say that the irreverence of the servant does not render the prayer of the Church unpleasing to Him,  (2) He who makes a vow, and resolves to do a certain act, fulfils his vow, even when fulfilling it he acts with voluntary distractions; so, a pari, with the recitation of the office,  (3) The administration of the sacraments--even the administration of Extreme Unction, the form of which is a prayer--with full voluntary distractions is valid; so, too, should be the recital of Breviary prayers.
10058{_}1365{_}(4) In the other opinion it is hard to see how, if voluntary distractions destroy the substance of prayer, involuntary distractions do not produce similar effect, and hence, there can be no prayer if there be distraction of any kind.
10058{_}1366{_}This opinion was held by Lugo (1583-1660), Gobat (1600-1679), Sporer (1609-1683), St. Antonnius (1389-1459), and other eminent men.
10058{_}1367{_}It is quoted by St. Alphonsus, as _satis probabilis_.
10058{_}1368{_}Of it, Lehmkuhl writes, "Quae ad substantiam divini officii dicamus satis probabiliter sufficere cum intentione orandi observasse attentionem externam" (II.
10058{_}1369{_}635).
10058{_}1370{_}What are the divisions or kinds of internal attention?
10058{_}1371{_}I. Objectively they are (1) spiritual attention, (2) literal attention, (3) superficial or material attention.
10058{_}1372{_}Spiritual attention is that advertence of soul which tends towards God, the Term of all prayer, when the soul meditates on the power, wisdom, goodness of God, on the Passion, on the Mother of God, on God's saints.
10058{_}1373{_}Literal attention is that which strives to lay hold of the meaning of the words said in the office.
10058{_}1374{_}Superficial attention is that advertence of soul which applies itself to the correct recitation of the words, avoiding errors of pronunciation, mutilation, transposition, etc., etc.
10058{_}1375{_}II.
10058{_}1376{_}Subjectively, virtual attention suffices; habitual is divided into actual and interpretative.
10058{_}1377{_}Actual attention is that which exists at the moment--e.g., the attention paid by a pupil to a question put by a teacher.
10058{_}1378{_}Virtual attention is attention which was once actual, but is not such at the time spoken of, but which lives virtually.
10058{_}1379{_}Habitual is attention which once was actual, which does not remain in act, but which was not retracted.
10058{_}1380{_}Interpretative attention is that which never existed at all, but which would have existed if the agent had adverted.
10058{_}1381{_}Which kind of internal attention is required in the reading of the Office?
10058{_}1382{_}I. Objectively, material, or superficial attention is necessary, since the Breviary is a vocal prayer, and therefore it is necessary to pronounce distinctly all the words of the day's office and to observe the rubrics.
10058{_}1383{_}But this suffices; it is not necessary that a priest reciting his Hours should carefully notice each word, it is sufficient to have general and moral attention to recite every part well, and with the intention of praying, "Sed sufficere moralem et generalem qua quis curet bene omnia dicere cum intentione orandi" (St. Alphonsus).
10058{_}1384{_}Hence, objectively, neither attention, which is called spiritual, because it is not easy to attain, nor the literal attention, which religious who do not understand Latin strive after, is needed for valid recitation.
10058{_}1385{_}By this, it is not meant to convey that spiritual attention is not very excellent and very commendable and praiseworthy.
10058{_}1386{_}Subjectively, virtual attention suffices; habitual does not suffice, neither does interpretative.
10058{_}1387{_}Best of all is actual attention, but it is not necessary, because it is not always within the power of mortals.
10058{_}1388{_}This want of internal attention is called mental distraction.
10058{_}1389{_}Theologians distinguish two kinds of distractions, voluntary and involuntary.
10058{_}1390{_}Voluntary distractions are thoughts which the mind freely and directly embraces to the exclusion of pious thoughts which should occupy it in prayer, of which the office is a high form; or they may be thoughts which arise from previous laziness, thoughtlessness, pre-occupation or some engrossing worldly affair.
10058{_}1391{_}Involuntary distractions are those which come unbidden and unsought to the mind, are neither placed directly, nor by their causes, by the person at prayer.
10058{_}1392{_}Does a person reciting the Hours sin if he have distractions?
10058{_}1393{_}If the distractions be involuntary there is no sin.
10058{_}1394{_}But if the distractions be voluntary there is sin, But, unless the mind be altogether filled with distractions, not thinking of God, of prayer, of the words or of the meaning, and unless the distractions are _fully voluntary_ and _reflective_ during a notable part of the office, there is no mortal sin.
10058{_}1395{_}Hence, St. Alphonsus, the great Doctor of Prayer, wrote, "_ut dicatur aliquis officio non satisfacere, non solum requiritur ut voluntarie se distrahat, sed etiam ut plene advertat se distrahi, nam alias iste, licet sponte se divertat non tamen sponte se divertit a recitatione_" (St. Alphonsus, n. 177).
10058{_}1396{_}Therefore, before a person accuse himself of not satisfying the precept of recitation, on account of inattention or distractions, he must be able to affirm positively (1)that he was wilfully distracted, (2)he must have noticed not only his distraction and mental occupation by vain thoughts, but he must have noticed _also_ that he was distracted in his recitation; (3)he must be able to state positively that the intention, resolution or desire to recite piously, which he made at the beginning of his prayer, was revoked with full advertence and that it did not exist either actually or virtually during the time of distraction in his recitation.
10058{_}1397{_}Seldom, indeed, are these conditions fulfilled, and seldom are there gravely sinful distractions.
10058{_}1398{_}This subject of attention in prayer, in the official prayer of the Church, is important.
10058{_}1399{_}Long and learned disputes about its nature and requirements occupied great thinkers in times long gone by.
10058{_}1400{_}To-day theologians argue on different sides; and anxiety, serious, painful and life-long, reigns in the souls of many who struggle to recite the office, _digne, attente ac devote_.
10058{_}1401{_}ARTICLE VII.-CAUSES WHICH EXCUSE FROM READING THE OFFICE.
10058{_}1402{_}Authors generally give six causes which excuse a person from saying the Hours: lawful dispensation, important work, grave illness, grave fear, blindness, want of a Breviary.
10058{_}1403{_}They are recorded in the well-known lines:--  "Quem Papa dispenset multus labor opprimit aeger Qui timet aut occulus, officioque caret."
10058{_}1404{_}1.
10058{_}1405{_}The obligation of reading the Office is imposed by the Church and the Pope can dispense in it even without cause.
10058{_}1406{_}Bishops can give temporary dispensations.
10058{_}1407{_}2.
10058{_}1408{_}A grave occupation excuses from the whole or from a part of the Office.
10058{_}1409{_}Thus, missioners giving missions or parish retreats are excused from the whole Office; so, too, are priest combatants in the battle line; but when in rest camps they are bound to say the Hours.
10058{_}1410{_}A priest engaged in saying his Office, if he receive an urgent call to a dying person may not have time to finish his Office before midnight.
10058{_}1411{_}He is exempt from the part of the Office omitted and does not sin by the omission.
10058{_}1412{_}The proposition claiming exemption from the Office for those engaged in great studies was condemned by Pope Alexander VII.
10058{_}1413{_}The biographers of Lamennais trace the beginning of his downfall to his exemption from his daily Office.
10058{_}1414{_}A difficulty arises sometimes as regards the full or partial or non-exemption of those who foresee that serious occupation which cannot be neglected must arise to prevent the recitation of the Hours.
10058{_}1415{_}In such cases priests are bound to recite the Office, or as much of it as possible, within the limits of the current day.
10058{_}1416{_}In doing this they may anticipate the times fixed for the recitation of the small Hours, and they may anticipate Vespers and Compline by reciting them in the forenoon.
10058{_}1417{_}If a priest foresees that he may not be able to recite Matins for next day he is not bound to anticipate, as there is no obligation to anticipation; the obligation is "recital between midnight and midnight."
10058{_}1418{_}It is becoming to anticipate, if possible, so that the Office may be full and entire.
10058{_}1419{_}If before midnight there be a cessation from necessary professional work (e.g., hearing confessions), a priest is bound to finish his Office for the day or to say as much of it as time allows.
10058{_}1420{_}If, however, there be time merely to take a necessary meal before midnight (e.g., to prepare for a late Mass on next day, Sunday), and not time to eat and to recite, the obligation of saying the Hours ceases.
10058{_}1421{_}A grave illness exempts from the saying of the canonical Hours.
10058{_}1422{_}Hence, those seriously ill, those who fear the saying of the Office may upset them in their weak state, and convalescents from a serious illness, are excused from saying the Hours.
10058{_}1423{_}In this matter the advice of a spiritual or a medical adviser should be faithfully carried out by patients.
10058{_}1424{_}St. Alphonsus teaches that invalids and convalescents may be allowed to say Mass and yet not be bound to say the Office, as the saying of Mass does not fatigue them so much as the saying of the Office (St. Alphonsus, n. 155).
10058{_}1425{_}A grave fear exempts from the saying of the Office.
10058{_}1426{_}A priest amongst furious persecutors of the Church should be excused from any recitation of his Hours which he fears may draw on him cruel or severe punishments.
10058{_}1427{_}Blindness makes the recitation of the Office a physical impossibility.
10058{_}1428{_}Even very defective sight, although not total blindness, exempts from the obligation of saying the Office.
10058{_}1429{_}In all such cases a formal declaration of exemption should be sought.
10058{_}1430{_}Some theologians hold that such priests, if they have committed to memory a notable part of the psalms, should repeat that part from memory.
10058{_}1431{_}The new psaltery makes such memorising an extremely difficult feat and no obligation for such a repetition from memory can be imposed.
10058{_}1432{_}Want of a Breviary excuses from the recitation of the Office.
10058{_}1433{_}For example, if a priest setting out on a long journey forgets to take his Breviary or leaves it in a railway carriage, and cannot procure another, or cannot procure another without, great inconvenience, he is exempt from the obligation of his Office; and the omission being involuntary is sinless.
10058{_}1434{_}The wilful casting away of a Breviary, as an excuse for not being able to read the Office, is gravely sinful; and unless the sinful desire be retracted there may be question of many mortal sins of wilful omission to fulfil the obligation, as the omissions are then wilful in cause.
10058{_}1435{_}Priests travelling are unable sometimes to recite the proper Office of the day, as their Breviaries lack something (e.g., the proper prayer or the lessons of the second nocturn).
10058{_}1436{_}The Sacred Congregation of Rites (December, 1854) decided "_Sacerdos peregre profectus cui molesti difficiliorque esset officii recitatio cui et pauca desunt in libro officii praesentis, nempe oratio et legenda, valet de communi absque obligatione propria deinde ad supplementum recitandi... atque ita servari mandavit_."
10058{_}1437{_}The psalms as arranged in the new psalter must always be said for a valid recitation of the Office (_v.
10058{_}1438{_}Divino Afflatu_).
10058{_}1439{_}What is a priest bound to do, who from a grave cause cannot find time to recite the whole Office but only a part of it?
10058{_}1440{_}St. Alphonsus gives the rule, "If you can recite a part equivalent to a small Hour, you are bound to do so under pain of mortal sin.
10058{_}1441{_}But if you cannot read or repeat a part equivalent to a small Hour, you are bound to nothing, as a part so small--less than a small Hour--taken separately, is considered inappreciable for the end the Church's law of recitation has in view."
10058{_}1442{_}ARTICLE VIII.--THE DIRECTION OF THE SCRUPULOUS.
10058{_}1443{_}Persons who are scrupulous about the recitation of the Hours should have help from their confessors, who should deal specifically with any of the scruples which arise in the daily task.
10058{_}1444{_}Scruples generally concern the necessary intention, the necessary attention, pronunciation, and the time necessary for a good and faithful recitation of the canonical Hours.
10058{_}1445{_}How should a confessor deal with scruples about intention?
10058{_}1446{_}A confessor should tell a cleric, scrupulous in this point, that his fear is groundless and that by the very act of taking up his Breviary he expresses his intention of praying, of saying his Hours; that it is not necessary that such intention be actual or reflexive, it is sufficient if it be virtual, and that such an intention _does_ exist every time one opens the Breviary to say his Hours.
10058{_}1447{_}The saying slowly and deliberately the prayer "_Aperi Domine_" is a great aid to the scrupulous in forming a right intention and in dispelling their vain fears.
10058{_}1448{_}Clerics troubled about attention are helped and comforted by their confessor repeating to them what they well know themselves, about voluntary and involuntary distractions, and the telling of the anxious ones that this very anxiety and anguish show that their fear of losing attention in their prayer is a true and real sign of its existence.
10058{_}1449{_}In dealing with scruples about vocal and integral pronunciation a confessor should advise that no stopping should be made in the saying of the psalms, etc., but that the recitation should be continued quietly, without restraining the voice, without impatience, and without scrutiny of the pronunciation of the part said, "God is a father, full of goodness, not an exacting taskmaster, and He is more honoured by moderate care than by a disturbing solicitude."
10058{_}1450{_}Above all things, a confessor should remember that it is important to forbid scrupulous persons to repeat the whole or even the part of an Hour.
10058{_}1451{_}An effort should be made by him to tranquilise the troubled soul with the principle that the precepts of the Church do not bind him to repeat the Hours with such inconvenience as leads to bodily and mental illness.
10058{_}1452{_}The Church is our mother and does not wish her children to be troubled and solicitous, but to pray in peace.
10058{_}1453{_}CHAPTER IV.
10058{_}1454{_}SOME RULES OF ASCETIC THEOLOGY FOR THE PIOUS RECITATION OF THE BREVIARY.
10058{_}1455{_}There are many reasons why we should recite the Divine Office devoutly, for (1) the words which we read are holy; (2) He to Whom we speak is God; (3) we speak in the name of Holy Church; (4) we are the associates of thousands on earth and in heaven who sing God's praises; (5) the purpose of our prayer is sublime; (6) it gives glory to God and draws down His grace and mercy on His Church; (7) and, finally, the recitation of the Office brings help and strength to those who repeat it fervently.
10058{_}1456{_}And, firstly, let us see what are the words of the Office.
10058{_}1457{_}They are the words of God or of His Church.
10058{_}1458{_}In the psalms, scripture lessons, gospel extracts, responses and antiphons, we have God's inspired word.
10058{_}1459{_}In the prayers, sermons, homilies, hymns, and often in the responses and antiphons, as the Church is guided and assisted by the Holy Ghost, it may be, in a sense, true to say that these her words are divine.
10058{_}1460{_}For what is more worthy of respect than the word of God?
10058{_}1461{_}St. Augustine says that it is no less worthy of respect than the body of Jesus Christ.
10058{_}1462{_}_Non minus est verbum Dei quam corpus Christi_ (Sermon 300).
10058{_}1463{_}How very careful should we be to treat the word of God with respect, worthily, attentively, and devoutly (_digne, attente ac devote_).
10058{_}1464{_}(2) To whom do we speak in our daily service of prayer?
10058{_}1465{_}We speak to our Master, Whose very special work we are doing in offering up the great prayer.
10058{_}1466{_}His adorable eyes are fixed upon us at this sacred duty.
10058{_}1467{_}He listens to us, He reads our thoughts.
10058{_}1468{_}He judges our intentions, our efforts and their fulfilment.
10058{_}1469{_}He is the King of kings, the Almighty God.
10058{_}1470{_}Mindful of His presence and majesty should we not try earnestly to bless His Holy name and to free our hearts from vain, evil and wandering thoughts?
10058{_}1471{_}We pray _ad benedicendum nomen sanctum tuum; munda quoque cor meum ab omnibus vanis perversis et alienis cogitationibus_.
10058{_}1472{_}(3) In whose name do we speak?
10058{_}1473{_}It is a great honour to be an ambassador for a great king and a mighty kingdom, guarding the interests of the fatherland in a foreign land.
10058{_}1474{_}The priest is always such an ambassador.
10058{_}1475{_}"For Christ, we are ambassadors," says St. Paul.
10058{_}1476{_}In this work of daily recitation of the Office, we are ambassadors, not of some petty king or tiny state, but we represent the entire Church, the well-beloved spouse of Christ, to whose prayer He ever hearkens.
10058{_}1477{_}_Sonet vox tua in auribus meis; vox enim tua dulcis est_ (Canticle of Canticles, ii.
10058{_}1478{_}14).
10058{_}1479{_}And St. Bernard says "_Sacerdos publica persona et totius Ecclesie os_."
10058{_}1480{_}Hence, every priest is the ambassador of Christ and of His Church, the guardian of His interests.
10058{_}1481{_}And as it is the duty of ambassadors to study carefully, to watch and further the interests of the kings whom they represent, it is a priest's duty to study carefully and further the interests of Christ's Church by the devout fulfilment of the great daily duty, the recitation of the Divine Office.
10058{_}1482{_}History brands as traitors those ambassadors who through ignorance of the language of the foreign court, or through want of vigilant attention, allow the interests of their royal masters to suffer.
10058{_}1483{_}What a punishment awaits the days and years of ignorant, careless or inattentive fulfilment of the great official work of a priest--the Divine Office.
10058{_}1484{_}Who are a priest's associates in this work?
10058{_}1485{_}They are the thousands of priests and religious throughout the world who say the Hours, and who send up daily and nightly the great prayer of praise and thanksgiving to God.
10058{_}1486{_}_Secundum nomen tuum, sic et laus tua in fines terrae_ (ps.
10058{_}1487{_}47, v. ii).
10058{_}1488{_}_Dies diei eructat verbum et nox nocti indicat scientiam_ (ps.
10058{_}1489{_}18, v. 3).
10058{_}1490{_}In this holy work of reciting the Hours, we are united with the angels and saints in heaven in honouring our common Creator; for, the Church herself reminds us of this ineffable honour in the hymn for the dedication of the Church:--            "Sed ilia sedes Coelitum            Semper resultat laudibus            Dumque trinum el unicum            Jugi canore jungimur            Almae Sionis aemuli."
10058{_}1491{_}"That house on high--it ever rings       With praises of the King of kings;       For ever there, on harps divine,       They hymn th' eternal One and Trine       We, here below, the strain prolong;,       And faintly echo Sion's song."
10058{_}1492{_}What are the ends for which the Office is said?
10058{_}1493{_}(a) To glorify God, (b) to help holy Church, and (c) to sanctify ourselves.
10058{_}1494{_}(a) "To glorify God," that is, to adore His infinite majesty, to thank Him for his innumerable and constant blessings, to satisfy His justice in expiating the sins of the world and to beg His grace and mercy.
10058{_}1495{_}The ends for which the Office are said are the same as those for which Mass is offered, for the Office is the supplement of the Mass (Tronson).
10058{_}1496{_}(b) "To help holy Church."
10058{_}1497{_}The Church militant has many and great needs.
10058{_}1498{_}It is her mission to extend the Kingdom of Christ, and to do this great work she needs freedom from hostile laws, strength and courage to withstand tyrants and persecution, unity and peace amongst her children and pastors, zeal in her ministers and recruits for her militant forces.
10058{_}1499{_}To obtain these results the Church relies very much on the devout recitation of the Office.
10058{_}1500{_}Doubtless, it is for these purposes that the Church has confided to the care of her chosen ministers this public official prayer and has laid no such obligation on the laity.
10058{_}1501{_}St. Alphonsus did not hesitate to say that if priests and religious said the Office as they should say it, the Church should not be in the deplorable state that it then was in.
10058{_}1502{_}This Doctor of the Church adds "that by devout saying of the Office many sinners could be drawn from the slavery of the devil and many souls would love God with more fervour."
10058{_}1503{_}The wants of the Church are greater now than they were ever before.
10058{_}1504{_}Each devoutly-said Hour draws down God's blessing on His Church.
10058{_}1505{_}What a vast number of blessings come from a life of daily recitation offered worthily, attentively and devoutly (_digne, attente, ac devote_).
10058{_}1506{_}(c) "The benefit of the person who recites the Hours."
10058{_}1507{_}The third end for which the canonical Hours are offered is for the benefit of the person who recites them.
10058{_}1508{_}St. Alphonsus wrote, "If they said the Office as they ought, priests themselves should not be always the same, always imperfect, prone to anger, greedy, attached to self-interest and to vanities....
10058{_}1509{_}But if they recited the Office, not as they say it with distractions and irreverences, but with devotion and recollection, uniting the affections of the heart with so many petitions which they present to God, they should certainly not be so weak as they are, but would acquire fervour and strength to resist all temptations and to lead a life worthy of priests."
10058{_}1510{_}Another blessing springs from the attentive recitation of the Breviary--viz., the daily withdrawal from the world and its cares which must be banished from the soul which speaks with God.
10058{_}1511{_}For, as St. Alphonsus writes, the saying of the Hours devoutly, gives occasion to pious souls to elicit many acts of virtue, acts of faith, of hope, of charity, of humility, etc.
10058{_}1512{_}For one psalm, says the saint, moves all the powers of the soul and causes us to elicit a hundred acts.
10058{_}1513{_}And in the Breviary are found the most beautiful formulae of adoration and praise, the psalms above all other parts of the Office being wonderfully rich in magnificent praise of God's attributes.
10058{_}1514{_}Where can such sublime forms of prayer and praise be found as in Psalms, 8, 9, 17, 18, 21, 23, 28, 29, 33, 45, 46, 49, 54--to name but a few?
10058{_}1515{_}Finally, the attentive recitation of the Breviary is a source of light and of grace and of merit.
10058{_}1516{_}How many lights in prayer spring from these divine words; how many maxims enter the soul, how many beautiful prayers are said, and if they be well said, they would obtain for priests treasures of grace, according to Christ's infallible promise, "Ask and you shall receive"?
10058{_}1517{_}A person can merit several degrees of glory by one devout recitation of the Office, what an abundance of merit may be gained by the devout recitations in a life of twenty, thirty or forty years!
10058{_}1518{_}And it was this thought of lost opportunities and of the great treasures within the reach of priests, which caused St. Alphonsus when an old man, to study the Breviary psalms and to write his well-known work.
10058{_}1519{_}Nor was St. Alphonsus alone in his opinion of the great means of sanctification which the Breviary affords to priests.
10058{_}1520{_}St. Joseph of Cupertino (1603-1663) was asked by Monsignor Claver, Bishop of Potenza, to point out a means for the greater sanctification of the priests of his diocese.
10058{_}1521{_}The saint replied, "Monsignor, if you wish to sanctify your priests strive to procure two things for them, that they say the Office piously and that they say Mass with fervour.
10058{_}1522{_}Nothing more is necessary to ensure their salvation" (_Life of St. Joseph Cupertino_ by Bernini).
10058{_}1523{_}The words of the wonderful Franciscan, whose life was a marvel of piety, were repeated a century later by St. Leonard of Port Maurice (1671-1751) and are often quoted as his own.
10058{_}1524{_}In every age of the Church earnest souls drew great sweetness and consolation from reading the psalms or from reading the canonical Hours.
10058{_}1525{_}Writers dealing with this part of priestly work quote the words of eminent servants of God, They quote St. Augustine, St. Gregory Nazianzan, St. Bernard, St. Catherine of Bologna, St. Philip Neri, St. Francis De Sales and St. Alphonsus.
10058{_}1526{_}It would make this section of this book too long to quote the words of these saints.
10058{_}1527{_}But the words of St. Francis De Sales seem to have a special force.
10058{_}1528{_}"Sometimes I am so low-spirited," wrote the Saint, "by business and events, that I do not know where to turn nor at what end to begin: but during the Office nothing annoys me, I have not even distractions, I imagine that I am in heaven singing with the angels the praises of my Creator; and on leaving the choir I find often that the mighty problems which had given me trouble are cleared away and, solved in an Instant."
10058{_}1529{_}Biographies of God's servants record many great favours bestowed on priests who recite the Breviary piously.
10058{_}1530{_}Cardinal Bona, recording a vision vouchsafed to St. Bernard, tells how the saint saw an angel beside each choir monk, recording his disposition of soul.
10058{_}1531{_}Some angels wrote in letters of gold, others in letters of silver, others in ink, others in water, and others held their pens but wrote nothing.
10058{_}1532{_}Our Lord explained to the saint the meaning of the vision; the writing in gold typified charity and the fervour of the recitation; the writing in silver denoted devotion but little charity or fervour; the words in ink-writing signified careful attention to the full verbal recitation but to little else; the words written in water meant distraction and little attention to the meaning or to the words; and the angels who wrote nothing watched the insolence of those who were voluntarily distracted.
10058{_}1533{_}The vision has furnished the theme of much pious writing and a theme for Christian painters.
10058{_}1534{_}It shows how God watches over the daily work of priests, while His angels record in golden or silvern letters the work of pious recitation, or perhaps hold their pens at rest.
10058{_}1535{_}What means should be used to promote pious recitation?
10058{_}1536{_}ARTICLE II.--THE MEANS TO ADOPT FOR THE PIOUS RECITATION OF THE BREVIARY.
10058{_}1537{_}A.--THE MEANS TO ADOPT BEFORE THE RECITATION.
10058{_}1538{_}Preparation is necessary before beginning every prayer, for the Holy Ghost says, "Before prayer prepare thy soul, and be not as a man that tempteth God" (Ecclesias.
10058{_}1539{_}18.
10058{_}1540{_}23).
10058{_}1541{_}This preparation necessary before other prayers is above all necessary before the recitation of the Divine Office, which is the greatest of all prayers.
10058{_}1542{_}Two kinds of preparation are necessary, the remote and the proximate.
10058{_}1543{_}The remote preparation demands the removal of all obstacles which impede prayer, and the greatest of all prayers, the Church's official prayer.
10058{_}1544{_}The chief or capital obstacles which impede or prevent a pious recitation of the Breviary are: sin, the passions, the absorbing thoughts of creatures and the ignorance of the Divine Office.
10058{_}1545{_}And the means to remove these obstacles are to purify the conscience, science, to mortify the passions, to guard the sense and to have an intelligent knowledge of the duty and requirements of a proper fulfilment of the daily task of the saying of the Canonical Hours.
10058{_}1546{_}The first means is to purify the conscience from sin, for sin hinders prayer.
10058{_}1547{_}But what effect has sin on the recitation of the Office?
10058{_}1548{_}The Office is a prayer, an elevation of the soul to God, and as all writers on ascetics teach, sin is a chain that binds us to earth; it is, says St. Francis, as birdlime which impedes the soul in its flight upwards.
10058{_}1549{_}Prayer is a conversation with God, but a soul loving sin cannot converse with God; "_Peccatores Deus non audit_" (St. John, ix.
10058{_}1550{_}31).
10058{_}1551{_}Prayer is an intimate union with God, but a soul resting in sin can have no intimate union with God; there can be no intimate union between light and darkness, between sanctity and sin, between good and evil; in a word, between Christ and Belial.
10058{_}1552{_}_Quae participatio, quae societas lucis ad tenebras?
10058{_}1553{_}Quae conventio Christi el Belial?_  The second means of procuring fervent prayer is the mortification of the passions.
10058{_}1554{_}It is not enough to secure fervour in prayer that our souls should be free from sin; we must struggle to master our passions.
10058{_}1555{_}This point is important--for a soul upset by its passions, anger, pride, etc., cannot with fervour recite the Hours, for it cannot converse with God, it cannot elevate itself to God, it can have no true union with God.
10058{_}1556{_}It cannot converse with God, for God will not converse with an unmortified soul for three reasons.
10058{_}1557{_}First, He will not speak if there be no one to listen, for the Holy Ghost tells us "Where there is no hearing, pour not out words" (Eccli.
10058{_}1558{_}xxxii.
10058{_}1559{_}6).
10058{_}1560{_}God wishes a soul in converse with Him to be calm and still, for God is not in the earthquake (3 Kings, xix.
10058{_}1561{_}ii.).
10058{_}1562{_}Again, even if God speaks to an unmortified soul, it cannot hear Him as the passions fix its attention on worldly matters.
10058{_}1563{_}And even when such a soul tries to listen and to understand, the passions surging and warring drown all sound and sense of holy things.
10058{_}1564{_}For, "the animal man perceiveth not these things that are of the spirit of God, for it is foolishness to him and he cannot understand, because it is spiritually examined" (I. Cor.
10058{_}1565{_}ii.
10058{_}1566{_}14).
10058{_}1567{_}The human soul cannot truly unite itself to God if the passions are not conquered, because by their very nature they are opposed to God and hence inspire estrangement from, and disgust for, holy things.
10058{_}1568{_}Thirdly, the senses must be guarded.
10058{_}1569{_}Our five senses can impede the recitation of the Office because they present to our souls images of the things which occupy them, and they can draw our will towards the pleasures which correspond with these objects.
10058{_}1570{_}It is necessary for the worthy, attentive and devout saying of the Office that each sense be guarded.
10058{_}1571{_}The sense of sight should be guarded from gazing at objects at hand, persons, books, landscape, etc.
10058{_}1572{_}The sense of hearing should be guarded in flying from the company of evil speakers, calumniators, detractors, those who speak of worldly affairs or who give evil counsel.
10058{_}1573{_}It is necessary, too, to guard the tongue from evil speech.
10058{_}1574{_}"I have set a guard to my mouth, when the sinner stood against me" (Psalm 38, 2); and it is well to guard against too frequent or too long conversations, which fill the soul with thoughts disturbing to a prayerful disposition.
10058{_}1575{_}The sense of touch should likewise be guarded, for St. Thomas says that the sense of touch is the maintenance of the other senses (1 P. q.
10058{_}1576{_}76, a.
10058{_}1577{_}75).
10058{_}1578{_}And when the foundations of a house commence to fall asunder, the walls, the frame and the roof totter and fall.
10058{_}1579{_}So it is with the senses; when the sense of touch is disturbed the other senses quickly complete the ruin.
10058{_}1580{_}What knowledge is needed for the valid and for the licit recitation of the Hours?
10058{_}1581{_}Must the person know the meaning of the words read?
10058{_}1582{_}No such knowledge is necessary, for God hears the prayer of the ignorant and illiterate and of the babes.
10058{_}1583{_}To the chief priests and scribes, who hearing the children crying out the Saviour's praise in the temple, Christ said "Yea, have you not read 'Out of the mouths of infants and sucklings thou hast perfected praise'" (St. Matth.
10058{_}1584{_}xxi.
10058{_}1585{_}15-16), St. Augustine defended from the sneers of the learned, those who prayed to God in rude and barbarous words, or words which they did not understand.
10058{_}1586{_}"_Noverint non esse vocem ad aures Dei nisi animi affectum_" (_De Catech._ Rud.
10058{_}1587{_}C.I.).
10058{_}1588{_}The Church has bound religious, both men and women, to say the Office in choir, even though they may not understand Latin.
10058{_}1589{_}Nevertheless, it is highly desirable that those who understand Latin should understand what they read daily in the Breviary.
10058{_}1590{_}God, the Church, the practice of the saints, our own intelligence, our spiritual advantage, demand that every priest should read with knowledge so that with more certainty he may read attentively and devoutly.
10058{_}1591{_}For (1) the Holy Ghost warns us to sing wisely, _Psallite sapienter_ (Ps.
10058{_}1592{_}46.8); (2) that priests may sing wisely, may say the daily Office piously is the reason and end of liturgical studies of the psalms and of the Breviary in theological colleges; (3) the saints who wrote so piously and so learnedly on the psalms and on psalmody are for ever impressing this matter of intelligent recitation.
10058{_}1593{_}St. Augustine wrote, "_Et quare dicta sunt, nisi ut sciantur?
10058{_}1594{_}Quare sonuerunt nisi ut audiantur?
10058{_}1595{_}Quare audita sunt nisi ut intelligantur_" (Tract xxxi.
10058{_}1596{_}in Joan).
10058{_}1597{_}Again, commenting on psalm 146, he writes, "David teaches that we sing wisely; let us not seek the mere sound for the ear, but a light for the soul."
10058{_}1598{_}St. Thomas Aquinas commenting on "For I pray in a tongue, my spirit prayeth, but my understanding is without fruit" (I. Cor.
10058{_}1599{_}xiv.
10058{_}1600{_}14) wrote "_Constat quod plus lucratur qui orat.
10058{_}1601{_}Nam, ille qui intelligit reficitur quantum ad intellectum et quantum ad affectum; sed mens ejus qui non intelligit est sine fructu refectionis_."
10058{_}1602{_}And (4) our own intellect tells us that the Breviary should be read intelligently and devoutly.
10058{_}1603{_}One of the ends of the Church in imposing the Divine Office as an obligation is, that by honouring the holy mysteries, or the holy memories of the saints, we may raise our hearts and souls to God, as St. Paul wishes us, "May the God of patience and of comfort grant you to be of one mind towards one another according to Jesus Christ, that with one mind and one mouth you may glorify God" (Rom.
10058{_}1604{_}xv.
10058{_}1605{_}5-6), an effect that cannot be produced by the recital of words which are not understood.
10058{_}1606{_}It is almost impossible to avoid very grave distractions and to sustain attention if there be not a good knowledge of the matter and form of the Hours recited.
10058{_}1607{_}It seems irrational that, priests should spend daily more than an hour reading words that they understand not at all, or very imperfectly; and that the beautiful and sublime thought and language of the book of psalms, which are admired by all educated men, should be, to those who read them every day for years, nothing but a tinkling cymbal, _vox et praeterea nihil_.
10058{_}1608{_}This is often the case even with priests who practise piously and methodically mental prayer.
10058{_}1609{_}And yet nowhere are such beautiful acts of faith and confidence in God's power expressed as in the Psalms (e.g., 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 10, 12, 16, 19, 25, 27, 30, 34, 43, 54, 55, 56, etc.
10058{_}1610{_}); no more sublime expressions of praise exist than in the Psalms 8, 9, 17, 18, 20, 21, etc.
10058{_}1611{_}Time spent in studying the history of the Breviary, the structure and the growth of the contents of each Hour, the meanings of the prayers and hymns, is time well spent.
10058{_}1612{_}B.--THE IMMEDIATE PREPARATION FOR THE RECITATION OF THE HOURS.
10058{_}1613{_}First.
10058{_}1614{_}It is necessary to foresee from the reading of the _Ordo_ what is to be said, and to mark all the psalms, lessons, responses, antiphons and prayers.
10058{_}1615{_}By this practice, St. Bonaventure says, all is recited and recited in order.
10058{_}1616{_}_Libri et alia necessaria ad officium praeparantur et legenda studiose ante praevisa, quando et quomodo sint dicenda dicuntur_ (Intit.
10058{_}1617{_}Novit, p. I., c. 4).
10058{_}1618{_}Unless this matter be arranged before the prayer, _Aperi_ is begun, a priest is certain to suffer from distractions, to run the risk of violating the rubrics and to lose some of the spiritual profit which arises from preparation.
10058{_}1619{_}This point of preparation is attended to by all thoughtful priests and it was ever the practice of the great students and lovers of liturgy.
10058{_}1620{_}Second.
10058{_}1621{_}It is necessary to recollect ourselves.
10058{_}1622{_}This is simply to draw off from profane thoughts the mind and the heart, and to apply them to the sublime work of conversing with God, which we do in the Divine Office.
10058{_}1623{_}This recollecting of our wandering thoughts before prayer is impressed on us by Holy Scripture, by the example of the saints, and by our own common sense.
10058{_}1624{_}Holy Scripture warns us "Before prayer prepare thy soul and be not as a man that tempteth God" (Ecclus.
10058{_}1625{_}18.
10058{_}1626{_}23).
10058{_}1627{_}And as typical of the preparation made by saintly priests, the example of St. Charles Borromeo may be mentioned.
10058{_}1628{_}The saint always spent a quarter of an hour in preparatory prayer before beginning the Church's official prayer.
10058{_}1629{_}The Venerable John D'Avila made the same practice general amongst his disciples.
10058{_}1630{_}This holy man narrates, how one day he met a priest of the Society of Jesus, who asked him to recite the Hours with him, and that before beginning their prayer the Jesuit fell on his knees, saying, "There are some who speak of saying the Office as if it were a trifle.
10058{_}1631{_}Come, they say, let us say our Hours together, and so immediately begin.
10058{_}1632{_}This is showing very little appreciation for so holy a duty, for it well merits a few moments at least of recollection" (Bacquez).
10058{_}1633{_}Our own common sense tells us not to rush heedlessly to begin any important work.
10058{_}1634{_}To converse with God is a work of sublime importance which needs preparation, so that it may be done attentively.
10058{_}1635{_}Third.
10058{_}1636{_}We must invoke God's aid by prayer.
10058{_}1637{_}No prayer is more suitable than the prayer given as a preparatory prayer in the Breviary, "_Aperi, Domine, os meum_ ... Open Thou, O Lord, my mouth to bless Thy holy name; cleanse my heart from vain, evil and wandering thoughts; enlighten my understanding, inflame my will, that so I may worthily, attentively and devoutly recite this Office and deserve to be heard in the presence of Thy Divine Majesty.
10058{_}1638{_}Through Christ our Lord.
10058{_}1639{_}Amen.
10058{_}1640{_}O Lord, in union with that divine intention wherewith Thou whilst here on earth didst Thyself praise God, I offer these Hours to Thee."
10058{_}1641{_}Fourth.
10058{_}1642{_}To unite ourselves with Jesus Christ.
10058{_}1643{_}In the prefatory prayer "_Aperi, Domine_," we say "_Domine, in unione_," etc.
10058{_}1644{_}In Baptism, Christians are united to Jesus, to His life, to His spirit.
10058{_}1645{_}He is the Head of the Church and we are its members.
10058{_}1646{_}And this union should be a real, explicit, vivifying union when we fulfil our ministry of social prayer.
10058{_}1647{_}This union with Christ is sought for by Himself, by the Apostles, by the Church, and is practised ever by God's saints.
10058{_}1648{_}The words of the prayer should be reduced to action.
10058{_}1649{_}1.
10058{_}1650{_}Christ our model in all things is our model in prayer, and so He teaches us that when we pray we must say "Our Father, Who art in Heaven," that is, to use His very words and sentiments.
10058{_}1651{_}And this desire of our Lord, that souls should be united to Him in prayer, has often been manifested by Him to His saints.
10058{_}1652{_}To St. Gertrude He said, "My daughter, behold My Heart; look upon It in future as supplying your own defects.
10058{_}1653{_}When you would pray, ask It to help you to give My Father the homage you owe Him.
10058{_}1654{_}I shall be ever ready to second you as soon as you call Me to your aid."
10058{_}1655{_}St. Bernard, schooled in this practice by the Holy Ghost, knew all its sweetness: "David," he says, "rejoiced of old to have found his heart to pray to his Master and his God--_Invenit servus tuus cor tuum ut oraret te oratione hac_ (II.
10058{_}1656{_}Kings viii.
10058{_}1657{_}27).
10058{_}1658{_}And I, that I may pray, have found the heart of my King and my Brother, of my sweet Saviour; shall I not then also pray?
10058{_}1659{_}Yes, certainly, for I am, too, happy, as I have, if not the Heart of Jesus in place of mine, at least have I mine in that of Jesus" (Bacquez, p. 191).
10058{_}1660{_}2.
10058{_}1661{_}St. Paul recommends us to offer our prayers through Jesus Christ.
10058{_}1662{_}"By him, therefore, let us offer the sacrifice of praise always to God, that is to say, the fruit of lips confessing to His name" (Heb.
10058{_}1663{_}xiii.
10058{_}1664{_}15).
10058{_}1665{_}3.
10058{_}1666{_}The Church wishes this union with Christ and mentions it several times in her prayers, _Per Dominum nostrum Jesum Christum_.
10058{_}1667{_}She expresses her wish in the preparatory prayer, _Aperi, Domine_; she wishes the words and sentiments of the psalms to be applied to Jesus, the Saviour, whom David typified, and to whom the psalms in great number relate.
10058{_}1668{_}And in the frequent repetition of the _Pater Noster_, we speak Christ's sentiments and words.
10058{_}1669{_}4.
10058{_}1670{_}The lives of the saints furnish many examples and precepts of this union with Christ in our prayer.
10058{_}1671{_}To the examples of St. Gertrude and St. Bernard many others can be added.
10058{_}1672{_}Several such examples are quoted by Bacquez in his work on the Office.
10058{_}1673{_}5.
10058{_}1674{_}The remembrance of the sublime work of the Office should aid in its fervent recitation.
10058{_}1675{_}Priests should remember the words of St. Alphonsus: "After the sacrifice of the Mass the Church possesses no treasure so great as the Divine Office."
10058{_}1676{_}"It is God's Church, the Spouse of Christ, who has done me the honour of choosing me for this great work--me, in preference to a hundred others.
10058{_}1677{_}She puts into my hand her holy book of heavenly language, and asks me to read its words before God, to unite with the angels and saints in honouring God."
10058{_}1678{_}6.
10058{_}1679{_}To propose some particular intention before the recitation of the Hours begins, and to renew it during the recitation is an excellent means of guarding against distractions and mechanical routine.
10058{_}1680{_}It sustains during the prayer the fervour with which it was begun.
10058{_}1681{_}St. Bonaventure said to priests "Give _great_ attention to the signs (_i.e._, to the directions, about kneeling, standing, sign of cross, etc.
10058{_}1682{_}), _greater_ attention to the words, and the _greatest_ attention to the (particular) intention."
10058{_}1683{_}But what intention ought we to have?
10058{_}1684{_}We should have general intentions and particular intentions.
10058{_}1685{_}We must have the general intentions of the Church, whose ambassadors we are.
10058{_}1686{_}We must pray that God be known and adored, loved and thanked and praised.
10058{_}1687{_}We must pray that the Church have freedom, that she may be exalted, that the kingdom of Christ may spread and flourish, that the Pope and clergy of the world may be blessed and guided by God, that holy souls may be confirmed in virtue and that sinners may be converted.
10058{_}1688{_}We should have also some particular intentions in reading our Hours.
10058{_}1689{_}Thus, we may pray to obtain a more lively faith, a greater hope, a more ardent charity, greater meekness and humility, greater patience, detachment from the world, greater fraternal charity, help in keeping vows--in a word, an increase of virtues, especially those in which we may have great wants.
10058{_}1690{_}Again, a priest may and should beg God to help him and guide him by his light and grace, in doubts, in trouble, in crosses, in his daily work as a priest, in his parish, in his schools, in his college.
10058{_}1691{_}Particularly and fervently should a priest pray for success in his religious instruction in school, in church, in the pulpit.
10058{_}1692{_}For St. Augustine tells us that success in this matter depends more on prayer than on preaching (_De Doc.
10058{_}1693{_}Christ., Lib_.
10058{_}1694{_}4, chap.
10058{_}1695{_}15).
10058{_}1696{_}And at every Hour a priest should pray for a happy death.
10058{_}1697{_}Before saying his Hours, a priest may form a special intention of praying for others, his superiors, his parents, his brothers and sisters, his benefactors, his friends, his enemies, for those who have asked for prayers, for some one in sorrow, for some one in sin, for a soul in purgatory.
10058{_}1698{_}Of course, these prayers benefit the priest who offers them, for as St. Gregory the Great said so well, "_Plus enim pro se valere preces suas efficit qui has et pro aliis impendit_" (Moral II.
10058{_}1699{_}25).
10058{_}1700{_}AIDS DURING THE RECITATION.
10058{_}1701{_}I.
10058{_}1702{_}A suitable place should be selected.
10058{_}1703{_}The Psalmist sang "_In omni loco dominationis ejus, benedic, anima mea, Domino_" (Ps.
10058{_}1704{_}102, 22).
10058{_}1705{_}Our Lord wishes us to pray always; St. Paul says (I. Tim.
10058{_}1706{_}ii.)
10058{_}1707{_}that we should pray in every place, and theologians teach that a priest may validly and licitly say his Hours walking in the fields, in his room, or in any suitable place.
10058{_}1708{_}The most suitable place is the church.
10058{_}1709{_}For it is a house of prayer (St. Matt.
10058{_}1710{_}xxi.
10058{_}1711{_}43), and the Holy Ghost asks us to go there to pray, "_in templo ejus omnes dicent gloriam_" (Ps.
10058{_}1712{_}28, 9).
10058{_}1713{_}The Apostles, going to the temple to pray at the sixth and at the ninth hour, show us how suitable is the place holier than the temple--the church.
10058{_}1714{_}The practice of the saints impresses on us the suitability of the church for the Church's official prayer.
10058{_}1715{_}In the life of every modern saint we find recommended and practised the saying of the Hours at the altar.
10058{_}1716{_}Perhaps, the example which is best known to missionary priests, is the example of the Cure d'Ars, who in the early days of his priestly life always said his Breviary kneeling in the sanctuary.
10058{_}1717{_}His parishioners liked from time to time to slip into the church to watch him.
10058{_}1718{_}"Often," says an eye-witness, "he paused while praying, his looks fixed on the Tabernacle, with eyes in which were painted so lively a faith that one might suppose our Lord was visible to his gaze.
10058{_}1719{_}Later, his church being continually filled with an attentive crowd following his least movements, he took pains to avoid everything that might excite their admiration.
10058{_}1720{_}Yet still, he might be frequently found, after a long day passed in the sacred tribunal, reciting his Hours on his knees, either in the sacristy or in a corner of the choir, a few steps from the altar; so strong was the attraction that drew him to unite his prayer to that of our Lord, so great was the love and respect inspired by the presence and infinite majesty of his Divine Master" (_Life of Cure d'Ars_, by Monnin).
10058{_}1721{_}Every priest must feel that the church benches, or the sanctuary, with their silence, their every part awakening and reminding the soul that this is the house of God, this is the gate of Heaven, are places most suitable for prayer and are great aids to fervent prayer.
10058{_}1722{_}The thought of the presence of Christ with His adoring angels, to whose songs of praise the priest should unite himself, should help wonderfully in the devout recitation of the Hours.
10058{_}1723{_}St. Alphonsus recommends that priests saying the Breviary should say it before a crucifix or before a statue or picture of the Blessed Virgin, so that gazing from time to time on these holy objects may foster or renew pious thoughts.
10058{_}1724{_}II.
10058{_}1725{_}A great aid to pious recitation of the Hours is to take up a respectful position.
10058{_}1726{_}The Office is a prayer, an elevation of the soul to God, and should be treated as such; and as everyone knows, the union of soul and body is such that in vocal prayer both are employed.
10058{_}1727{_}If the body take up a lazy or unbecoming position in prayer, it is an insult to God to Whom prayer is offered, and is a certain source of distraction and faulty prayer.
10058{_}1728{_}Habit does much in this matter, and where a priest labours to correct an inclination to take up a too comfortable position in saying his Hours, he is striving to pray well.
10058{_}1729{_}Priests, young and old, say writers on this point, should be vigilant in this aid to fervent prayer.
10058{_}1730{_}The well-known words of St. Teresa recommending a comfortable attitude in prayer do not clash with this doctrine.
10058{_}1731{_}In the _Selva_, St. Alphonsus writes: "It is related that while two religious recited Matins a devil appeared, caused an intolerable stench, and through mockery said, 'To the prayer which you offer such incense is suited'--_ad talem orationem tale debetur incensum_."
10058{_}1732{_}Which attitude is the best?
10058{_}1733{_}Seeing the examples of the saints, St. Charles Borromeo, St. Vincent de Paul, St. Francis de Sales, St. John de la Salle, the Cure d'Ars, and of many other saintly men, the best attitude in reciting the Hours is kneeling.
10058{_}1734{_}Other saints accustomed themselves to recite their Hours standing, with head uncovered.
10058{_}1735{_}Others followed, in private recitation, all the positions--sitting, kneeling, standing--required in choir.
10058{_}1736{_}The practice is said to aid in banishing distractions, and contributes greatly to attention and devotion.
10058{_}1737{_}Of course, in private recitation no one is bound to any of these practices.
10058{_}1738{_}But they have proved useful to many in practising devout prayer.
10058{_}1739{_}Everyone is bound to pray with fervour, and a respectful attitude is a big help towards that end.
10058{_}1740{_}Slow, deliberate pronunciation is another aid to the fervent saying of the Hours of the Breviary.
10058{_}1741{_}The lives of saintly men show their practice in this matter.
10058{_}1742{_}Knowing that they were the ambassadors of the Church in presenting her praise, thanks and wants to God, they read with care and attention.
10058{_}1743{_}From their slow and deliberate reading of the holy words, their souls drew out the sublime thoughts and sentiments which their lips expressed.
10058{_}1744{_}In rapid reading, the mind and heart have not time to think well on the meaning of the words and of the sentiments, and hence, no holy thoughts fill the soul, no acts of virtue are elicited, no prayer of petition is offered, no holy resolutions are formed.
10058{_}1745{_}Indeed, very often--to quote the words of a venerable author--priests seem to say with their lips and to express by their rapid reading, not _Deus in adjutorium meum intende_, O God, make haste to help me!
10058{_}1746{_}but _Domine ad festinandum me adjuva_--"O God, help me to hasten?"
10058{_}1747{_}Wise old Rodriguez advises readers of spiritual books to observe a hen drinking and to imitate her slow and deliberate sipping, by reading in small quantities, with pauses.
10058{_}1748{_}Sometimes priests acquire the habit of hurried reading, quite unconsciously, and afterwards labour hard, and in vain, too, to correct it.
10058{_}1749{_}It is important for beginners in the Breviary to go at a slow pace, as the trot and the gallop are fatal to good and pious recitation.
10058{_}1750{_}Sometimes priests excuse this hurried reading, as they wish to save time!
10058{_}1751{_}Why do priests wish to save time?
10058{_}1752{_}"For study," some may say; but the obligation of the Divine Office precedes all obligations of study, and its devout recitation is of far greater importance to the priest and to the Church than is any other or every other study.
10058{_}1753{_}Some priests gallop through the Hours, to gain time for other ministerial work, they say.
10058{_}1754{_}But they forget that the primary work--after the celebration of Mass--and the _most important work_ of a priest, is the great official prayer of the Church.
10058{_}1755{_}Who amongst priests leads the life of ceaseless toil which the Cure d'Ars led?
10058{_}1756{_}And we have read how he said his Hours.
10058{_}1757{_}St. Francis Xavier found time to preach to his many neophytes, to teach them, to baptize them, and yet he did not use the permission given him to shorten his Breviary prayer.
10058{_}1758{_}He read the whole Office daily and added to it prayers to obtain the grace of better attention and devotion.
10058{_}1759{_}Sometimes the reading of the Hours is hurried for a motive less praiseworthy than the motives of study or of priestly work.
10058{_}1760{_}_Producitur somnus, producitur mensa, produncuntur confabulationes, lusus, nugae nugarum; solius supremae Magestratis, cultus summa qua potest celeritate deproperatur_ (Kugler, _De Spiritu Eccles_.
10058{_}1761{_}), "On this, God complained one day to St. Bridget, saying that some priests lose so much time every day in conversing with friends on worldly affairs; and afterwards, in conversing with Him, while they recite the Office, they are so hurried that they dishonour Him more than they glorify Him" (St. Alphonsus, _Selva_).
10058{_}1762{_}In the hurried reading of the Office, time, a few minutes perhaps, is gained, but what is lost?
10058{_}1763{_}Does the loss of all the lights and graces and blessings of the Office compensate for the time gained?
10058{_}1764{_}It is important that all who read the Breviary hurriedly, or who may be tempted to acquire the habit, should weigh well the words read therein (Friday's Vespers) "_Labor labiorum ipsorum operiet eos; cadent super eos carbones_" (Ps.
10058{_}1765{_}139).
10058{_}1766{_}"The labour of their lips shall overwhelm them; burning coals shall fall upon them."
10058{_}1767{_}To acquire this important habit, the practice of reading at a slow pace the words of the Breviary, authors suggest several little hints.
10058{_}1768{_}One is, never to start reading the Hours unless there be _ample_ time for finishing the Hour or Hours intended to be then and there read.
10058{_}1769{_}The practice of squeezing the small Hours into scraps of time (e.g., in the intervals between hearing confessions in the confessional, at a session) is fatal to careful and pious reading.
10058{_}1770{_}Another hint is, to read everything, every word (_e.g., Pater Noster, Ave, Credo_), and to repeat nothing from memory, because the printed words meeting the eyes and the spoken words reaching the ears help to fix the attention and there is less risk of their passing unnoticed.
10058{_}1771{_}This was the practice of St. Charles Borromeo.
10058{_}1772{_}St. Philip Neri never recited from memory even in saying the small Hours.
10058{_}1773{_}St. Vincent de Paul always spent a great time in saying his Breviary.
10058{_}1774{_}His intense fervour was helped by his careful reading of every word, and this practice of keeping his eyes fixed steadily on the printed matter of the book he recommended to his congregation of priests.
10058{_}1775{_}Some holy priests maintained that they could recite from memory with greater fervour than from the reading of the pages of the Breviary; but the practice is not one for the many.
10058{_}1776{_}Another hint to help pious recitation is to _earnestly wish_ to say the Office worthily, attentively and devoutly.
10058{_}1777{_}This wish must bring up before the mind the thought of how displeasing to God and how great is the daily loss--not to speak of a lifetime's loss-to the soul of a priest who prays carelessly, tepidly and mechanically.
10058{_}1778{_}But in spite of all precautions, it may be noticed during the recitation of the Hours that, without our own fault, the words are said too quickly.
10058{_}1779{_}It is advised, then, to pause and to say mentally what the Venerable Boudon was wont to say to his soul in similar circumstances: "To punish and mortify thee, I will go more slowly; I will devote to my office to-day a longer time" (Bacquez).
10058{_}1780{_}IV.
10058{_}1781{_}To prevent distractions and to banish them are no easy matters.
10058{_}1782{_}It is impossible to avoid all distractions.
10058{_}1783{_}Involuntary distractions do not hinder merit; still it is important that an effort be made to diminish and repress the quality of such disturbing elements in prayer.
10058{_}1784{_}First of all, we can never totally avoid all distractions, nor can we entirely and completely remove them when they enter our souls.
10058{_}1785{_}The human soul cannot pray for any notable time without distraction.
10058{_}1786{_}The greatest saints knew this well.
10058{_}1787{_}St. Augustine wrote, "_Vult se tenere ut stet, et quodammodo fugit a se nec invenit cancellos quibus se includat_" (in Psalm 95).
10058{_}1788{_}St. Thomas wrote "_Vix unum Pater noster potest homo dicere quin mens ad alia fertur_."
10058{_}1789{_}The author of the _Imitation of Christ_ wrote, "For I confess truly that I am accustomed to be very much distracted.
10058{_}1790{_}For oftentimes I am not there where I am bodily standing or sitting, but am rather there where my thoughts carry me" (Bk.
10058{_}1791{_}iii.
10058{_}1792{_}c. 48).
10058{_}1793{_}The same writer wrote, "And I, a wretch and the vilest of men....
10058{_}1794{_}I can hardly spend one half hour as I ought."
10058{_}1795{_}St. Teresa wrote, "I am not less distracted than you are during Office, and try to think that it arises from weakness of head.
10058{_}1796{_}Do not fear to think so, too.
10058{_}1797{_}Does not our Lord know, that when we perform this duty we would wish to do it with the greatest possible attention?"
10058{_}1798{_}After reading these words we can understand how prayer offered up with involuntary distractions is true, holy prayer.
10058{_}1799{_}St. Thomas tells us "_Dicendum quod in spiritu et veritate orat, qui ex instinctu spiritus ad orandum accedit, etiamsi ex aliqua infirmitate mens postmodum evagetur.... Evagatio vero mentis quae fit praeter propositum orationis fructum non tollat_" (2.2. q.
10058{_}1800{_}83, a.
10058{_}1801{_}13).
10058{_}1802{_}Nevertheless, every effort should be made to avoid and to banish distractions.
10058{_}1803{_}The ways of doing this are given in all treatises on prayer.
10058{_}1804{_}Every priest knows them well.
10058{_}1805{_}There are negative means and positive means.
10058{_}1806{_}The negative means consist in withdrawing the senses and the powers of the soul from everything disturbing the soul's converse with God; in guarding against any too absorbing interest in worldly affairs, so that the mind is unmanageable and cannot be fixed on sacred things.
10058{_}1807{_}St. Francis of Assisi, working at a piece of furniture before saying Terce, was, during the saying of that hour disturbed by the thought of his manual work.
10058{_}1808{_}When he re-entered his cell he took the bit of work and threw it in the fire saying, "I wish to sacrifice to the Lord the thing which hindered my prayer to Him."
10058{_}1809{_}The positive means of avoiding and of banishing distractions are given above; they are to read slowly, to read every word, to read in a becoming position, to observe choir directions, to give ample time to each Hour.
10058{_}1810{_}Another rule given by writers on the pious recitation of the Office, is to pause at certain places in the psalms to renew attention and elicit affections.
10058{_}1811{_}Some authors recommend such pauses at the end of the invitatory, at the end of each hymn, or after each _Gloria_.
10058{_}1812{_}"Study well the _Gloria Patri_," said St. Francis of Assisi, "for in it you find the substance of the scriptures."
10058{_}1813{_}V. To apply the mind to what is read is another help to pious recitation.
10058{_}1814{_}It seems to be a useless repetition of an obvious fact that to apply the mind to the prayers read, helps to ward off and to drive away distractions.
10058{_}1815{_}Such a practice is natural for a person of intelligence, and the Church wishes and expects such intelligent and heartfelt prayer.
10058{_}1816{_}God said to the Jewish priests what applies to the Christian priesthood, too: "And now, O ye priests, this commandment is to you, if you will not hear, if you will not lay it to heart to give glory to My name, saith the Lord of Hosts, I will curse your blessings, because you have not laid it to heart" (Mal.
10058{_}1817{_}ii.
10058{_}1818{_}1-2).
10058{_}1819{_}Christ complained about the Jewish people who honoured Him with their lips, but had their hearts far from Him.
10058{_}1820{_}And God's great servants realized this fully.
10058{_}1821{_}St. Paul said, "And he that speaketh by a tongue (the gift of speaking strange tongues) let him pray that he may interpret.
10058{_}1822{_}For if I pray in a tongue my spirit prayeth, _but my understanding is without fruit_.
10058{_}1823{_}What is it then?
10058{_}1824{_}I will pray with the spirit.
10058{_}1825{_}I will pray also with the understanding.
10058{_}1826{_}I will sing with the spirit, I will sing also with the understanding" (I. Cor.
10058{_}1827{_}xiv.
10058{_}1828{_}13-15).
10058{_}1829{_}St. Gregory the Great said that true prayer consists not only in the articulation of the words, but also in the attention of the heart; for to obtain the divine graces our good desires have greater efficacy than mere words (_Moral, lib_.
10058{_}1830{_}22.
10058{_}1831{_}_cap_.
10058{_}1832{_}13).
10058{_}1833{_}Peter de Blois wrote of the priests of his time, "_Labia sunt in canticis et animus in patinis_!
10058{_}1834{_}Their lips are in the psalms, but their heart is in the dishes!"
10058{_}1835{_}(_Selva_).
10058{_}1836{_}"_Age quod agis_," says the _Imitation of Christ_.
10058{_}1837{_}VI.
10058{_}1838{_}It is advisable not to dwell on the literary excellence of the Breviary during the recitation of the Office.
10058{_}1839{_}It is a useful thing that priests should recognise the authorship of the psalms recited, their probable dates, the circumstances of their composition, the sublimity of their thought, the peculiarity of their Hebrew style, the rhythm and poetry of the Hebrews.
10058{_}1840{_}But the _dwelling_ on these thoughts leads to distractions.
10058{_}1841{_}Again, some priests, like the clerics of the Renaissance and post-Renaissance times, despise and dislike the Breviary for its alleged barbarous style.
10058{_}1842{_}These unworthy and foolish sentiments are met with, very rarely.
10058{_}1843{_}They are opposed to the priestly spirit, which should love and respect the Scripture extracts, God's inspired words.
10058{_}1844{_}The homilies from the Fathers are well chosen, and suitable for the greatest prayer and for the greatest prayerbook the world has ever known.
10058{_}1845{_}The hymns are the wonder and study of scholars of every religion.
10058{_}1846{_}St. Augustine, after his conversion even, felt a repugnance for the holy Scriptures as unequal to Cicero in form.
10058{_}1847{_}But in his mature age and considered judgment, the saint reversed his judgment; "_non habent_," he wrote of the Pagan classics, "_illae paginae vultum pietatis, lacrymas confessionis spiritum contribulatum cor contritum et humiliatum_" (Confess.
10058{_}1848{_}Bk.
10058{_}1849{_}7, c. 21).
10058{_}1850{_}VII.
10058{_}1851{_}To think of Christ's Passion is another aid to good Breviary recitation.
10058{_}1852{_}We have seen in the theological part of this book (page 4) the seven principal stages of the Passion which correspond with the seven principal parts of the Office.
10058{_}1853{_}And this devout thought on some scene of the Passion is recommended by all writers on the Divine Office, as an easy and very profitable means and aid to attentive and devout saying of the Hours.
10058{_}1854{_}It is a means practised by thousands of priests.
10058{_}1855{_}St. Bonaventure recommended that at each Hour some thought of the mysteries of the life and death of Christ should be held in mind.
10058{_}1856{_}Thus, Matins, the night Office, might be offered up in honour of the birth and infancy of Christ; Lauds, in honour of His resurrection; Terce, in honour of the coming of the Holy Ghost; None, in memory of Christ's death; Vespers, in thanksgiving for the Eucharist.
10058{_}1857{_}VIII.
10058{_}1858{_}To remember the presence of God, of our angel guardian, and of the demons, is a practice recommended by writers on recitation of the Office in or out of choir.
10058{_}1859{_}This thought of the presence of God was one of the aids recommended by St. Benedict to his religious, to aid their devout fulfilment of the great work of reciting their Hours worthily, attentively, and devoutly.
10058{_}1860{_}Centuries after St. Benedict's death we find St. Bonaventure repeating this advice to his novices.
10058{_}1861{_}Blessed Peter Faber, S.J., to make his Breviary prayer more fervent, used to picture to himself the presence of his guardian angel at his side recording his pious and holy thoughts, and the demon recording his distractions.
10058{_}1862{_}"Dearly beloved priest," wrote St. Alphonsus, "when you take the Breviary in your hand, imagine that an angel stands on one side to register your merits in the Book of Life if you say the Office with devotion, and on the other a devil who, if you recite it with distraction, writes your faults in the book of death.
10058{_}1863{_}With this thought excite yourself to say the Office with the greatest possible devotion.
10058{_}1864{_}Endeavour, then, not only at the beginning of the Office, but also at the beginning of each psalm, to renew your attention, that you may be able to excite in your heart all the sentiments that you shall read" (_Selva_).
10058{_}1865{_}ARTICLE IV.--AFTER SAYING THE DIVINE OFFICE.
10058{_}1866{_}1.
10058{_}1867{_}Give God thanks for His goodness in permitting us to join in the great work, for hearing our prayer, and for His helps and graces during its duration.
10058{_}1868{_}2.
10058{_}1869{_}Ask God's pardon for faults committed in the course of this prayer of His Church.
10058{_}1870{_}3.
10058{_}1871{_}Devoutly recite the "_Sacro-sanctae et Individuae Trinitati_ ... Amen.
10058{_}1872{_}V. _Beata viscera_....R. _Et beata ubera_...." This prayer, which is generally printed in Breviaries immediately before the Psalter, is to be said kneeling, where this is physically possible.
10058{_}1873{_}This is necessary in order to gain the indulgence granted by Pope Pius X. to all persons obliged to recite the Divine Office.
10058{_}1874{_}It is not of obligation and its omission is not sinful.
10058{_}1875{_}It forms no part of the obligatory Office.
10058{_}1876{_}"It must be said kneeling, but at the request of Cardinal Asquini, Prefect of the Congregation of Indulgences, Pope Pius IX.
10058{_}1877{_}was pleased to make one exception (July 12, 1865) in favour of persons who were not able to say it kneeling--_infirmitatis tantum causa_.
10058{_}1878{_}Hence, travellers or persons on a journey are not exempted, for they can say it kneeling at the end of the journey.
10058{_}1879{_}It is sufficient to say the '_Sacro-sanctae_' once only, that is, at the end of Compline, with the intention of obtaining pardon of all the defects a person may have been guilty of in saying the entire Office.
10058{_}1880{_}Yet it may be repeated after each Hour, e.g., after Matins, and Lauds, after the small Hours and after Compline; in each case one would thereby get forgiveness for the faults committed during the part of the Office recited.
10058{_}1881{_}This explanation has been given by the Holy Father (Pius IX.)
10058{_}1882{_}himself.
10058{_}1883{_}The usage amongst the chapters at Rome, as at St. Peter's, St. Mary's, etc., is to recite it every time they leave the choir" (Maurel, S.J., _Le chretien e claire sur la nature et l'usage des Indulgences_).
10058{_}1884{_}The beauty and sublimity of this prayer is not always appreciated.
10058{_}1885{_}Its translation here may inspire fresh thoughts of fervour.
10058{_}1886{_}"To the most holy and undivided Trinity, to the humanity of our Lord Jesus Christ crucified, to the fruitful virginity of the most glorious Mary ever a Virgin, and to the company of all the saints, be given by every creature eternal praise, honour, power and glory, and to us the remission of all our sins.
10058{_}1887{_}Amen.
10058{_}1888{_}Blessed be the womb of the Virgin Mary, which bore the Son of the Eternal Father.
10058{_}1889{_}And blessed be the breasts which gave suck to Christ, our Lord."
10058{_}1890{_}In connection with this prayer an interesting question is discussed in the _Irish Ecclesiastical Record_ (No.
10058{_}1891{_}540.
10058{_}1892{_}December, 1912).
10058{_}1893{_}Is this prayer merely a sacramental?
10058{_}1894{_}Has it an indulgence attached to it at all?
10058{_}1895{_}The querist quotes _The new Raccolta_, in answering the second part of his query but wishes to know if it be an indulgence how it produces its effects.
10058{_}1896{_}"For either the defects committed in reading the Divine Office are voluntary or involuntary.
10058{_}1897{_}If voluntary they are sins and consequently cannot be touched by an indulgence; if involuntary they are not sinful and therefore stand in no need of an indulgence."
10058{_}1898{_}In a very long reply Dr. John M. Harty sums up, "For our part we adhere to the view which says that the efficacy of the privilege annexed by Leo X. and Pius X. to the _Sacro-sanctae_ is derived from an indulgence.
10058{_}1899{_}At the same time we think that these prayers are also sacramentals, since they are official prayers of the Church.
10058{_}1900{_}Under this aspect, they obtain the ordinary benefits which are attached to sacramentals, and, accordingly lead to a remission of sin and temporal punishment by means of sorrow and satisfaction, which are elicited under the influence of the abundant graces given by God, through the intercession of the Church.
10058{_}1901{_}They also placate God, so as to render Him willing to grant His favours even though defects exist in the recitation of the Office....
10058{_}1902{_}Though these defects are not produced _ex opere operato_, they nevertheless are real, and are an encouragement to priests, whose human frailty prevents the perfect performance even of the most sacred functions of their priestly office."
10058{_}1903{_}PART III  THE CANONICAL HOURS.
10058{_}1904{_}CHAPTER I.  MATINS.
10058{_}1905{_}_Etymology_.
10058{_}1906{_}The word _Matins_ is derived from _Matuta_, the Latin name for the Greek goddess of morning.
10058{_}1907{_}The word used in the Roman Breviary is _matutinum (i.e., tempus)_.
10058{_}1908{_}It is the old name for Lauds, _Laudes matutinae_.
10058{_}1909{_}The word was also used to denote the office of Vigils.
10058{_}1910{_}Hence, the word was used in three senses, to denote the nocturns and lauds, to denote Lauds only and to denote the vigil office.
10058{_}1911{_}In liturgical study the word was confusing, and sometimes it is the context only which gives the author's meaning.
10058{_}1912{_}This, the principal Hour of the Church's public prayer, was, in the early days of Christianity, said at night, and was called _Nocturnum_ and _Vigiliae_.
10058{_}1913{_}_Origin_.
10058{_}1914{_}The night office of vigils dates from the very earliest days of Christianity.
10058{_}1915{_}It derived its name from the vigils or night watches of the soldiers, who divided the night, from six o'clock in the evening to six o'clock in the morning, into four watches of three hours each.
10058{_}1916{_}The nightly meetings of the Christians came to be called by the name _vigils_, but the meetings were not begun at the stated hours of military vigil and did not finish with them.
10058{_}1917{_}Why these meetings of Christians were held at night, and in what their religious exercises consisted in, both in matter and form, is an unsolved problem.
10058{_}1918{_}But it is certain that they resembled the services of the Jewish synagogue in the readings from Scripture, psalm-singing and prayers, and differed from those services by having readings from the Gospels, the Epistles, and from non-canonical books, such as the Epistle of St. Clement.
10058{_}1919{_}The Eucharistic service always formed part of them.
10058{_}1920{_}Indeed, the very name, Synagogue was given to these assemblies of Christians, as we see from the Pastor of Hermes.
10058{_}1921{_}In their common prayer, they faced towards the East, as the Jews did towards Jerusalem.
10058{_}1922{_}They had precentors and janitors as in the Jewish rites.
10058{_}1923{_}Their services consisted of the readings from the Mosaic law, from Gospels and Epistles, exposition of Scripture, a set sermon, long and fervent "blessings" or thanksgiving and psalms.
10058{_}1924{_}Before there were any written gospels to read, we gather that the reading of the Old Law, of the Prophets and the Psalms, was followed by a set sermon on the life and death of Christ (Bickel, _Messe und Pascha,_ p, 91).
10058{_}1925{_}From St.
10058{_}1926{_}Basil (fourth century) it is concluded that two choirs sang the Psalms.
10058{_}1927{_}Cassian writes that the monks of the fifth century celebrated the Night Office with twelve psalms and readings from the Old and the New Testaments.
10058{_}1928{_}Hence, "we find the same elements repeated, the psalms generally chanted in the form of responses, that is to say, by one or more cantors, the choir repeating one verse which served as a response, alternately with the verses of the psalms, which were sung by the cantors, readings taken from the Old and the New Testaments and, later on, from the works of the Fathers and Doctors; litanies, supplications, prayers for divers members of the Church, clergy, faithful, neophytes and catechumens; for emperors, travellers; the sick; and generally for all the necessities of the Church, and even for Jews and for heretics.
10058{_}1929{_}It is quite easy to find these essentials in our modern Matins" (Dom Cabrol, _Cath.
10058{_}1930{_}Encyclopedia_, art.
10058{_}1931{_}"Matins").
10058{_}1932{_}Matins on account of its length and position in the Breviary is the most important part of the daily Office.
10058{_}1933{_}And, on account of the variety and beauty of its elements, is considered the most remarkable.
10058{_}1934{_}The prayer _Pater Noster_ begins the Office.
10058{_}1935{_}It is the Lord's prayer, _divina institutions formata_, when Christ told His Apostles "_Sic vos orabitis_" (St. Matt.
10058{_}1936{_}vi.
10058{_}1937{_}9).
10058{_}1938{_}It is the most excellent of all prayers, being most excellent in its author, its form, its depth of meaning, its effects.
10058{_}1939{_}The prayer consists of a preface, "Our Father, Who art in heaven, hallowed be Thy name, Thy Kingdom come, Thy will be done on earth as it is in heaven."
10058{_}1940{_}And in the body of the prayer are seven petitions--three for the honour and glory of God, in and by ourselves, and four for our own wants, spiritual and temporal.
10058{_}1941{_}Very excellent matter on the greatest of prayers is to be found in the _Catechism of the Council of Trent_ (translation, Duffy, Dublin) and in _A Lapide_ (St. Matt.
10058{_}1942{_}vi.).
10058{_}1943{_}Writers on liturgy say that the recitation of the _Pater Noster_ as the opening prayer of Matins was _not obligatory_ until the beginning of the twelfth century.
10058{_}1944{_}It is said that the monks were wont to say a _Pater Noster_ at each altar in the church before entering their stalls for Office recitation.
10058{_}1945{_}This practice delayed the beginning of the Office in choir, and a rule was made that those who wished to say this prayer must say it in their stalls, in a low tone.
10058{_}1946{_}Of course, in the Breviary of Pius V. (1568) this practice became obligatory on each person bound to read the Hours.
10058{_}1947{_}_Ave Maria_.
10058{_}1948{_}This is a leading prayer amongst the great prayers of the Mass and the Office.
10058{_}1949{_}It, too, is excellent in its authors, its form (clear, short devotional), in motive (in honouring Mary, Mother of God, and in begging her intercession).
10058{_}1950{_}It is divided into three parts, the words of the angel, of St. Elizabeth and of the Church, Devout thoughts on this prayer have been penned by countless clients of Mary in every age.
10058{_}1951{_}Priests are familiar with many such writings, great and small, but _A Lapide_ (St. Luke I.)
10058{_}1952{_}bears reading and re-reading.
10058{_}1953{_}The prayer, as it stands in the Breviary to-day, is not of very ancient date.
10058{_}1954{_}"In point of fact there is little or no trace of the Hail Mary as an accepted devotional formula before 1050.... To understand the developments of the devotion, it is important to grasp the fact that the _Ave Maria_ was merely a form of greeting.
10058{_}1955{_}It was, therefore, long customary to accompany the words with some external gesture of homage, a genuflexion, or at least an inclination of the head....
10058{_}1956{_}In the time of St. Louis the _Ave Maria_ ended with the words _benedictus fructus ventris tui_: it has since been extended by the introduction both of the Holy Name and of a clause of petition....
