We meet the _Ave_ as we know it now, printed in the Breviary of the Camaldolese monks and in that of the Order de Mercede C. 1514.
10058{_}1957{_}...
10058{_}1958{_}The official recognition of the _Ave Maria_ in its complete form, though foreshadowed in the Catechism of the Council of Trent, was finally given in the Roman Breviary of 1568" (Father Thurston, S.J., _Cath.
10058{_}1959{_}Encyclopedia_, art.
10058{_}1960{_}"Hail Mary.")
10058{_}1961{_}_Credo_.
10058{_}1962{_}The Apostles' Creed is placed at the beginning of Matins, because Matins is the beginning of the whole Office, and faith is the beginning, the _principium_ of every supernatural work.
10058{_}1963{_}St. Paul teaches us that it is necessary for us to stir up our faith when we approach God, "For he that cometh to God must believe that He is."
10058{_}1964{_}In reciting the Creed we should think of the sublime truths of our faith, and our hearts should feel, what our lips say, "For with the heart we believe unto justice; but with the mouth confession is made unto salvation" (Rom.
10058{_}1965{_}x.
10058{_}1966{_}10).
10058{_}1967{_}We should remember too, that this formula of faith comes to us from Apostolic times and that it has been repeated millions of times by saints and martyrs; their sentiments of belief, of confidence in God and love of God should be ours.
10058{_}1968{_}_Domine labia mea aperies_.
10058{_}1969{_}The practice of this beautiful invocation dates from the time of St. Benedict (480-553).
10058{_}1970{_}In his Office it stood after the words _Deus in adjutorium_.
10058{_}1971{_}These words _Domine labia mea aperies_, taken from the Psalm _Miserere_, remind us of God purifying the lips of Isaias His prophet with a burning coal, of how God opened the lips of Zachary to bless God and to prophesy.
10058{_}1972{_}"And immediately his mouth was opened and his tongue loosed, and he spoke blessing God" (St. Luke, i.
10058{_}1973{_}64).
10058{_}1974{_}Very appropriately, does the priest reciting the Divine Office ask God to open his lips, to fortify his conscience, to touch his heart.
10058{_}1975{_}_Deus in adjutorium_.
10058{_}1976{_}These words, the opening words of Psalm 69, were always and everywhere used by the monks of old, says Cassian, who called this short prayer the formula of piety, the continual prayer.
10058{_}1977{_}The Church repeats it often in her Office.
10058{_}1978{_}St. John Climacus says it is the great cry of petition for help to triumph over our invisible enemy, who wishes to distract us and to mar our prayer.
10058{_}1979{_}It should be said with humility and with confidence in God.
10058{_}1980{_}In repeating these holy words we make the sign of the Cross; for, all grace comes from the sacrifice of the Cross; and besides, it is a holy and an ancient practice to begin all good works with the sacred sign.
10058{_}1981{_}_Gloria Patri_.
10058{_}1982{_}This little prayer indicates the purpose and end of the recitation of the Office, the glory of the Holy Trinity.
10058{_}1983{_}"Bring to the Lord glory and honour; bring to the Lord glory to His name" (Psalm 28).
10058{_}1984{_}The many repetitions of this formula in the Church liturgy shows the great honour which she pays to it, and the trust she places in its efficacy.
10058{_}1985{_}It was especially loved by St. Francis of Assisi, who said that it contained all wisdom.
10058{_}1986{_}This form of doxology, "Glory be to the Father, to the Son, and to the Holy Ghost," was adopted to repel Arianism, by giving to the faithful a compact theological formula by which they could end every dispute.
10058{_}1987{_}Some authors quote St. Ephrem (circa 363) as the originator of this much-used prayer.
10058{_}1988{_}The form would seem to be of Syrian origin, translated into Greek and later into Latin (Dom Cambrol, _Dictionnaire d' Archeologie Chretienne_, I., 2282, _et seq.,_ word Antienne, Liturgie; _Month_, May, 1910).
10058{_}1989{_}_Invitatory_.
10058{_}1990{_}_Venite Adoremus_....
10058{_}1991{_}The cry of the Church calling on all to adore and praise God, Who has done all for us, Who is the Great Shepherd, and we, the sheep of His fold, should not harden our hearts as did the ungrateful Jews.
10058{_}1992{_}We should pray for all, Catholics, infidels and sinners.
10058{_}1993{_}"A message from the saints.
10058{_}1994{_}Let us imagine, like St. Stephen at his martyrdom, we are privileged to see the heavens opened, and before our eyes the City of God, with its twelve gates all of pearl, and its streets of pure gold, as it were transparent glass, is laid bare, and that we see the angels in their legions, and the redeemed of the Lord around the throne of God.
10058{_}1995{_}Thousands of thousands are ministering to Him," as St. John tells us, "and ten thousand times a hundred thousand stand before Him," and we hear the voice of God, as the noise of many waters in company with that great multitude which no man can number, out of every tribe and nation, clothed in white robes, with palms in their hands, coming into Sion with praise, with everlasting joy upon their heads, for from their eyes God has wiped away all tears, and sorrow and mourning have fled away.
10058{_}1996{_}"There are the white-robed army of Martyrs, holy Confessors, too, men of renown in their generation, and Virgins, the Spouses of Christ: there are those who have come through great tribulation, who once, perchance, were far from God, but have washed their robes in the blood of the Lamb and are now numbered among the people of God, sitting in the beauty of peace and in the tabernacle of confidence and in wealthy rest.
10058{_}1997{_}Let us bring them all before us in vision.
10058{_}1998{_}They have overcome the beast and are standing by the sea of glass, having the harps of God; the Prince of Pastors has appeared to them and they have received a never-failing crown of glory and by the Lamb of God they have been led to fountains of the waters of life."
10058{_}1999{_}Let us listen as they sing their canticle to God, "Holy, Holy, Holy, Lord God of Hosts, who is and who was and who is to come"; let us listen as they sing to us, for we are fellow citizens with them, and where they are we also must be if we remain faithful to the end.
10058{_}2000{_}What do they sing, "O come let us praise the Lord with joy; let us joyfully sing to God, our Saviour" (_Sing ye to the Lord_, pp.
10058{_}2001{_}94-95--Rev.
10058{_}2002{_}R. Eaton).
10058{_}2003{_}The authorship of this psalm--which is said daily in Matins--is attributed to David in the Septuagint and Vulgate.
10058{_}2004{_}Its Latin form in the invitatory differs slightly from the Vulgate text.
10058{_}2005{_}The Breviary retains here the text of St. Jerome's revision and the Vulgate contains the second and more correct revision.
10058{_}2006{_}_Hymns_.
10058{_}2007{_}The hymn is an answer to the invitation given to us in the invitatory, to praise God and to rejoice with Him.
10058{_}2008{_}It is a song of joy and praise.
10058{_}2009{_}Hymns were introduced into the Divine Office in the Eastern Church before the time of St. Ambrose (340-397).
10058{_}2010{_}To combat the Arians, who spread their errors by verse set to popular airs, St. Ambrose, it is said, introduced public liturgical hymn-singing in his church in Milan, and his example was followed gradually through the Western Church.
10058{_}2011{_}(See Note A, _infra_.)
10058{_}2012{_}The final stanza of a Breviary hymn is called the doxology ([Greek: doxa] praise, [Greek: logos] speech), a speaking of praise.
10058{_}2013{_}Hymns which have the final stanza proper, the _Ave Maris stella_, Lauds hymn of the Blessed Sacrament, Matins hymn for several Martyrs, the first Vesper hymn of the Office of Holy Cross, and the Vesper hymns of St. Venantius and St. John Cantius, never change the wording of the stanza.
10058{_}2014{_}But, _where the metre of the hymn_ admits such a change as possible in the last stanza.
10058{_}2015{_}(a) From Christmas to Epiphany _Jesu tibi sit gloria, Qui natus es de Virgine_ is inserted in all hymns, even on saints' offices.
10058{_}2016{_}(b) From Epiphany till end of its octave, _Jesu tibi sit gloria, Qui apparuisti gentibus_.
10058{_}2017{_}(c) From Low Sunday till Ascension Thursday, on Pentecost Sunday and its octave, all hymns end in _Deo Patri sit gloria, Et Filio qui a mortuis_.
10058{_}2018{_}This is the ending for all hymns of saints' feasts in Paschal times, excepting those hymns mentioned above.
10058{_}2019{_}(d) From Ascension to Pentecost (except in the hymn _Salutis humanae Sator_) the doxology is _Jesu tibi sit gloria, Qui victor in coelum redis_.
10058{_}2020{_}(e) Feast of Transfiguration has _Jesu, tibi sit gloria, Qui te revelas parvulis_.
10058{_}2021{_}In all other hymns the doxology is read as it is printed in the Breviary.
10058{_}2022{_}_Antiphons_.
10058{_}2023{_}Antiphon, coming from Greek words meaning a re-echoing of the sound, is a chant performed alternately by two choirs, and was used in pagan drama, long before the Christian era.
10058{_}2024{_}At what date it was introduced into Church liturgy it is difficult to determine.
10058{_}2025{_}Some say it was introduced by St. Ignatius, second Bishop of Antioch.
10058{_}2026{_}It is certain that it was used by bishops and priests to attract, retain and teach the faithful during the Arian heresy.
10058{_}2027{_}In church music, the lector ceased to recite the psalm as a solo and the faithful divided into two choirs, united in the refrain _Gloria Patri_.
10058{_}2028{_}With us, the antiphon generally is a verse or verses from Scripture, recited before and after each psalm.
10058{_}2029{_}"The verse which serves as the antiphon text contains the fundamental thought of the psalm to which it is sung and indicates the point of view from which it is to be understood.
10058{_}2030{_}In other words, it gives the key to the liturgical and mystical meaning of the psalm, with regard to the feast on which it occurs" (_Cath.
10058{_}2031{_}Encycl._, art.
10058{_}2032{_}"Antiphon").
10058{_}2033{_}_Psalms._ In the Breviary, before the recent reform, twelve psalms were recited in the first nocturn of Sundays and on ferias.
10058{_}2034{_}This recitation of twelve psalms was, Cassian tells us, caused by the apparition of an angel, who appeared to the monks and sang at one session twelve psalms, terminating with _Alleluia_.
10058{_}2035{_}The event was mentioned at the Council of Tours, In the new reform, nine psalms are recited at Matins; they should, the old writers on liturgy tell us, remind us of the nine choirs of angels who without ceasing sing God's praise.
10058{_}2036{_}In the new Psalter, the Psalms have been divided into two large divisions, Psalms I.--CVIII.
10058{_}2037{_}being assigned to the night Office, Matins; and Psalms CIX.--CL.
10058{_}2038{_}for the day Offices, Lauds to Compline.
10058{_}2039{_}From this latter division has been made:--  (1) a selection of psalms suitable by their character and meaning to Lauds (_vide infra_, psalms at Lauds);  (2) a selection of psalms suitable to Compline;  (3) the psalms long used in the small Hours of Sunday's Office;  (4) the first psalms assigned by Pope Pius V. to Prime on Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday and Friday.
10058{_}2040{_}The remaining psalms are divided into seven groups, in simple numerical order.
10058{_}2041{_}The psalms of Matins generally come first, and are followed immediately by the groups of psalms for the day Hours.
10058{_}2042{_}In the new Breviary, seven new canticles are added to the ten, which stood in the older book.
10058{_}2043{_}The ten taken from the old and from the new Testament are _Audite coeli_ (Deut., chap.
10058{_}2044{_}32) in Lauds for Saturday; _Benedicite_ (Daniel, chap.
10058{_}2045{_}3) Sunday's Lauds; _Cantemus_ (Exod., chap.
10058{_}2046{_}15) Thursday's Lauds; _Confitebor_ (Isaias, chap.
10058{_}2047{_}12) Monday's Lauds; _Domine audivi_ (Habacuc, chap.
10058{_}2048{_}3) Friday's Lauds; _Ego dixi_ (Isaias, chap.
10058{_}2049{_}38) Tuesday's Lauds; _Exultavit_ (I. Kings, chap 2) Wednesday's Lauds.
10058{_}2050{_}From the new Testament we have _Benedictus, Magnificat, Nunc dimittis_.
10058{_}2051{_}To these are now added _Audite verbum_ (Jeremias, chap.
10058{_}2052{_}31), _Benedictus es_ (I.
10058{_}2053{_}Paralip., chap.
10058{_}2054{_}29), _Benedictus es_ (Daniel, chap.
10058{_}2055{_}3), _Hymnum cantemus_ (Judith, chap.
10058{_}2056{_}16), _Magnus es_ (Tobias, chap.
10058{_}2057{_}13), _Miserere nostri_ (Ecclus.
10058{_}2058{_}36), _Vere tu es Deus_ (Isaias, chap.
10058{_}2059{_}45).
10058{_}2060{_}(_Cf.
10058{_}2061{_}The New Psalter_, Burton and Myers, pp.
10058{_}2062{_}51-52).
10058{_}2063{_}"The psalms retain the accentuation of the Latin words, which was inserted at the request of Pius V. in the Reformed Breviary of 1568; and also the asterisk, which was introduced to mark the division of the verses of the Psalms in Urban VIII.
10058{_}2064{_}'s Reform in 1632."
10058{_}2065{_}The verse division of the psalms do not, in the Breviary, always coincide with those of the Vulgate--e.g., Psalm X.:--  PSALTER                        VULGATE  Dominus in templo sancto suo   Dominus in templo sancto suo Dominus in coelo sedes ejus    Dominus in coela sedes ejus: (v.4).
10058{_}2066{_}Oculi ejus in pauperem respsiciunt;                                palpebrae ejus                                interrogant filios hominum                               (verse 5).
10058{_}2067{_}The present verse divisions of the Vulgate were introduced by a Calvinistic printer of Geneva, who used them in an edition of the Greek new Testament published in 1561.
10058{_}2068{_}Formerly, biblical chapters were, for sake of reference, divided into seven sections denoted by letters of the alphabet a, b, c, etc.
10058{_}2069{_}In the older breviaries, the reference to the little lesson at Compline stood, I.
10058{_}2070{_}Pet.
10058{_}2071{_}v.c.
10058{_}2072{_}The new Breviary has adopted the modern form of reference, and we now read I.
10058{_}2073{_}Pet.
10058{_}2074{_}v. 8-9.
10058{_}2075{_}It is sometimes confusing to find reference made to the psalms by non-Catholic writers.
10058{_}2076{_}This arises from the different method of numbering which is used by them.
10058{_}2077{_}In the Greek version of the old Testament--the septuagent--the Psalter is arranged differently from the Hebrew.
10058{_}2078{_}Psalms 9 and 10 are counted as one and so are Psalms 114 and 115, but 116 and 117 are divided into two, leaving the complete number 150, as in the Hebrew version.
10058{_}2079{_}The Vulgate and the Douay version follow the Greek, and Psalm 9 contains 21 verses, not 38 as in the English Authorised Version.
10058{_}2080{_}The English revised version follows the numbering of the Vulgate.
10058{_}2081{_}"Our Latin version of the Psalms is that of the old Itala; it was not made directly on the Hebrew original ... it is then a translation (the Greek).
10058{_}2082{_}By the time of St. Jerome, it had become very faulty, owing to the very many transcriptions which had been made of it; and this great scholar revised it, about 383 A.D., on the request of Pope Damascus.
10058{_}2083{_}His corrections were not very numerous, because, he feared to upset, by too many changes, the habits of the faithful, most of whom knew the psalms by heart.
10058{_}2084{_}This first version is known as the Roman Psalter.
10058{_}2085{_}It was soon deemed insufficient.
10058{_}2086{_}St. Jerome once more set to work between 387 and 391, and published a second edition, more carefully and more extensively corrected, of the Italic version of the Psalms; it is called the _Gallican Psalter_, because it was adopted by the churches of Gaul.
10058{_}2087{_}When he, later on, translated the Old Testament from the Hebrew, he published his third edition of the Psalms, the _Hebraic Psalter_.
10058{_}2088{_}This version was a good one, but the faithful were so familiar with the old Itala psalter that the Church, in her wisdom, thought best to keep it in the editions of the Vulgate according to the Gallican form.... Our official version of the psalms is then in many ways defective.
10058{_}2089{_}It is frequently incorrect and barbarous in style, obscure in places, and even fails at times to give the exact sense of the original.
10058{_}2090{_}Although our Vulgate is not perfect, it possesses admirable strength and conciseness, joined to an agreeable savour which gives it the greatest value and causes the words of the sacred singers, under this form of the Latin spoken by the people, to strike the mind and become engraved upon the memory much better than if they were clothed in all the elegance of a modern tongue" (Vigouroux; _Manuel Biblique_, tom.
10058{_}2091{_}ii., 663-664).
10058{_}2092{_}The following replies by the Biblical Commission (May, 1910) may not be deemed out of place:--  I.
10058{_}2093{_}Whether the appellations, Psalms of David, Hymns of David, Davidical Psaltery, employed in the old collections and in the Councils themselves to designate the Book of the one hundred and fifty Psalms of the Old Testament, as well as the opinion of many Fathers and Doctors who held that absolutely all the psalms of the Psaltery are to be ascribed to David alone, have so much force that David must be regarded as the sole author of the entire Psaltery?
10058{_}2094{_}ANSWER: In the negative.
10058{_}2095{_}II.
10058{_}2096{_}Whether it may rightly be argued from the concordance of the Hebrew text with the Alexandrine Greek text and other ancient versions, that the titles prefixed to the Hebrew text are older than the version known as the Septuagint, and that therefore they have been derived if not from the authors themselves of the Psalms at least from the ancient Judaic tradition?
10058{_}2097{_}ANSWER: In the affirmative.
10058{_}2098{_}III.
10058{_}2099{_}Whether the said titles of the Psalms, as witnesses of Judaic tradition, may be prudently called into question when there is no grave argument against their genuineness?
10058{_}2100{_}ANSWER: In the negative.
10058{_}2101{_}IV.
10058{_}2102{_}Whether, considering the not unfrequent testimonies of the Sacred Scripture concerning the natural skill of David, illumined by the gift of the Holy Ghost, in the composition of religious canticles, the institutions laid down by him for the liturgical chant of the Psalms, the attribution to him of Psalms made both in the Old and New Testament and in the very inscriptions which have been prefixed to the Psalms from antiquity, and in addition to all this the agreement of the Jews and the Fathers and Doctors of the Church, it can be prudently denied that David is the principal author of the canticles of the Psaltery, or that it can be affirmed that only a few of the canticles are to be attributed to the Royal Psalmist?
10058{_}2103{_}ANSWER: In the negative to both parts.
10058{_}2104{_}V. Whether, specifically, the Davidical origin can be denied of those psalms which both in the Old and the New Testament are cited expressly under the name of David, among which are specially to be reckoned Psalm II., "Quare fremuerunt gentes"; Psalm XV., "Conserva me Domine"; Psalm XVII., "Diligam te, Domine fortitudo mea"; Psalm XXXI., "Beati quorum remissae sunt iniquitates"; Psalm LXVIII., "Salvum me fac, Deus"; Psalm CIX., "Dixit Dominus Domino meo"?
10058{_}2105{_}ANSWER: In the negative.
10058{_}2106{_}VI.
10058{_}2107{_}Whether it is possible to admit the opinion of those who hold that among the Psalms of the Psaltery there are some, either of David or of other authors which on account of liturgical or musical reasons, the carelessness of amanuenses or other unknown causes, have been divided or united; and also that there are other Psalms such as the "Miserere mei, Deus," which in order that they might be better adapted to the historical circumstances or solemnities of the Jewish people have been slightly revised or modified, by the omission or addition of a versicle or two saving, however, the inspiration of the whole sacred text?
10058{_}2108{_}ANSWER: In the affirmative to both parts.
10058{_}2109{_}VII.
10058{_}2110{_}Whether the opinion can with probability be maintained of those among more recent writers who have endeavoured to show from merely internal indications or an inaccurate interpretation of the sacred text that not a few of the psalms were composed after the time of Esdras and Nehemias, or even after the time of the Macchabees?
10058{_}2111{_}ANSWER: In the negative.
10058{_}2112{_}VIII.
10058{_}2113{_}Whether from the manifold testimonies of the Sacred Books of the New Testament, and the unanimous agreement of the Fathers, as well as from the admission of the writers of the Jewish people, several prophetic and Messianic psalms are to be recognised, as prophesying concerning the coming kingdom, priesthood, passion, death and resurrection of the future Redeemer; and that therefore the opinion is to be absolutely rejected of those who, perverting the prophetic and Messianic character of the Psalms, twist these same prophecies regarding Christ into merely a prediction regarding the future lot of the chosen people?
10058{_}2114{_}ANSWER: In the affirmative to both parts.
10058{_}2115{_}On May 1, 1910, in an audience graciously granted to both Most Reverend Consultors Secretaries His Holiness approved the foregoing answers and ordered that they be published.
10058{_}2116{_}Rome, May 1, 1910.
10058{_}2117{_}PULCRANUS VIGOUROUX, P.S.S.
10058{_}2118{_}LAURENTIUS JANSSENS, O.S.B.
10058{_}2119{_}Consultors Secretaries.
10058{_}2120{_}The Psalms were always dear to the hearts of Christians.
10058{_}2121{_}Our Lord died with the words of a psalm on His sacred lips: "Into thy hands I commend my spirit" (Psalm 30, v. 6).
10058{_}2122{_}Millions of dying Christians have repeated His great prayer.
10058{_}2123{_}On the Church's very birthday, when St. Peter preached the first Christian sermon, he had three texts and two of them were from the Psalms (Acts II.).
10058{_}2124{_}To an educated and rigid Pharisee like St. Paul they were a treasure house of teaching.
10058{_}2125{_}To the early Christians the Psalms were a prayer book, for there was no Christian literature.
10058{_}2126{_}It was twenty-five years after the Ascension before the first books of the New Testament were written.
10058{_}2127{_}Hence St. Paul and St. James tell their fellow Christians to use the Psalms in worship (Ephesians, v. 19; Colos.
10058{_}2128{_}iii.
10058{_}2129{_}16; I. St. James 5-13).
10058{_}2130{_}Some of the greatest of the early Christian writers and saints, Origen, St. Athanasius, Hilary of Poitiers, St. Ambrose, St. Chrysostom, Bede, and St. Augustine all studied the psalms deeply and wrote learned commentaries on them.
10058{_}2131{_}The works of later saints abound in happy and beautiful quotations from these religious poems.
10058{_}2132{_}With them, too, as with those holy people of whom St. Chrysostom wrote, "David is first, last and midst."
10058{_}2133{_}For many years no priest was ordained who could not recite the whole Psalter without the aid of a book, This veneration of the inspired words deserves respect and imitation.
10058{_}2134{_}The learned Calmet (1672-1757) writing of the universal esteem and study of the Psalms, said that then there existed more than a thousand commentaries on them.
10058{_}2135{_}Since then, the number has been doubled; so great and universal is the reverence and esteem in which this book of Scripture is held.
10058{_}2136{_}To conclude this very long note on the Psalms I quote the quaint words of a mediaeval poet.
10058{_}2137{_}It shows how the saints of old found their Master in the songs of His great ancestor:--       Rithmis et sensu verborum consociatum      Psalterium Jesu, sic est opus hoc vocitatum,      Qui legit intente, quocunque dolore prematur,      Sentiet inde bonum, dolor ejus et alleviatur;      Ergo pius legat hoc ejus sub amore libenter,      Cujus ibi Nomen scriptum videt esse frequenter.
10058{_}2138{_}_Versicle and respond_ are placed after the psalms and before the lessons to rouse the attention which is necessary before all prayer, and the lessons are a noble form of prayer.
10058{_}2139{_}These little prayers are of very ancient origin and were dealt with by Alcuin (735-804) in his recension of the Gregorian books for use in Gaul.
10058{_}2140{_}His pupil, Amalare, also studied them, so that a meaning should be found in what was sung, and that the truncated repetitions should be avoided.
10058{_}2141{_}He retained what was traditional and ancient, introduced versicles and responds taken from ancient Roman books and from books belonging to Metz, selected passages from the Gospels which seem to fit in with the antiphons and added them to what he found in the Roman books, made alterations in the order here and there and gave completion to the whole by adding some offices for saints' days proper to the Church of Metz (Baudot, _The Roman Breviary_, p. 88).
10058{_}2142{_}Amalare had been administrator of the diocese of Lyons during the exile of Agobard the Archbishop.
10058{_}2143{_}The latter, with learning and bitterness, attacked the reforms of Amalare, but, "in spite of all, the reform of Amalare held its ground in Metz, and then in the greater number of the churches north of the Alps" (Baudot, _op.
10058{_}2144{_}cit._).
10058{_}2145{_}Much of the work of Amalare stands in our Breviary.
10058{_}2146{_}_Pater Noster_ is said to beg from God, light and grace to understand the doctrine contained in the lessons.
10058{_}2147{_}In choir, a part of the Pater Noster is said in common and in a loud voice to recall the Communion of saints.
10058{_}2148{_}_Absolutions and Blessings_.
10058{_}2149{_}"The custom of giving a blessing before the lections was already in existence in the fourth century.
10058{_}2150{_}The ruler of the choir, who gave it in the beginning, gave also the signal for the termination of the lesson by the words, 'Tu autem' (scil, desine or cessa), to which the reader responded 'Domine miserere nobis,' while the choir answered _Deo gratias_.
10058{_}2151{_}In the palace of Aix-la-Chapeile, it was by knocking, and not by the words _Tu autem_, that the Emperor Charlemagne gave the signal for the conclusion of the lections, while the lector recited himself, _Tu autem, Domine miserere nobis_.
10058{_}2152{_}The _Rituale Ecclesiae Dunelmensis_, containing fragments of the Roman liturgy from the end of the seventh to the ninth and tenth centuries, includes forms of blessing for the different festivals, sometimes three, sometimes nine.
10058{_}2153{_}In the latter case each lesson was provided with its own form of blessing, which correspond with the mystery commemorated by the festival.
10058{_}2154{_}The absolutions, _Exaudi Domine_ and _A vinculis peccatorum_ did not appear until the succeeding period" (Baudot, _op.
10058{_}2155{_}cit._, p. 74).
10058{_}2156{_}In offices of three and of nine lessons, the lessons are preceded by the absolutions and blessings as they stand in the ordinarium, except in the Office for the Dead and Tenebrae Offices when they are not said.
10058{_}2157{_}The Absolution is said immediately after the Pater Noster which follows the versicle and response under the third, sixth or ninth psalm.
10058{_}2158{_}The first benediction is said immediately after it, and the second and third at the conclusion of the responses after each lesson and in reply to the words Jube Domine benedicere.
10058{_}2159{_}The three words are to be said (when only one person recites the office) before the short Lesson at Prime and Compline.
10058{_}2160{_}In an office of nine lessons, the absolutions and benedictions in the first two nocturns do not vary; but in the third nocturns the eighth benediction may be, if the office is of a saint, Cujus festum, or if of two or more saints, Quorum (vel quarum) festum.
10058{_}2161{_}The ninth may be _Ad societatem_ or, if the ninth lesson be a gospel extract with homily, _Per evangelica_.
10058{_}2162{_}In offices of three lessons the Absolution Exaudi is said on Monday and Thursday; Ipsius, on Tuesday and Friday; A vinculis, on Wednesday and Saturday.
10058{_}2163{_}But the benedictions vary.
10058{_}2164{_}Thus, when a gospel extract and a homily are read, the three benedictions are Evangelica, Divinum, Ad societatem.
10058{_}2165{_}When with the three lessons, no gospel extract is read, the benedictions are Benedictione, Unigenitus, Spiritus Sancti.
10058{_}2166{_}In an office of a saint or saints, where the total number of lessons to be said is three (e.g., the Office of SS.
10058{_}2167{_}Abdon et Sennen, 30 July), where first two lessons are from Scripture occurring and last lesson gives lives of these saints, the benedictions are, Ille nos, Cujus (vel Quorum aut Quarum) festum, Ad societatem.
10058{_}2168{_}_Lessons._ In the early days of Christendom, the Divine Office consisted in the singing of psalms, the reading of portions of Sacred Scripture and the saying of prayers.
10058{_}2169{_}The principle of continuous reading of the books of the Bible bears an early date.
10058{_}2170{_}Later were added readings from the acts of the martyrs, and later still, readings from the homilies of the Fathers.
10058{_}2171{_}Till the seventh century the ferial Office had no lessons and the Sunday Office had only three, all taken from the Bible, which was read in its entirety, yearly.
10058{_}2172{_}In the seventh century, ferial Offices received three lessons.
10058{_}2173{_}About the time of St. Gregory, (died 604) the Office for Matins was divided into three parts or nocturns, each having lessons.
10058{_}2174{_}The lessons for the second and third nocturns were not taken from the Bible, but from the works of the Fathers.
10058{_}2175{_}These extracts were collected in book form--the _homilaria_.
10058{_}2176{_}The collection of extracts made by Paul the deacon (730-797) and used by Charles the Great (742-814) in his kingdom, form the foundation of the collected extracts in our Breviaries.
10058{_}2177{_}The scripture lessons in our Breviaries are generally known as "the scripture occurring," and are so arranged that each book of scripture is begun at least, except the books, Josue, Judges, Ruth, Paralipomenon and the Canticle of Canticles.
10058{_}2178{_}Quignonez arranged in his reform that the whole Bible should be read yearly.
10058{_}2179{_}But his book was withdrawn by Pope Paul IV.
10058{_}2180{_}in 1558.
10058{_}2181{_}Although the ecclesiastical year begins with Advent, the beginnings of the Bible are not read till March.
10058{_}2182{_}Hence, we begin the lessons from Genesis, after Septuagesima Sunday, and not, as we should naturally expect, at Advent, the beginning of the ecclesiastical year.
10058{_}2183{_}The order in which the Scripture lessons are read does not follow the order in which the books of the Bible stand in the sacred volume.
10058{_}2184{_}Thus, the Acts of the Apostles begin on the Monday after Low Sunday and are read for a fortnight; The Apocalypse begins on the third Sunday after Easter and is read for a week; then the Epistle of St. James begins, and so on, with special regard to the feasts of the time, rather than to the order of the books of the Bible.
10058{_}2185{_}The lessons of the second nocturn are generally commemorative of a saint or some episode of a saint's life.
10058{_}2186{_}They have been much, and often ignorantly criticised, even by priests.
10058{_}2187{_}The science of hagiology is a very wide and far-reaching one, which demands knowledge and reverence.
10058{_}2188{_}Priests wishing to study its elements may read with pleasure and profit and wonder _The Legends of the Saints_, by Pere H. Delehaye, S.J., Bollandist (Longmans, 3s.
10058{_}2189{_}6d.).
10058{_}2190{_}"Has Lectiones secundi Nocturni ex Historiis sanctorum, quas nunc habemus recognitas fuisse a doctissimis Cardinalibus Bellarmino et Baronio, qui rejecerunt ea omnia, quae jure merito in dubium revocari poterant et approbatus sub Clemente VIII."
10058{_}2191{_}(Gavantus).
10058{_}2192{_}And Merati adds "quod aliqua qua controversia erant utpote alicujus aliquam haberent probabilitatem, ideo rejecta non fuerant sed retenta eo modo quo erant cum falsitatis argui non possent, quamvis fortasse opposita sententia sit a pluribus recepta" (Merati, _Obser.
10058{_}2193{_}ad Gavant_, sec.
10058{_}2194{_}v., chap.
10058{_}2195{_}xii., nn.
10058{_}2196{_}10 and 16).
10058{_}2197{_}The words of these learned men and the writings of the learned Bollandist mentioned above are worthy of consideration, as sometimes priests are puzzled about the truth and accuracy of the incidents recorded in those lessons of the second nocturn.
10058{_}2198{_}They should be treated with reverence.
10058{_}2199{_}The ignorant flippancy of a priest in an article (in a very secular periodical) on St. Expeditus gave great pain to Catholics and gave material for years to come to scoffing bigots.
10058{_}2200{_}"Legends, _i.e._, narratives, were based upon documents of the nature described above, and worked up by later writers, either for the purpose of edification or from the point of view of the historian.
10058{_}2201{_}The writings, however, differ endlessly as to their value, according to the knowledge and authority possessed by the writers, and according to their nearness to the events described.
10058{_}2202{_}There were many martyrs whose sufferings were recorded in no acta or passiones, but were imprinted on the memory of men and became part of the traditions handed down in the community, until they were finally committed to writing.
10058{_}2203{_}The later this took place the worse for the authenticity.
10058{_}2204{_}For it was then that anachronisms, alterations in titles, changes in the persons and other similar historical errors could more easily creep into the narrative, as we know in fact they have done in many instances.
10058{_}2205{_}The historical sense was unfortunately lacking to the Franks and Byzantines, as well as all idea of sound criticism.
10058{_}2206{_}"A false kind of patriotism and national pride often go along with credulity, so that we find here and there in literature of this kind, even downright fabrication.
10058{_}2207{_}After the introduction of printing, by which literature became more widely diffused, and comparative criticism was rendered possible, it at once became evident among Catholics that error was mixed with truth and that a sifting of the one from the other was necessary, and, in many cases, possible" (Kellner, _Heorlology_, pp.
10058{_}2208{_}209-210).
10058{_}2209{_}"It was not the intention of the Church or of the compilers and authors of the service books to claim historical authority for their statements.
10058{_}2210{_}And so, the Popes themselves have directed many emendations to be made in the legends of the Breviary, although many others still remain to be effected" (Dom Baumer, _Histoire Du Breviare Roman_).
10058{_}2211{_}Cf.
10058{_}2212{_}Dom Cabrol, _Le Reforme du Breviare_, pp.
10058{_}2213{_}61-63.
10058{_}2214{_}_Responsories._ (Title XXVII.).
10058{_}2215{_}In the new Breviary the responsories to the lessons have been restored to their place of honour.
10058{_}2216{_}They are of ancient origin, but "how they came to have a place in the Divine Office, who was responsible for their composition, what was the process of development until they reached their present form, are questions upon which liturgical writers are not quite agreed" (Rev.
10058{_}2217{_}M. Eaton, _Irish Eccles.
10058{_}2218{_}Record_, January, 1915).
10058{_}2219{_}Amalare of Metz found them fully formed and placed.
10058{_}2220{_}The rule of St. Benedict, written about 530 A.D., mentions them as a recognised part of Matins.
10058{_}2221{_}In solemn vigils, in the early Church, the congregation took part in the psalm singing, and hence we find _psalmi responsorii_ mentioned, and we still have a typical instance in the Invitatory Psalm of our Office.
10058{_}2222{_}Probably, some similar practice existed in the readings from Sacred Scripture.
10058{_}2223{_}"At those primitive vigils, then, after the reading of the Sacred Scripture, the responsory was given by the precentor and the assembled faithful took up the words and chanted them forth in the same simple melody.
10058{_}2224{_}Next, a verse was sung frequently echoing the same sentiment, and the choir again, as in the _psalmi responsorii_, repeated the refrain or the responsorii proper.
10058{_}2225{_}Frequently other verses were added according to the dignity of the festivals, and after each the faithful struck in with the original refrain.... At first those responsories would probably have been extempore ... left to the genius or to the inspiration of the individual chanter, but gradually, by a survival of the fittest, the most beautiful ones became stereotyped and spread throughout several churches.... Later they were carefully collected, arranged and codified by St. Gregory or one of his predecessors and passed into all the books of liturgy" (Rev.
10058{_}2226{_}M. Eaton, _loc.
10058{_}2227{_}cit._).
10058{_}2228{_}Monsignor Battifol (_History of the Roman Breviary_, Eng, trans., p. 78) says that these parts of the liturgy, in beauty and eloquence rival the chorus dialogues of Greek drama, and quotes as an example the _Aspiciens a longe_ from the first Sunday of Advent.
10058{_}2229{_}_Rubrics._ The responsories, as a rule, are said after each lesson of Matins.
10058{_}2230{_}When the _Te Deum_ is said after the ninth lesson, there are only eight responsories.
10058{_}2231{_}At the end of the third, sixth and eighth lesson the _Gloria Patri_ with a repetition of part of the responsory is said.
10058{_}2232{_}It is said in the second responsory in offices of three lessons only.
10058{_}2233{_}In Passiontide the _Gloria Patri_ is not said, but the responsory is repeated _ab initio_.
10058{_}2234{_}In the Requiem Office _Gloria Patri_ is replaced by "_requiem aeternam_."
10058{_}2235{_}In the Sundays of Advent, Sundays after Septuagesima until Palm Sunday, and in the triduum before Easter, there are nine responsories recited.
10058{_}2236{_}Perhaps an explanation of the rubric may not be useless.
10058{_}2237{_}The asterisk (*) indicates the part which should be repeated first after the verse and immediately after the _Gloria Patri_.
10058{_}2238{_}The _Gloria Patri_ should be said to include the word _sancto_, and _sicut erat_ should not be said.
10058{_}2239{_}Some responsories have two or three asterisks, and then the repetitions should be made from one asterisk to another and not as far as the verse ending.
10058{_}2240{_}Examples may be seen in the responsories for the first Sunday of Advent and in the _Libera nos_ of the Requiem Office.
10058{_}2241{_}The responsories of the Requiem Office--which is almost the only Office which missionary priests have an opportunity of reciting in choir--are highly praised for their beauty of thought and expression.
10058{_}2242{_}They were compiled by Maurice de Sully (circa 1196), Bishop of Paris.
10058{_}2243{_}_Symbolism of the Rubric._ The responsories are placed after the lessons, the old writers on liturgy say, to excite attention and devotion, to thank God for the instruction given in the lessons, to make us realise and practise what has been read and to teach us that "Blessed are they who hear the word of God and keep it."
10058{_}2244{_}Again, those writers knew why the chanter said only one verse and the worshippers replied in chorus--to show that all their souls were united and free from schism.
10058{_}2245{_}_Te Deum_ (Title XXXI.).
10058{_}2246{_}_Author._ In the Breviary prior to the reform of Pius X., this hymn was printed under the words "Hymnus SS.
10058{_}2247{_}Ambrosii et Augustini."
10058{_}2248{_}However, "no one thinks now of attributing this canto to either St. Ambrose or St. Augustine" (Battifol, _op.
10058{_}2249{_}cit._, p. 110).
10058{_}2250{_}Formerly, it was piously believed to have been composed and sung by these saints on the evening of Augustine's baptism.
10058{_}2251{_}The question of the authorship of this hymn has led to much study and much controversy.
10058{_}2252{_}Some scholars attribute it to St. Hilary, others to Sisebut, a Benedictine; others to Nicetas, Bishop of Treves, in the year 527.
10058{_}2253{_}To-day, the opinion of the learned Benedictine, Dom.
10058{_}2254{_}Morin--who follows the readings of the Irish manuscripts--that the hymn was written by Nicetas of Remesiana (circa 400 A.D.), is the most probable.
10058{_}2255{_}This opinion has been criticised by several Continental scholars (V. _Cath.
10058{_}2256{_}Encly_., art.
10058{_}2257{_}"Te Deum").
10058{_}2258{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2259{_}The Te Deum is always said at the end of Matins, unless in Matins of Feast of Holy Innocents, of Sundays of Advent, and from Septuagesima to Palm Sunday, and ferias outside Eastertide (from Low Sunday to Ascension Day).
10058{_}2260{_}_The Structure of the Hymn_.
10058{_}2261{_}In this wonderful composition, there are probably two hymns connected, and followed by a set of versicles and.
10058{_}2262{_}responses, which might be used with any similar hymn.
10058{_}2263{_}It is probable that the first hymn (_Te Deum ... Paraclitum Spiritum_), lines 1 to 13 of Te Deum are older than the second part, which was written probably as a sequel to the early hymn.
10058{_}2264{_}The rhythm of the hymn is very beautiful, being free from abruptness and monotony.
10058{_}2265{_}Students of poetry may note that seven lines have the exact hexameter ending, if scanned accentually, as voce proclamant; Deus sabbaoth, etc.
10058{_}2266{_}Seven have two dactyls, as laudabilis numerus, laudat exercitus; one ends with spondees, apostolorum chorus.
10058{_}2267{_}The other six lines have a less regular ending.
10058{_}2268{_}This hymn of praise to the Blessed Trinity is divided into two parts and seems to be modelled on the lines of the Psalm 148, _Laudate Dominum de coelis_ (see Sunday Lauds I.).
10058{_}2269{_}The verses 1 to 6 of the hymn, like the opening verses of the psalm, record the worship and adoration of the angels.
10058{_}2270{_}The second part of the hymn records the worship of human beings living or dead--Apostles, Prophets, Martyrs.
10058{_}2271{_}The second hymn, _Tu Rex gloriae Christi_, etc., is a prayer to Christ, the God Incarnate, the Redeemer now in Glory, to aid His servants and to aid them to be of the number of His saints in everlasting glory.
10058{_}2272{_}The third part of the hymn, vv.
10058{_}2273{_}22-29 (_Salvum fac_ ... _in aeternum_) is considered by scholars to be simply versicles, responses and prayers; the verses 22-23 (Salvum fac... usque in aeternum).
10058{_}2274{_}being the versicle, and verses 24-25 (Per singulos dies... saeculi), verse 2 of Psalm 144 being the response before the beautiful verses of prayer "Dignare Domine die isto sine peccato nos custodire," etc.
10058{_}2275{_}"Vouchsafe, O Lord, to keep us this day from sin; O Lord, have mercy on us," etc., etc.
10058{_}2276{_}This hymn has a special interest for Irish priests, as the Irish recensions of it, found in the Bangor Antiphoner (to be seen in the Library of Trinity College, Dublin) are of the greatest value to scholars engaged in critical study.
10058{_}2277{_}They date from the tenth century, and give Nicetas as the author.
10058{_}2278{_}The wording in the old Irish Antiphoner differs in some verses from the text given in our Breviary.
10058{_}2279{_}Thus, in verse 6, the Bangor text has, _universa_ before the word _terra_; again, in verse 18, the Breviary reads "_Tu ad deteram Dei sedes_," Bangor, and probably more correctly, reads _sedens_.
10058{_}2280{_}Verses 26-29, "_Dignare Domine_... _confundar in aeternum_" are not found in the Irish book.
10058{_}2281{_}Those who wish to study these old Irish MSS.
10058{_}2282{_}may receive great help from Warren's _Bangor Antiphoner_ (II., pp.83-91) and light comes too from Julian's _Dictionary of Hymnology_ (pp.
10058{_}2283{_}1120-1121).
10058{_}2284{_}SOME TEXTS AND INTENTIONS WHICH MAY HELP TOWARDS THE WORTHY RECITATION   OF MATINS (_vide_ pages 4, 120).
10058{_}2285{_}"Matutina ligat Christum qui crimina purgat."
10058{_}2286{_}"Although I should die with Thee, I will not deny Thee."
10058{_}2287{_}"And in like manner also said they all."
10058{_}2288{_}"Pray, lest you enter into temptation,"   "And being in agony He prayed the longer."
10058{_}2289{_}"Friend, whereunto art thou come?--"   "And they holding Jesus led Him away"--the Garden.
10058{_}2290{_}"Art thou one of His disciples?"
10058{_}2291{_}"My kingdom is not of this world"--Before the High Priest.
10058{_}2292{_}_General Intentions_:-Exaltation of the Church; the Pope; the Mission to the heathen; Christian nations; the conversion of the heretics, infidels and sinners; the Catholic laity; the Catholic priesthood.
10058{_}2293{_}_Personal Intentions_:-Lively faith; a greater hope; ardent charity.
10058{_}2294{_}_Special Intentions_:-For parents; for benefactors; for those in sorrow; dying sinners; deceased priests of Ireland; for the conversion of England; for vocations to the priesthood.
10058{_}2295{_}CHAPTER II.
10058{_}2296{_}LAUDS.
10058{_}2297{_}_Etymology, Definition, Symbolism_.
10058{_}2298{_}The word "Lauds" is derived from the Latin _laus_, praise.
10058{_}2299{_}It is applied to this Hour, as it is _par excellence_, the hour in which God's praises are chanted by His Church.
10058{_}2300{_}This Hour succeeds Matins and precedes Prime.
10058{_}2301{_}The name is said to have been given to this Hour on account of the last three Psalms, which formerly formed part of the Office.
10058{_}2302{_}In these Psalms, 148, 149, 150, the word _Laudate_ recurs several times.
10058{_}2303{_}Before the eighth century the Hour was called "Matutinum," or morning Office, and sometimes it was called _Gallicinum_ or _Galli cantus_ from being recited at cock-crow.
10058{_}2304{_}This is the Office of daybreak and hence its symbolism is of Christ's resurrection.
10058{_}2305{_}"Christ, the light of the world, rose from the tomb on Easter morning, like a radiant sun, trampling over darkness and shedding His brightness upon the earth.
10058{_}2306{_}The hymns, psalms, antiphons and versicles of Lauds, all proclaim the mystery of Christ's Resurrection, and the light which enlightens our souls.
10058{_}2307{_}The reform of the Psalter in 1911 has not always preserved this liturgical idea; nevertheless, the character of the Office has not been altered.
10058{_}2308{_}Lauds remains the true morning prayer, which hails in the rising sun, the image of Christ triumphant--consecrates to Him the opening day.
10058{_}2309{_}No other morning prayer is comparable to this" (Dom.
10058{_}2310{_}F. Cabrol, _The Day Hours of the Church_, London, 1910).
10058{_}2311{_}_Antiquity_.
10058{_}2312{_}The Christians, in their night vigils, followed the pious practices of the Jews, as to prayers at dead of night and at dawn, Hence, the Hour, Lauds is of great antiquity, coming, perhaps, from Apostolic times.
10058{_}2313{_}It is found well established in the very earliest accounts of Christian liturgy.
10058{_}2314{_}The old writers on liturgy loved to dwell on pious congruities and parallelisms.
10058{_}2315{_}They ask the questions, why did the early Christians pray at dawn and why is the practice continued?
10058{_}2316{_}They answer at great length, I will try to summarise their holy themes.
10058{_}2317{_}The early Christians prayed at dawn, 1. that in the New Law the figures of the Old may be fulfilled; 2. to honour the risen Saviour and to remind us of our resurrection; 3. to glorify Jesus typified by the physical light.
10058{_}2318{_}"I am the Light of the world" (St. John, viii.
10058{_}2319{_}12); 4. because at dawn, after rest, body and soul are refreshed and ready to devote all their powers to God, free from distractions and noise.
10058{_}2320{_}Each dawn, revealing God's wondrous work, should hear God's praises in the most sublime words ever uttered, the Psalms (e.g., _Dominus regnavit, Jubilate Deo_, etc., etc.
10058{_}2321{_}); 5. because God seems more disposed to hear prayers made at that hour.
10058{_}2322{_}For, He has said, "Yet if thou wilt arise early to God and wilt beseech the Almighty...
10058{_}2323{_}He will presently awake unto thee and make the dwelling of thy justice peaceable" (Job, viii.
10058{_}2324{_}5-6).
10058{_}2325{_}"I love them that love me; and they that in the morning early watch for me shall find me" (Proverbs viii.
10058{_}2326{_}17).
10058{_}2327{_}_Structure_.
10058{_}2328{_}If Lauds succeeds Matins immediately, _Pater Noster_ and _Ave Maria_ are omitted, and the Hour begins with _Deus in adjutorium_.
10058{_}2329{_}At these words it is a practice but not an obligation to make the sign of the cross from head to breast (see Vespers, _infra_).
10058{_}2330{_}Then the Gloria Patri, Sicut erat, Amen, Alleluia are said before the antiphons and psalms.
10058{_}2331{_}But if a notable delay--say, of ten minutes' duration--be made between the end of Matins and the start of Lauds, the _Pater Noster_ and _Ave Maria_ begin Lauds.
10058{_}2332{_}After the psalms, comes the Capitulum, the Hymn, Versicle and Response, antiphon to Benedictus, Canticle _Benedictus Dominus Deus Israel, Gloria Patri, Sicut erat_, Antiphon to Benedictus repeated, _Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo, Oremus_, collect, commemorations preceded by versicle, response and _Oremus_ before each.
10058{_}2333{_}Then _Dominus vobiscum_, _Et cum spiritu tuo_, _Benedicamus Domino, Deo Gratias, Fidelium animae, Amen_.
10058{_}2334{_}If another Hour do not succeed immediately, _Pater Noster_ (said silently), _Dominus det nobis_ (with a sign of the cross) _suam pacem, Et vitam aeternam_.
10058{_}2335{_}_Amen_.
10058{_}2336{_}Then is said the antiphon of the Blessed Virgin, Alma Redemptoris or Ave Regina, or Regina Coeli, or Salve Regina, according to the part of the ecclesiastical year for which each is assigned, with _versicle, response, oremus, collect, Divinum auxilium_.... Amen.
10058{_}2337{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2338{_}In the paragraphs dealing with the structure of this hour is given the rule for saying _Pater Noster_ and _Ave_, The Psalms for Lauds in the new Breviary follow these rules:--  _General Rule_: Psalms of the current day.
10058{_}2339{_}_Exception_: Sunday Psalms on the excepted Feasts.
10058{_}2340{_}In applying the general rule to Sundays and week days, it will be seen that the Psalter contains two sets of Psalms for Lauds.
10058{_}2341{_}The use of the two sets is as follows:--  _Sundays_:    (i) Throughout the year: first set of Psalms.
10058{_}2342{_}(ii) Sundays from Septuagesima to Easter: second set of Psalms.
10058{_}2343{_}_Ferias_: The first set of Psalms is to be used on:--     (i) Ferias throughout the year, not including those in Advent,        Septuagesima, Sexagesima and Quinquagesima weeks.
10058{_}2344{_}(ii) Ferias in Paschal time.
10058{_}2345{_}(iii) Feasts at any season of the year.
10058{_}2346{_}(iv) Vigils of Christmas and Epiphany.
10058{_}2347{_}The second set of Psalms is to be used on:--     (i) Ferias of Advent.
10058{_}2348{_}(ii) Ferias from Septuagesima to Wednesday in Holy Week, inclusive.
10058{_}2349{_}(iii) Vigils (common) outside Paschal time, when the Office of Vigil        is said (_New Psalter and Its Uses_, p. 188).
10058{_}2350{_}On Maundy Thursday, Good Friday and Holy Saturday, the Psalms of the Feria are to be said.
10058{_}2351{_}But the Canticle of Moses (Deut, 33) is not said on Holy Saturday.
10058{_}2352{_}_Antiphons_.
10058{_}2353{_}As a general rule antiphons of the current day of the week are to be said.
10058{_}2354{_}_Exceptions_.
10058{_}2355{_}(1) On excepted Feasts, (2) non-excepted Feasts which have proper antiphons, (3) Holy Week has special antiphons, (4) Six ferias before Christmas have special antiphons.
10058{_}2356{_}In Paschal time, all psalms and the canticles are recited under one antiphon.
10058{_}2357{_}Antiphon of Benedictus (1) Sunday antiphons are proper.
10058{_}2358{_}(2) Ferias throughout the year have antiphons of current feria.
10058{_}2359{_}But Ferias in Advent, and in Lent, in Passiontide, Paschal time and September Ember days have proper antiphons.
10058{_}2360{_}(3) Feasts have antiphons from proper or from common.
10058{_}2361{_}_Capitulum_ (Title XXIX.).
10058{_}2362{_}_Etymology, meaning and synonyms_.
10058{_}2363{_}The word _capitulum_ comes from the Latin, and means a little chapter, a heading, a beginning, an abridgment, because this little chapter is a little lesson, a brief extract from Sacred Scripture, the head or the beginning of the Epistle of the Mass of the Feast (Gavantus, Bona).
10058{_}2364{_}It is found in every Hour, except Matins.
10058{_}2365{_}It is known by other names, the summarium, collectio, collatio, lectio brevis, epistoletto, lectiuncula, Versiculus brevis.
10058{_}2366{_}_Antiquity_.
10058{_}2367{_}Some authors hold that this usage of reading a brief extract from Sacred Scripture is of Jewish origin.
10058{_}2368{_}For, the Jews were accustomed to interpose brief readings from Scripture prose in their psalm chanting service.
10058{_}2369{_}The _capitulum_ is found in Christian services of the fourth century; and St. Ambrose (340-397) is said to have instituted the _capitula_ of Terce, Sext and None.
10058{_}2370{_}This new practice spread quickly and several councils recommended or ordered the usage--e.g., the Council of Agde In 506 A.D.  _Remarks._ The _Capitulum_ is said always except from Holy Thursday to the Vespers of Saturday preceding Low Sunday, and in Requiem Offices.
10058{_}2371{_}In Compline it is said after the Hymn.
10058{_}2372{_}The _Capitulum_ of Lauds is ordinarily taken from the beginning of the Epistle of the Mass of the day of the feast.
10058{_}2373{_}Sext and None generally have their _capitula_ drawn from the middle and end of the same Epistle extract.
10058{_}2374{_}Terce has generally the same words for the _Capitulum_, as Vespers and Lauds, because it is the grandest and most sublime of the little Hours.
10058{_}2375{_}The _Capitulum_ is said without a blessing being sought, because it is (in choir) read by the Hebdomadarius, who there represents the person of Christ, just as the _Capitulum_ does too, and for Whom it would not be consonant to ask a blessing.
10058{_}2376{_}It concludes without _Tu autem_, because these words are correlative of _Jube_.
10058{_}2377{_}And since it is such a short lesson it is easy to recite it without fault or sin, the more so as it is read by the Hebdomadarius, who should be advanced in perfection.
10058{_}2378{_}It is short, whilst the lessons of Matins, the night Office, are long, because the day is specially given to toil and the night to contemplation.
10058{_}2379{_}During the recital of this little lesson all turn to the altar through respect for Christ, figured by the _Capitulum_.
10058{_}2380{_}Sometimes the words of the _Capitulum_ are from the Itala version and not from the Vulgate.
10058{_}2381{_}_Psalms and Canticles of Lauds_.
10058{_}2382{_}The Office of Lauds now consists of four Psalms and a canticle, followed by a little chapter, a hymn, versicle, antiphon, of Benedictus, the canticle, Benedictus and prayer.
10058{_}2383{_}One of the characteristics of Lauds is the canticle taken from the Old Testament.
10058{_}2384{_}Fourteen canticles taken from the Old Testament now find a place in our Breviaries.
10058{_}2385{_}Formerly, only seven canticles from the Old Testament were given in the Psaltery (cf.
10058{_}2386{_}_supra_, p. 149).
10058{_}2387{_}"If, according to the new distribution of the Psalter, the Psalms for Lauds do not refer so directly to the symbolism of sunrise, they are nevertheless more varied and are generally well chosen.
10058{_}2388{_}The canticles inserted among the Psalms have also been changed.
10058{_}2389{_}The whole selection is worthy of note.
10058{_}2390{_}It contains, besides those given in the former arrangement of the Psalter, others which are very beautiful and admirably prayerful.
10058{_}2391{_}"The hymns for Lauds, all ancient and varying with the seasons, form a fine collection.
10058{_}2392{_}Their theme is one: the rising of the sun as a symbol of Christ's resurrection, and the crowing of the cock, which arouses the sluggish and calls all to work.
10058{_}2393{_}Some of these hymns are of considerable poetical merit: that for Sunday, _Aeterne Rerum conditor_, is a little masterpiece.
10058{_}2394{_}"The 'Benedictus' corresponds with the _Magnificat_ of Vespers.
10058{_}2395{_}Both are sung with the same solemnity and are of the same importance; they form as it were the culminating point of their respective Hours, and for feast days the altar is incensed while they are chanted.
10058{_}2396{_}"The 'Benedictus' or Canticle of Zachary recalls the Precursor's mission of proclaiming the Messiah and the new alliance.
10058{_}2397{_}It is altogether appropriate to the Office of daybreak, as ushering in the dawn of a new era.
10058{_}2398{_}The closing verse speaks of the light which the announcement of the Messiah shed upon the nations 'sitting in darkness and in the shadow of death'" (Dom Cabrol, Introduction to _Day Hours of the Church_).
10058{_}2399{_}"This Canticle of Zachary (St. Luke i.
10058{_}2400{_}68-79) naturally falls into two parts.
10058{_}2401{_}The first (verses 68 to 75, 'Benedictus Dominus ... diebus nostris') is a song of thanksgiving for the fulfilment of the Messianic hopes of the Jews, to which is given a Christian sentiment.
10058{_}2402{_}The power, which was of old in the family of David for the defence of the nation, is being restored, and in a higher and more spiritual sense.
10058{_}2403{_}The Jews mourning under the Roman yoke prayed for deliverance through the house of David.
10058{_}2404{_}The 'deliverance,' a powerful salvation ('cornu salutis nobis') was at hand so that the Jews were seeing the fulfilment of God's promise made to Abraham, and this deliverance, this salvation was such that 'we may serve Him without fear in holiness and justice, all our days' (St. Luke i.
10058{_}2405{_}75).
10058{_}2406{_}"The second part of the canticle (verses 76-80, 'Et tu puer ... ad dirigendos pedes nostros') is an address by Zachary to his own son, who was to take an important part in the scheme of the powerful salvation and deliverance by the Messiah.
10058{_}2407{_}This canticle is known as the canticle of joyous hope, hence its use at funerals at the moment of interment, when words of thanksgiving for the Redemption are specially in place as an expression of Christian hope" (_Catholic Encyclopedia_, art.
10058{_}2408{_}"Benedictus").
10058{_}2409{_}_Oratio_ (Title XXX.).
10058{_}2410{_}The word _oratio_ has various meanings.
10058{_}2411{_}In the liturgy it is translated by the word "collect."
10058{_}2412{_}The word "collect" means either that the priest who celebrates Mass collects in a short form the needs, the thanksgivings and the praises of the people, to offer them up to God; or most probably "the original meaning seems to have been this: it was used for the service held at a certain church on the days when there was a station held somewhere else.
10058{_}2413{_}The people gathered together and became a collection at the first church; after certain prayers had been said they went in procession to the station church.
10058{_}2414{_}Just before they started, the celebrant said a prayer, the _oratio ad collectam_ (_ad collectionem populi_), the name would then be the same as _oratio super populum_, a title that still remains in our Missal, in Lent, for instance, after the Post-Communion.
10058{_}2415{_}This prayer, the collect, would be repeated at the beginning of Mass at the station itself.
10058{_}2416{_}Later writers find other meanings for the name.
10058{_}2417{_}Innocent III.
10058{_}2418{_}says that in this prayer the priest collects all the prayers of the faithful" (_De Sacr.
10058{_}2419{_}Altar.
10058{_}2420{_}Mystic_.
10058{_}2421{_}ii., 2).
10058{_}2422{_}See also Benedict XIV.
10058{_}2423{_}(_De SS.
10058{_}2424{_}Missae Sacr_.
10058{_}2425{_}ii., 5,--Dr. A. Fortescue, _Cath.
10058{_}2426{_}Encyl_., art.
10058{_}2427{_}"collect").
10058{_}2428{_}_Antiquity of collects_.
10058{_}2429{_}No one can say with certainty who the composers of the collects were.
10058{_}2430{_}All admit the antiquity of these compositions.
10058{_}2431{_}In the fourth century certain collects were believed to come from apostolic times; indeed, the collects read in the Mass on Good Friday, for Gentiles, Jews, heretics, schismatics, catechumens and infidels bear intrinsic notes of their antiquity.
10058{_}2432{_}Other liturgical collects show that they were composed in the days of persecution.
10058{_}2433{_}Others show their ages by their accurate expression of Catholic doctrine against, and their supplications for, heretics, Manicheans, Sabbelians, Arians, Pelagians and Nestorians.
10058{_}2434{_}St, Jerome in his Life of St. Hilarion (291-371) writes, "Sacras Scriptures memoriter tenens, post orationes et psalmos quasi Deo praesente recitabat."
10058{_}2435{_}It is said that St. Gelasius (d. 496), St. Ambrose (d. 397), St, Gregory the Great (d. 604) composed collects and corrected existing ones.
10058{_}2436{_}The authorship and the period of composition of many of the Breviary collects are matters of doubt and difficulty.
10058{_}2437{_}Even the date of the introduction of collects into the Divine Office is doubtful.
10058{_}2438{_}In the early Christian Church there seems to have been one and only one prayer, the _Pater Noster_, in liturgical use.
10058{_}2439{_}St. Benedict laid it down in his rule that there should be none other.
10058{_}2440{_}It is generally held by students of liturgy that the collects were originally used in Mass only and were introduced into the Office at a time much later than their introduction into the Mass books.
10058{_}2441{_}In the Masses for Holy Week we see the collects in their oldest existing form.
10058{_}2442{_}The rite of the Mass has been shortened at all other seasons, and there remains now only the greeting, _Oremus_, and the collect itself.
10058{_}2443{_}The _Oremus_ did not refer immediately to the collect, but rather to the silent prayer that went before it.
10058{_}2444{_}This also explains the shortness of the older collects.
10058{_}2445{_}They are not the prayer itself, but its conclusion.
10058{_}2446{_}One short sentence summed up the petitions of the people.
10058{_}2447{_}It is only since the original meaning of the collect has been forgotten that it has become itself a long petition with various references and clauses (compare the collects for the Sundays after Pentecost with those of modern feasts)--(_Cath.
10058{_}2448{_}Encyl._, art.
10058{_}2449{_}"Collects").
10058{_}2450{_}The following examples which are not extreme, may help to make clear and emphatic the matter of the shortness of the old and the length of the new collects.
10058{_}2451{_}"Protector in te sperantium, Deus, sine quo nihil est validum, nihil est sanctum: multiplica super nos misericordiam tuam; ut te rectore, te duce, sic transeamus per bona temporalia, ut non amittamus aeterna.
10058{_}2452{_}Per Dominum."
10058{_}2453{_}_Translation_--"O God, the Protector of all that hope in Thee, without Whom nothing is sure, nothing is holy, bountifully bestow on us, Thy mercy, that Thou being our ruler and our guide, we may so pass through temporal blessings that we lose not the eternal.
10058{_}2454{_}Through our Lord ..." (Collect for third Sunday after Pentecost.)
10058{_}2455{_}"Omnipotens et misericors Deus qui beatam Joannam Franciscam tuo amore succensam admirabili spiritus fortitudine per omnes vitae semitas in via perfectionis donasti, quique per illam illustrare Ecclesiam tuam nova prole voluisti: ejus meritis et precibus concede ut qui infirmitatis nostrae conscii de tua virtute confidimus coelestis gratiae auxilio, cuncta nobis adversantia vicamus.
10058{_}2456{_}Per Dominum ..."  _Translation_-"Almighty and merciful God Who inflaming blessed Jane Frances with love, didst endow her with a marvellous fortitude of spirit to pursue the way of perfection In all the paths of life, and wast pleased through her to enrich Thy Church with a new offspring, grant by her merits and intercession that we, who, knowing our own weakness, trust in Thy strength, may by the help of Thy heavenly grace overcome all things that oppose us.
10058{_}2457{_}Through our Lord" (Collect of St. Jane Frances Fremiot De Chantal, August 21).
10058{_}2458{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2459{_}In Vespers and Lauds the collect is said after the antiphons of the _Magnificat_ and _Benedictus_, unless the _Preces_ (q.v.)
10058{_}2460{_}are to be said in these hours.
10058{_}2461{_}Then the _Preces_ are said after the antiphons, and the collects follow after them immediately.
10058{_}2462{_}The collect of a ferial Office is found in Office of the previous Sunday, except in ferias of Lent and Rogation days which have special and proper collects.
10058{_}2463{_}At Prime and the other Hours the collect is said after the little respond, unless the _Preces_ be recited.
10058{_}2464{_}They precede the collect.
10058{_}2465{_}At Compline the collect is said after the antiphon _Salva nos_ if the _Preces_ be not recited.
10058{_}2466{_}At Prime and Compline the collects of the Psalter are never changed except during the last three days of Holy Week.
10058{_}2467{_}In this triduum, in all hours up to and including None on Holy Saturday the collect is said after the Psalm _Miserere_.
10058{_}2468{_}Before reciting the collect in the Office, everyone in deacon's orders or in priesthood says _Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo_, and this is said even if the Office be said privately.
10058{_}2469{_}All others reciting the Office say _Domine exaudi orationem meam.
10058{_}2470{_}Et clamor meus ad te veniat_.
10058{_}2471{_}Then the word _Oremus_ is prefixed to the recitation of the collect, and at the end, _Amen_ is said.
10058{_}2472{_}If there be only one collect, the _Dominus vobiscum_ or the _Domine exaudi_ with the responses _Et cum spiritu tuo; Et clamor meus ad te veniat_ is repeated after the _Amen_.
10058{_}2473{_}But if there be more than one collect, before each is said its corresponding antiphon and versicle and also the word, _Oremus_.
10058{_}2474{_}After the last collect is said, the _Dominus vobiscum_ and _Et cum spiritu tuo_ are repeated.
10058{_}2475{_}Then we add _Benedicamus Domino; Deo Gratias, Fidelium animae_....
10058{_}2476{_}This latter verse is not a constant sequel to the _Benedicamus_, as we see in Prime, where the verse _Pretiosa_ succeeds it; and again in Compline it is succeeded by _Benedicat et custodiet_.
10058{_}2477{_}The concluding words of the prayers or collects vary.
10058{_}2478{_}If the prayer is addressed to God the Father, the concluding words are _Per Dominum_ (see the collects given above).
10058{_}2479{_}If the prayer be addressed to God the Son, the concluding words are _Qui vivis et regnas_--e.g., Deus qui in tuae caritatis exemplum ad fidelium redemptionem .... Qui vivis et regnas (Collect for St. Peter Nolasco's feast, 3ist January).
10058{_}2480{_}If in the beginning of the prayer mention is made of God the Son, the ending should be _Per eundem, e.g.,_ Domine Deus noster?
10058{_}2481{_}qui, beatae Brigittae per Filium tuurn unigenitum secreta coelestia revelasti; ...
10058{_}2482{_}Per eundem Dominum (collect for feast, 8th October).
10058{_}2483{_}But if the mention of God the Son is made near the end of the collect, the ending is _Qui tecum vivit et regnal, e.g._, "Famulorum tuorum, quaesumus, Domine.... Genitricis Filii tui Domini nostri intercessione salvemur: Qui tecum vivit et regnat" (collect of Assumption, 15th August).
10058{_}2484{_}If the name of the Holy Ghost occur in the prayer, the conclusion is, _In unitate ejusdem Spiritus sancti, e.g._, "Deus, qui hodierna die corda ... in eodem spiritu recta ... _in imitate ejusdem Spiritus_" (collect: for Pentecost Sunday).
10058{_}2485{_}The following lines, giving the rules for terminations, are well known and are useful, as a help to the memory:--       _Per Dominum_ dicas, si Patrem quilibet oras      Si Christum memores, _Per eundem_, dicere debes      Si loqueris Christo, _Qui vivis_ scire memento;      _Qui tecum_, si sit collectae finisin ipso      Si Flamen memores _ejusdem_ die prope finem  When there are several collects an ending or conclusion is added to the first and last only.
10058{_}2486{_}_Dominus vobiscum_ is said before the first collect only, but each collect is preceded by the word _Oremus_, unless in the Office for the Dead.
10058{_}2487{_}_Explanation of the Rubric_.
10058{_}2488{_}Where a feast is transferred either occasionally or always and its collect contains words such as _Hanc diem, hodiernom diem_, it is not allowed to change the wording, without permission of the Congregation of Rites (S.R.C., 7th September, 1916).
10058{_}2489{_}If the collect of a commemoration be of the same form as the prayer of the feast, the former is taken from the common of saints, in proper place.
10058{_}2490{_}_Dominus vobiscum_.
10058{_}2491{_}This salutation is of great antiquity.
10058{_}2492{_}It was the greeting of Booz to his harvestmen (Ruth, ii.
10058{_}2493{_}4).
10058{_}2494{_}The prophet used the selfsame salutation to Azas.
10058{_}2495{_}And the Angel Gabriel expressed the same idea, _Dominns tecum_, to the Blessed Virgin.
10058{_}2496{_}It was blessed and honoured by our Lord Himself, when to His apostles he said "Ecce ego vobiscum sum omnibus diebus" (St. Matt.
10058{_}2497{_}28.
10058{_}2498{_}20).
10058{_}2499{_}This beautiful salutation passed into Church liturgy at an early date, probably in apostolic times.
10058{_}2500{_}Its use in liturgy was mentioned at the Council of Braga (563), and it is found in the Sacramentarium Gelasianum (sixth century).
10058{_}2501{_}These words are called the divine salutation.
10058{_}2502{_}They mean that the priest who utters them is at peace with all clergy and people and thus wishes God to remain with them--the highest and holiest of wishes.
10058{_}2503{_}For the presence of God, Who is the source of every good and the author of every best gift, is a certain pledge of divine protection and of that peace and consolation which the world cannot give.
10058{_}2504{_}This formula is used even in private recitation of the Office, as the priest prays in union with and in the name of the Church.
10058{_}2505{_}The words _Et cum spiritu tuo_ add a new and further significance to the salutation; for it is the spirit, the human soul, that prays, and when the spirit prays in the name of the Church for her children, its work is a work of high spiritual order, demanding the use of all the soul's powers,  _Oremus._ This exhortation is of very great antiquity, and in this form is found in the liturgies of St. James and of St. Mark.
10058{_}2506{_}In those days it was said by the priest in a loud voice.
10058{_}2507{_}The priest, the mediator, following the example of the great Mediator, Christ, calls others to join with him in prayer.
10058{_}2508{_}St. Augustine tells us, that sometimes after pronouncing the word _Oremus_, the priest paused for a while and the people prayed in silence, and then the priest "collected" the united prayers of the congregation and offered them to God, hence the name _collect_ (St. Augustine, Epistle 107), (_cf._ Probst., _Abendl Messe_, p. 126).
10058{_}2509{_}_Invocation and Conclusion_.
10058{_}2510{_}Prayer is addressed generally to God the Father.
10058{_}2511{_}This practice is in accordance with the example and doctrine of Christ, "Father, I give Thee thanks" (St. John, xi, 41); "Amen, amen, I say to you; if you ask the Father anything in My name, he will give it to you" (St. John, xvi.
10058{_}2512{_}23).
10058{_}2513{_}"And He taught us to say 'Our Father.'"
10058{_}2514{_}In the early ages of the Church, seldom was prayer addressed to God, the Son.
10058{_}2515{_}Innocent III.
10058{_}2516{_}tells us that the reason for the practice was a fear that such prayer might lead the catechumens, the Jews or the Pagans converted to Christianity, to allege or to believe that Christians worshipped several Gods.
10058{_}2517{_}However, with the advent of the early heresies, it became necessary to formulate prayers witnessing the divinity of Christ and His equality in all things to the Father and the Holy Ghost.
10058{_}2518{_}In some of the great prayers of the liturgy, the three Persons of the Holy Trinity are named to show their equality and unity of nature and substance.
10058{_}2519{_}Nearly all the prayers of this kind are the products of the Church during the storms of early heresy against the divinity, nature or personality of Christ.
10058{_}2520{_}The conclusions of the prayers generally contain the words _Per Dominum nostrum Jesum Christum_, because all graces come through Jesus Christ, our Lord and Saviour, Who pleads, as Mediator between God and Man, as He Himself has said, "No man cometh to the Father but by Me" (St. John, xiv.
10058{_}2521{_}6).
10058{_}2522{_}Hence, in every collect, we may distinguish five parts: the invocation, the motive, the petition, the purpose, the conclusion.
10058{_}2523{_}(1) The Invocation takes some form such as _Deus, Domine_.
10058{_}2524{_}(2) The motive is commonly introduced by the relative _qui_; e.g., Deus, _qui corda fidelium sancti spiritus illustratione docuisti_.
10058{_}2525{_}(3) The petition, the body or centre or substance of the prayer, is always noted for the solemn simplicity of language, which marks liturgical prayer, e.g., _Multiplica_ super nos misericordiam tuam.
10058{_}2526{_}(4) The purpose is an enforcement of the petition.
10058{_}2527{_}It has reference, generally, to the need of the petitions and is marked usually with the word _ut_.
10058{_}2528{_}"Multiplica super nos misericordiam tuam, _ut_ quae, nobis agendis praecipis, te miserante adimplere possimus" (prayer for feast of St. Patrick).
10058{_}2529{_}(5) The _conclusion_ varies, e.g., "Per Dominum nostrum," "Per eundem Dominum," etc.
10058{_}2530{_}"Those who pay intelligent attention to the liturgical chant at High Mass, and in particular to the chant of the celebrant, will be able to discover for themselves that the intonations used in the singing of the collect and the Post-Communion serve, as a rule, to mark off two at least of the main divisions indicated.
10058{_}2531{_}Two inflections, a greater and a lesser, occur in the body of the prayer, the greater for the most part coming at the close of the 'motive,' while the lessor concludes the 'petition' and produces the purpose of the prayer.
10058{_}2532{_}When the prayers are correctly printed, as in the authentic 'Missale Romanum,' the place of the inflexions is indicated by a colon, 'punctum principals,' and a semicolon, 'semi-punctum,' respectively.
10058{_}2533{_}These steps, it will he observed, indicate, not precisely 'breaks in the sense' (as Haberl incorrectly says) but rather the logical divisions of the sentence, which is not quite the same thing" (Father Lucas, S.J., _Holy Mass_, chap, vi.).
10058{_}2534{_}The question is often asked, why _Dominus vobiscum_ is said after the collect, or prayer.
10058{_}2535{_}Writers on liturgy reply that it is so placed because Christ frequently used the salutation _Pax vobis_, and the priest in public prayer holds the place of Christ, and as he, the priest, used this formula of salvation before the collect to obtain the spirit of prayer and the grace of God, he repeats it so that these gifts may be retained.
10058{_}2536{_}In the collects, the fatherland of the saints is rarely found, because the saints' true home and fatherland is heaven, where they were born again to life eternal, and their fatherland is not this valley of exile where they spent their temporal life.
10058{_}2537{_}Nor are their surnames given in the collects (see the collect of St. Jane Frances Fremiot de Chantel given on p. 180).
10058{_}2538{_}But it is not infrequent in the collects to find certain appellations characterising a saint or noting some special prerogative or wonderful gift of grace.
10058{_}2539{_}The Church's collects record the wonderful gifts of St. John Chrysostom ("the golden-mouthed"), St. Peter Chrysologus ("qui ob auream ejus eloquentiam Chrysologi cognomen adeptus est") (_Rom.
10058{_}2540{_}Brev_.).
10058{_}2541{_}Sometimes the nation or earthly home of a saint is given in a collect to distinguish one saint from another.
10058{_}2542{_}This is seen in the case of saints bearing the name of Mary, which if used absolutely or unqualifiedly refers to the Mother of God.
10058{_}2543{_}See the collects for St. Mary Magdalen, St. Mary of Egypt, etc.
10058{_}2544{_}The collect or prayer is placed at the end of the Hours to collect or gather up the fruits of all the prayers that precede; to beg from God that His grace may follow our actions as it precedes them; that the prayer may be a shield and buckler against all temptations which may be encountered.
10058{_}2545{_}The prayers at Prime and at Compline never vary, to remind us, the old writers tell us, that all our acts should be invariably referred to God.
10058{_}2546{_}In the early ages of the Church, all public prayers, both in Mass and in Office were offered up by both priests and people with outstretched arms.
10058{_}2547{_}This practice is observed still, in a certain way, in Mass.
10058{_}2548{_}_Benedicamus_ is the prayer to thank God for all His graces.
10058{_}2549{_}_Fidelium animae_.
10058{_}2550{_}This prayer is said after every Hour, unless where the hour is said in choir and followed immediately by Mass.
10058{_}2551{_}It Is omitted, too, before the Litany.
10058{_}2552{_}De Precibus (Title XXXIV.).
10058{_}2553{_}These are prayers which are said at some of canonical Hours, before the collect or oratio.
10058{_}2554{_}They commence with Kyrie eleison or Pater Noster.
10058{_}2555{_}They consist of versicles and responses and these differ from other versicles and responses, which are generally historic, e.g., In omnem terram exivit sonus eorum, Amavit eum Dominus et laudavit eum.
10058{_}2556{_}But the versicles and responses of the _preces_ are always a call to God or an exhortation to praise God (e.g., Fiat misericordia tua, Domine), super nos, Quemadmodum speravimus in te (see Prime, infra, page 193).
10058{_}2557{_}These prayers are of great antiquity, mention of them being found in the works of Amalare (ninth century).
10058{_}2558{_}They are said in some Offices in Vespers, Compline, Lauds, Prime and Little Hours.
10058{_}2559{_}Before the reform of the Breviary by Pope Pius X,, the Preces at Vespers contained six short prayers and the Psalm, Miserere.
10058{_}2560{_}In the new Breviary nine short prayers are given in the Preces--the six former prayers being retained and three new ones, Pro Papa; Pro antistite; Pro benefactoribus, being added.
10058{_}2561{_}The Miserere is omitted.
10058{_}2562{_}The same additions were made in Lauds and the Psalm, De Profundis omitted.
10058{_}2563{_}In Prime and the Little Hours, the preces are unchanged standing in the new Breviary as in the old.
10058{_}2564{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2565{_}The Preces are recited in the Office of--  (1) Prime and Compline on certain days;  (2) Lauds, Prime, Terce, Sext, None, Vespers and Compline of certain feasts.
