10058{_}2566{_}The preces feriales at Lauds and Vespers are the same in structure.
10058{_}2567{_}They have the same structure in Terce, Sext, None, but differ in character.
10058{_}2568{_}The preces dominicales at Prime and Compline have a form of their own, additions being made in the preces of Prime when said on a feria.
10058{_}2569{_}1.
10058{_}2570{_}The Preces Feriales are said at Lauds on Ferias of Lent, Advent and Passiontide, Ember days, except Ember day at Pentecost and on Vigils (except on Vigil of Christmas, Epiphany, Ascension, Friday after Ascension and Vigil of Pentecost)--when the Office on those days is of the current feria.
10058{_}2571{_}2.
10058{_}2572{_}At Prime (i) Preces Dominicales are said in all semi-doubles, simples, Ferial Offices.
10058{_}2573{_}(i) They are said at Little Hours if said at Lauds.
10058{_}2574{_}(ii) At Prime, Preces Feriales are said if they have been said at Lauds.
10058{_}2575{_}3.
10058{_}2576{_}At Vespers Preces Feriales are said (1) on ferias of Advent and Lent when office is of feria.
10058{_}2577{_}4.
10058{_}2578{_}At Compline, Preces Dominicales are said on all (i) semi-doubles, (ii) simples, (iii) all Ferias, _unless_ at Vespers a double or an octave was celebrated.
10058{_}2579{_}SOME TEXTS AND INTENTIONS WHICH MAY HELP TOWARDS THE DEVOUT    RECITATION OF LAUDS.
10058{_}2580{_}1.
10058{_}2581{_}"And very early in the morning, the first day of the week, they come to the sepulchre, the sun being now risen."
10058{_}2582{_}They said to one another, "Who shall roll us back the stone from the door of the sepulchre?"
10058{_}2583{_}(St. Mark, xv.).
10058{_}2584{_}2.
10058{_}2585{_}"And looking, they saw the stone rolled back.... And entering the sepulchre they saw a young man sitting on the right side, clothed with a white robe; and they were astonished.
10058{_}2586{_}Who sayeth to them, Be not affrighted; you seek Jesus of Nazareth Who was crucified.
10058{_}2587{_}He is risen, He is not here" (St. Mark, xv.).
10058{_}2588{_}3.
10058{_}2589{_}"Behold Jesus sayeth to her (Magdalen) 'Woman, why weepest thou?'"
10058{_}2590{_}4.
10058{_}2591{_}"Behold Jesus met them (the women) saying to them 'All hail.'"
10058{_}2592{_}(5) "See my hands and feet, that it is I myself, handle and see" (St. Luke, xxiv.).
10058{_}2593{_}6.
10058{_}2594{_}"Bring hither thy hand and put it into My side and be not faithless."
10058{_}2595{_}7.
10058{_}2596{_}"My Lord and my God" (St, John, xx.).
10058{_}2597{_}_General Intentions_.
10058{_}2598{_}The wants of the Church, peace among nations--vocations to the priesthood--Church students--souls in Purgatory.
10058{_}2599{_}_Personal Intentions_.
10058{_}2600{_}A glorious resurrection; fervour in saying the Office; fervour in saying Mass; fervour in priestly work; forgiveness of all sin.
10058{_}2601{_}_Special Intentions_.
10058{_}2602{_}For Catholic Ireland; for the conversion of America; for peace throughout the world.
10058{_}2603{_}PRIME (TITLE XV.).
10058{_}2604{_}_Etymology_.
10058{_}2605{_}The name _Prime_ is derived from the Latin _prima_ because this part of the Office was said at the first hour of the day, 6 a.m., with us, following the old Roman distribution of the day.
10058{_}2606{_}_Origin_.
10058{_}2607{_}It was stated by some writers that this Hour was established by St. Clement and should therefore date from almost apostolic times.
10058{_}2608{_}But modern writers, following the statement of Cassian, date the origin of this Hour from about the year 382.
10058{_}2609{_}It was believed, too, that the monastery indicated by Cassian as the cradle of Prime was the monastery of Bethlehem, St. Jerome's monastery.
10058{_}2610{_}But it was probably established not there, but in a monastery in the neighbourhood, Dair-er-Raociat (convent of the shepherds) or in Seiar-en-Ganheim (enclosure of the sheep).
10058{_}2611{_}Cassian tells us the reason that led to the introduction of this Hour.
10058{_}2612{_}Lauds ended at dawn, and the monks retired to rest.
10058{_}2613{_}As no other choir work called them until Terce, at 9 a.m., some of them were inclined to rest until that hour and to neglect the spiritual reading and manual work laid down by their rule.
10058{_}2614{_}To prevent this prolonged rest, it was decided to introduce a short choir service, the recital of a few psalms, and then the monks went to work until Terce (_Cath.
10058{_}2615{_}Encyclopedia_, "Prime").
10058{_}2616{_}_Contents_.
10058{_}2617{_}Originally the matter for Prime was drawn from Lauds and was a repetition of part of Lauds.
10058{_}2618{_}Prime consists of two parts.
10058{_}2619{_}The first part consists of hymn, psalms, little chapter and collect.
10058{_}2620{_}The prayers and confiteor inserted before the collect and said on certain days are adjuncts.
10058{_}2621{_}The second part contains the Martyrology (when Prime is said in choir) and other prayers peculiar to the Hour.
10058{_}2622{_}"The reason for this divergence may be traced to the fact that Prime is of monastic institution and the second portion, which is said in the chapter house, has reference to monastic customs.
10058{_}2623{_}The Martyrology and Necrology having been read, prayers were said for the dead recommended to the Community, as benefactors, friends, patrons, protectors, etc.
10058{_}2624{_}Then followed a special prayer in preparation for manual labour of the day, and a chapter of the rule was read, on which the Abbot briefly commented or else gave some admonition to the Community.
10058{_}2625{_}This monastic character will be easily recognised by a glance at the formulas used.
10058{_}2626{_}The prayer, 'Sancta Maria et omnes sancti' forms a natural conclusion, to the reading of the Martyrology, The 'Deus in adjutorium,' the 'Pater Noster' with accompanying versicles, and the collect, are the prayers before manual labour: 'Respice,' etc., Look, O Lord, upon Thy servants and upon Thy works... and direct Thou the work of our hands.
10058{_}2627{_}'Dirige et sanctificare,' etc., 'Vouchsafe to direct and sanctify our senses, words and actions,' etc.
10058{_}2628{_}Whilst the 'Dominus nos benedicat' and the 'Fidelium animae' are the conclusion of the prayers for the dead" (Dom Cabrol, Introduction to the _Day Hours of the Church_).
10058{_}2629{_}_Structure_:-i. Pater, Ave, Credo, silently.
10058{_}2630{_}2.
10058{_}2631{_}Deus in adjutorium.
10058{_}2632{_}... Domine ad adjuvandum .. with sign of the cross, Gloria Patri.
10058{_}2633{_}... Sicut erat.
10058{_}2634{_}... 3.
10058{_}2635{_}Hymn, _fam lucis_.
10058{_}2636{_}4.
10058{_}2637{_}Antiphon, first words only.
10058{_}2638{_}5.
10058{_}2639{_}Psalms for the Sunday or feria as rubrics direct, with the Athanasian Creed if it be ordered, then the antiphon in full.
10058{_}2640{_}6.
10058{_}2641{_}Regi saeculorum ... or, Pacem et veritatem.
10058{_}2642{_}... Deo Gratias, Christie, Fili Dei vivi.... 7.
10058{_}2643{_}Preces, if they are ordered in the Office of the Day, Preces Dominicales or Preces feriales as rubrics direct.
10058{_}2644{_}These include versicles, responses, confiteor, misereatur... indulgentiam... versicles responses.
10058{_}2645{_}8.
10058{_}2646{_}Dominus vobiscum.
10058{_}2647{_}Et cum spiritu tuo.
10058{_}2648{_}Oremus, Domine Deus..... Amen.
10058{_}2649{_}Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo.
10058{_}2650{_}9.
10058{_}2651{_}Benedicamus Domino, Deo Gratias.
10058{_}2652{_}10.
10058{_}2653{_}In choir, the martyrology is here read, 11.
10058{_}2654{_}Pretiosa... mors.... 12.
10058{_}2655{_}Sancta Maria et omnes Sancti.... 13.
10058{_}2656{_}Thrice, Deus in adjutorium meum intende, Domine ad adjuvandum... without the sign of cross, Gloria Patri.... Sicut erat.
10058{_}2657{_}14.
10058{_}2658{_}Kyrie Eleison, Christe Eleison, Kyrie Eleison, Pater Noster, qui es in coelis... (in silence).
10058{_}2659{_}Et ne nos inducas in tentationem.
10058{_}2660{_}Sed libera nos a malo.
10058{_}2661{_}15.
10058{_}2662{_}Respice in servos tuos....
10058{_}2663{_}Et sit splendor....16.
10058{_}2664{_}Gloria Patri.... Sicut erat....Oremus, Dirigere et sanctificare.... l7.
10058{_}2665{_}Jube, Domine.... Deus et actus nostros....Amen.
10058{_}2666{_}18.
10058{_}2667{_}Lectio brevis, which in feast offices is the Capitulum from None.
10058{_}2668{_}19.
10058{_}2669{_}Adjutorium nostrum in nomine Domine (with sign of cross on forehead, breast and shoulders); Qui fecit....20.
10058{_}2670{_}Benedicite, Deus; Domine nos benedicat...in pace, Amen.
10058{_}2671{_}To the lectio brevis at Prime, Tu autem Domine, miserere nobis, is added.
10058{_}2672{_}_The Athanasian Creed_.
10058{_}2673{_}In the Roman Breviary prior to the reform of 1911, the title given to the formula of faith was Symbolum S. Athanasi.
10058{_}2674{_}In the new Breviaries the title stands Symbolum Athanasianum.
10058{_}2675{_}Why was the change made?
10058{_}2676{_}During the past two hundred years the authorship of this formula has led to great discussion and its reading has led to much bitter and heated controversy in Anglican and Protestant churches.
10058{_}2677{_}Many contended for its retention in Protestant services and many rejoiced at its partial exclusion, its truncated revision and clamoured for its rejection everywhere from service.
10058{_}2678{_}Controversy led to the study of its origin.
10058{_}2679{_}In 1872 a Protestant author, Ffoulkes, maintained that it was not composed by St. Athanasius (296-373) but by Paulinus of Aquileia (A.D. 800).
10058{_}2680{_}But the literature of the age of Charlemagne proves that this creed had at the beginning of the ninth century an antiquity of at least more than a century (Ommaney, _History and Structure of the Athanasian Creed_, Oxford, 1897).
10058{_}2681{_}Scholars, basing their opinions on words found in the _Expositio Fidei Fortunati_, date the origin of this symbol from the fifth century.
10058{_}2682{_}It contains certain expressions which a writer subsequent to the Council of Chalcedon (451) would have been most unlikely to employ, and omits certain expressions which such a writer would have been most unlikely to omit.
10058{_}2683{_}However, it is likely that the creed dates from the fifth century.
10058{_}2684{_}Who its author was, is quite doubtful.
10058{_}2685{_}It was not St. Athanasius, it may have been St. Hilary of Aries, or St. Vincent of Lerins, or some local bishop in southern France, "But let us only suppose that the real author was some local bishop--or the theologian employed by some local bishop--and that it was composed in the first instance for purely local use in some district of southern France--then does not the difficulty disappear, and are not the facts of its silent and gradual adoption suitably explained?
10058{_}2686{_}Not coming from an author of wide reputation, it would not at first have attracted much attention and would have been used only in the locality of its origin; from there its use would have spread to neighbouring districts; as it got more known it would have been more widely adopted, and the compactness and lucidity of its statements, and the enthusiasm-inspiring character of its style would have contributed to make it highly prized wherever it was known.
10058{_}2687{_}Then would come speculation as to its authorship, and what wonder if in uncritical times an Athanasian authorship was first guessed, then confidently affirmed and believed?"
10058{_}2688{_}(Father Sydney F. Smith, S.J., _The Month_, October, 1904).
10058{_}2689{_}This opinion is only one of several held by Catholic scholars.
10058{_}2690{_}Dom Morin holds strongly, and gives very good reasons for his view, that it was written by Martin of Braga between the years 550 and 580.
10058{_}2691{_}It was written, he says, for the people of Galicia in Spain, who had been recently converted from Arianism (_Journal of Theological Studies_, April, 1911).
10058{_}2692{_}It was adopted into Gallican liturgy and office about 980, and in the Roman office only when the Curial Breviary was adopted.
10058{_}2693{_}"The liturgical use of the Athanasian Creed was Frankish in origin (ninth century) and spread through the influence of the Cluniac reform (tenth century), but only found its way to Rome in the Supplementary prayers in the twelfth and thirteenth century" (Burton and Myers, _op.
10058{_}2694{_}cit_., p. 51).
10058{_}2695{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2696{_}Athanasian Creed, to be said (1) Trinity Sunday, (2) Sundays after Epiphany, (3) Sundays after Pentecost unless there be in (2) and (3) the commemoration of a double, or of an octave.
10058{_}2697{_}Why is prayer offered at this first hour of the day?
10058{_}2698{_}Writers on liturgy answer, 1st to offer to God the first fruits of our day, of our work, of our devotion, following in this the example of Christ, Who from His first entry into the world offered Himself to His Father for the salvation of mankind.
10058{_}2699{_}2d To beg of Him to keep us safe during the day, 3d To beg of Him to keep us free from sin, "ut in diurnis actibus nos servet a nocentibus."
10058{_}2700{_}"May God in all our words and deeds       Keep us from harm this day.
10058{_}2701{_}May He in love retain us still,       From tones of strife and words of ill,       And wrap around and close our eyes       To earth's absorbing vanities.
10058{_}2702{_}May wrath and thoughts that gender shame       Ne'er in our breasts abide.
10058{_}2703{_}And painful abstinences tame       Of wanton flesh, the pride" (Hymn at Prime).
10058{_}2704{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}2705{_}The Office of Prime begins in choir with the silent recitation of _Pater Noster, Ave, Credo_.
10058{_}2706{_}Then, if in choir (aloud) Deus in adjutorium.
10058{_}2707{_}... Domine ad adjirvandum.
10058{_}2708{_}... Gloria Patri.... Alleluia, or Laus tibi.... Then the hymn _fam lucis_ is said.
10058{_}2709{_}The antiphon for the day is said as far as the asterisk (*), then the Psalms of the day's Office as arranged in the new Pian Psaltery, according to the day of the week, except on some special feasts, when the Psalms at Prime are the Sunday psalms.
10058{_}2710{_}When the _ordo recitandi_ marks an Office as _officium solemne_ (an excepted feast), the psalms at Lauds and Hours are the Sunday psalms; and at Prime the psalm _Deus in nomine tuo_ (Psalm 53) takes the place of Psalm _Confitemini_ (Psalm 117).
10058{_}2711{_}At Prime, and at the small Hours, Terce, Sext, None, only one antiphon is said.
10058{_}2712{_}It is said in full at the end of the last Psalm in each Hour.
10058{_}2713{_}The Capitulum, the little Responsory, _Christe_, _Fili Dei vivi_ ... is then said.
10058{_}2714{_}In this responsory the versicle _Qui sedes ad dexteram Patris_ is sometimes changed, e.g., in paschal time it is, _Qui surrexisti a mortuis_.
10058{_}2715{_}The manner of reciting this responsory is sometimes not correctly understood, owing, perhaps, to its printed form in some Breviaries.
10058{_}2716{_}The normal method is to repeat the _whole_ response, then say the versicle, and then the second portion of the response; then the _Gloria Patri el Filio et Spiritui Sancto, without the Sicut erat_, is said, and the response repeated.
10058{_}2717{_}The versicle _Exsurge_ and the response _Et libera_ are then said.
10058{_}2718{_}This is the method of recitation in all the small Hours and at Compline.
10058{_}2719{_}After this responsory, if the Office be of double rite or be an Office within an octave, or on the vigil of Epiphany or on Friday or Saturday after Ascension, or on a Sunday on which a double is commemorated, or an octave is celebrated, or on a semi-double feast within an octave, _Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo_, and the prayer _Dominus Deus omnipotens_ is said.
10058{_}2720{_}But if the Office be not any of these mentioned just now, the responsory is followed by the _Preces_.
10058{_}2721{_}_Preces_ (Title XXXIV.)
10058{_}2722{_}In the Breviary there are two sets of preces, the Preces Dominicales for Sunday and the Preces Feriales for ferial Offices.
10058{_}2723{_}These ferial preces of Prime differ from the ferial preces of Lauds, and are said in Prime when the ferial preces are said in Lauds, That is, on the ferias of Advent, Lent, Passiontide, Ember days and Vigils.
10058{_}2724{_}The ferial preces of Lauds are found in the Breviary, immediately after the second set of Psalms for ferial Lauds and after the short responsory in the psalm arrangements for the days of the week.
10058{_}2725{_}(See Lauds, _supra_, p.
10058{_}2726{_}188.)
10058{_}2727{_}These prayers were introduced at a very early stage of Christian liturgy.
10058{_}2728{_}St. Isidore writes that they come from Greek liturgy and the opening words _Kyrie eleison_ seem to indicate remnants of an old litany.
10058{_}2729{_}Formerly they were read oftener during the liturgical year than we now are called on to repeat them.
10058{_}2730{_}They are sometimes referred to as the _preces flebiles_, tearful prayers, because they are said in times of penance, and are formed to excite tears.
10058{_}2731{_}In choir recitation they are said kneeling.
10058{_}2732{_}When the preces or the preces feriales are said the sign of the cross is made from the forehead to the breast, at the words _Adjutorium nostrum in nomine Domini_.
10058{_}2733{_}Then the Confiteor is said.
10058{_}2734{_}The Confiteor was from an early date a prayer said privately as a preparation for Mass.
10058{_}2735{_}It is found in several forms; _Confiteor Deo, beatae Mariae, omnibus sanctis et vobis_ (Sarum Missal), but since the time of St. Pius V. (1566-1572) our present form alone was followed and allowed (S. R. C., 13th February, 1666).
10058{_}2736{_}If the Office be recited privately or with one or two companions, the _confiteor_ is said once only and simultaneously in the preces, and the words _vobis fratribus_ and _vos fratres_, which priests say in the opening prayer of Mass are omitted.
10058{_}2737{_}It should be remarked, too, that the _Misereatur_ and _Indulgentiam_ have not in this location _vestri, vestris, vos,_ but _nostri, nostris, nos_.
10058{_}2738{_}Sometimes errors in this part of the recitation of the Office are unnoticed, and this pronoun error makes the formula meaningless.
10058{_}2739{_}After the _Indulgentiam_ come the concluding versicles of the preces, Dignare ... sine peccato ... miserere ... miserere ... Fiat ... Quemadmodum ... Domine ...
10058{_}2740{_}Et ... Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo, and the prayer _Domine Deus Omnipotens_ ... Amen.
10058{_}2741{_}... Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo.
10058{_}2742{_}... Benedicamus Domino, Deo gratias.
10058{_}2743{_}If the Office be said in choir, the martyrology is read at this part of Prime.
10058{_}2744{_}The reading of the martyrology is not of obligation in private recitation of the Office; but the reading of it was highly recommended, even in private recitation, by Pope Gregory XIII.
10058{_}2745{_}(14th January, 1584; see his words in the beginning of the Martyrology).
10058{_}2746{_}Then are said, Pretiosa ... mors ... sancta Maria ... Deus in adjutorium... Domine ad adjuvandum (both the latter being repeated thrice) ... Gloria Patri ... Sicut erat ... Kyrie eleison ... Christe eleison ... Kyrie eleison ... Pater Noster (silently) until words "Et ne nos" ... Sed libera ... Respice ...
10058{_}2747{_}Et sit ... Gloria Patri ... Sicut erat ... Oremus, Dirigere et ... Amen, Jube Domine ...
10058{_}2748{_}Dies et actus ... Amen.
10058{_}2749{_}The short lesson which, on all feasts, is the same as the chapter which is said at None will be found in the proper or common, under that Hour, The new Psalter and new rubrics made no change in this matter.
10058{_}2750{_}Hence, for example, on the feast of SS.
10058{_}2751{_}Peter and Paul the short lesson at end of Prime is taken from None of the feast, "Et Petrus ad se reversus"; the short lesson for Prime on the feast of St. Aloysius is "Lex Dei ejus" and not the short lesson printed in the Psalter under the day's Office.
10058{_}2752{_}On all Sundays and week days it varies according to the season.
10058{_}2753{_}Thus--  1.
10058{_}2754{_}From the 14th January until the first Saturday in Lent, from Monday to Wednesday in Trinity week, from the Friday after the octave of Corpus Christi until the Saturday before Advent, the short lesson is "Dominus autem" (II.
10058{_}2755{_}Thess.
10058{_}2756{_}iii.
10058{_}2757{_}),  2.
10058{_}2758{_}From the first Sunday of Advent until the 23rd December inclusive it is "Domine miserere" (Isaias xxxiii,).
10058{_}2759{_}3.
10058{_}2760{_}From the first Sunday of Lent until the Saturday before Passion Sunday inclusive it is "Quaerite Dominum" (Isaias iv.).
10058{_}2761{_}4.
10058{_}2762{_}From Passion Sunday until Wednesday in Holy Week it is "Faciem meam" (Isaias, 1.
10058{_}2763{_}),  5.
10058{_}2764{_}From Easter Sunday to the Vigil of Ascension inclusive, the short lesson is "Si consurrexists" (Coloss.
10058{_}2765{_}iii.).
10058{_}2766{_}At the end of the short lesson the words "Tu autem Domine, miserere nobis; Deo gratias" are added, and after these words are said "Adjutorium nostrum ... Qui fecit ... Benedicite Deus" and the Blessing, "Dominus nos benedicat ... requiescant in pace, Amen."
10058{_}2767{_}Then _Pater Noster_ is said silently, unless another Hour is to follow immediately.
10058{_}2768{_}TEXTS AND INTENTIONS FOR PIOUS RECITATION OF PRIME.
10058{_}2769{_}1.
10058{_}2770{_}"Herod and his army set him at nought" (St. Luke, c. 25).
10058{_}2771{_}2.
10058{_}2772{_}"Not this man, but Barrabas.
10058{_}2773{_}Crucify Him."
10058{_}2774{_}3.
10058{_}2775{_}"I find no cause in Him.
10058{_}2776{_}I will chastise Him and let Him go" (St. Luke).
10058{_}2777{_}4.
10058{_}2778{_}"But Jesus he delivered up to their will" (St. Luke, c. 23).
10058{_}2779{_}5.
10058{_}2780{_}"Shall I crucify your King?," (St. John, 19).
10058{_}2781{_}_General Intentions_.
10058{_}2782{_}The Pope and his intentions; the propagation of the Faith; the priesthood; the Catholic laity; Catholic Missions in the East; Catholic Europe.
10058{_}2783{_}_Personal Intentions_.
10058{_}2784{_}The spirit of meekness and humility; greater devotion to the Eucharist; greater love of the Blessed Virgin; the priestly vows.
10058{_}2785{_}_Special Intentions_.
10058{_}2786{_}For our friends; for the sick and sorrowful; for the Church in Scotland; for our enemies; for the priesthood of America.
10058{_}2787{_}CHAPTER III.
10058{_}2788{_}TERCE, SEXT, NONE (TITLE XVI.).
10058{_}2789{_}TERCE.
10058{_}2790{_}_Etymology._ The word Terce comes from the Latin word _tertia (hora)_, third.
10058{_}2791{_}Because this little Hour was said at the third hour of the Roman day, that is, about 9 o'clock in the forenoon,  _Structure._ It consists of Pater Noster, Ave, Deus in adjutorium, Gloria Patri ... Sicut erat ... Amen, Alleluia, Hymn, opening words of the antiphon, the three psalms, antiphon in full, capitulum, response, Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo, Oremus, collect, Dominus vobiscum, Et cum spiritu tuo, Benedicamus ... Deo gratias, Fidelium animae.... Amen.
10058{_}2792{_}And Pater Noster is said silently if another Hour is not begun immediately.
10058{_}2793{_}Terce is called the golden Hour, _hora aurea_, because at this time of the day, the third Hour, the Holy Ghost, who is typified by gold, descended on the apostles.
10058{_}2794{_}It is called sometimes the sacred Hour (_hora sacra_) because in conventional churches it is recited immediately before Holy Mass.
10058{_}2795{_}It is the most solemn of all the small Hours.
10058{_}2796{_}_Antiquity._ The custom of praying at these three hours, terce, sext and none, is very ancient.
10058{_}2797{_}It was in use amongst the devout Jews, and the early converts to Christianity retained the practice.
10058{_}2798{_}The Apostolic Constitutions contain the words "Preces etiam vestras facite hora tertia."
10058{_}2799{_}Why does the Church wish us to pray at the third hour?
10058{_}2800{_}The question is asked by liturgists of olden times.
10058{_}2801{_}Their replies are:--  1. to remind us of the hour when our Saviour was condemned (St. Mark, c. 15).
10058{_}2802{_}2. to remind us of the hour at which the Holy Ghost descended on the Church.
10058{_}2803{_}3. as the Church's hymn tells us that at this hour of the day when men are engrossed in worldly affairs, they especially need God's help,       "Come, Holy Ghost, Who ever One,       Reignest with Father and with Son.
10058{_}2804{_}It is the hour, our souls possess       With Thy full flood of holiness.
10058{_}2805{_}Let flesh and heart and lips and mind       Sound forth our witness to mankind.
10058{_}2806{_}And love light up our mortal frame       Till others catch the living flame,       Now to the Father, to the Son,       And to the Spirit, Three in One,       Be praise and thanks and glory given,       By men on earth, by saints in heaven.
10058{_}2807{_}Amen."
10058{_}2808{_}(Translation by Cardinal Newman of St. Ambrose's         hymn, _Nunc sancte_).
10058{_}2809{_}TEXTS AND INTENTIONS FOR PIOUS RECITATION OF TERCE.
10058{_}2810{_}1.
10058{_}2811{_}"Therefore, Pilate took Jesus and scourged Him."
10058{_}2812{_}2.
10058{_}2813{_}"And the soldiers plaiting a crown of thorns put it on His head; and they put on Him a purple garment."
10058{_}2814{_}3.
10058{_}2815{_}"And they came to Him and said, 'Hail, King of the Jews,' and they gave Him blows" (St. John).
10058{_}2816{_}4.
10058{_}2817{_}"Jesus, therefore, came forth bearing the crown of thorns and the purple garment, and he (Pilate) sayeth to them 'Behold the Man!'"
10058{_}2818{_}_General Intentions._ The Pope's Intentions; the conversion of heretics; the conversion of the Jews.
10058{_}2819{_}_Personal Intentions._ Devotion to the Holy Ghost; devotion to the Passion.
10058{_}2820{_}_Special Intentions._ Vocations in America and Australia; for the Irish people throughout the world; for the souls of our deceased penitents.
10058{_}2821{_}SEXT.
10058{_}2822{_}_Etymology_.
10058{_}2823{_}The word Sext comes from the Latin word _sexta, (hora)_, the sixth hour, because the little Hour should be said at what was the sixth hour of the Roman day, about mid-day with us.
10058{_}2824{_}_Structure._ The structure of this hour is similar to that given in Terce above, the hymn, antiphon, psalms, little chapter and responses differing, but the order and form being similar in both.
10058{_}2825{_}_Antiquity._ The Psalmist wrote, "Vespere et mane et meridie narrabo et annuntiabo, et exaudiet vocem meam" (Ps.
10058{_}2826{_}54).
10058{_}2827{_}This practice of devout Jews was maintained by the early Christians and in the Acts of the Apostles we read, "Ascendit Petrus in superiora ut oraret circam horam sextam" (Acts x, 9).
10058{_}2828{_}At this hour, the Christians met for public, joint prayer.
10058{_}2829{_}Why does the Church wish us to pray at the sixth hour of the day?
10058{_}2830{_}1.
10058{_}2831{_}Because at this hour Christ instructed the Samaritan woman, the type of the Gentiles; and He promised to give the living water, springing up unto life everlasting, which was His blood, poured out on Calvary at the sixth hour.
10058{_}2832{_}2.
10058{_}2833{_}Because at this sixth hour Christ was raised on the cross for our salvation and it is right and just, daily, to remember Him and His great love for us.
10058{_}2834{_}Besides, it is to realise His words "And if I be lifted up from the earth, I will draw all things to myself" (St. John xii.
10058{_}2835{_}32).
10058{_}2836{_}And the Church, in the opening words of Sext for Sunday, impresses this idea on us "Deficit in salutare meum anima mea," "My soul hath fainted after thy salvation" (Ps.
10058{_}2837{_}118).
10058{_}2838{_}3.
10058{_}2839{_}To ask God to grant us health and peace of heart, as the hymn for Sext sings:--       "O God, Who canst not change nor fail,       Guiding the hours as they go by,       Brightening with beam the morning pale,       And burning in the midnight sky,       Quench Thou the fires of hate and strife,       The wasting fever of the heart;       From perils guard our feeble life,       And to our souls Thy grace impart.
10058{_}2840{_}Grant this, O Father, only Son,       And Holy Ghost, God of Grace,       To whom all glory, Three in One,       Be given in every time and place--Amen."
10058{_}2841{_}(Translation by Cardinal Newman of St. Ambrose's        hymn, _Rector potens_).
10058{_}2842{_}TEXTS AND INTENTIONS FOR THE PIOUS RECITATION OF SEXT.
10058{_}2843{_}1.
10058{_}2844{_}"And they took Jesus, and after they had mocked Him, they took off the purple from Him and put His own garments on Him and led Him out to crucify Him" (St. Mark, c. 15).
10058{_}2845{_}2.
10058{_}2846{_}"Bearing His own cross, Jesus went forth to that place called Calvary."
10058{_}2847{_}3.
10058{_}2848{_}"Daughters of Jerusalem, weep not for Me, but for yourselves."
10058{_}2849{_}_General Intentions._ The wants of the Church; for peace and goodwill amongst all States and peoples; for the Pope; for Church students.
10058{_}2850{_}_Personal Intentions._ For patience; for fraternal charity; for the love of the practice of mortification.
10058{_}2851{_}_Special Intentions._ For Catholic schools; for increase in number of daily communicants; for the success of catechists and their work.
10058{_}2852{_}NONE.
10058{_}2853{_}_Etymology._ The word _None_ comes from the Latin word _nona_, ninth (_hora nona_), because this part of the Office was said at the ninth hour of the Roman day, that is, about three o'clock in our modern day.
10058{_}2854{_}_Antiquity._ This hour was set apart in Apostolic times for joint prayer, "Now Peter and John went up into the Temple at the ninth hour of prayer" (Acts iii.
10058{_}2855{_}1).
10058{_}2856{_}_Structure._ See note under this head at Terce.
10058{_}2857{_}Why does the Church desire prayer at the ninth hour?
10058{_}2858{_}1.
10058{_}2859{_}In this she follows the example of her Founder, Christ, Who prayed at the ninth hour.
10058{_}2860{_}"At the ninth hour, Jesus cried out with a loud voice, saying 'Eloi, Eloi, lamma sabacthani?'
10058{_}2861{_}which is, being interpreted, 'My God, my God, why hast Thou forsaken me?'"
10058{_}2862{_}(St. Mark xv.
10058{_}2863{_}34).
10058{_}2864{_}2.
10058{_}2865{_}That ninth hour was the long-wished-for and long-watched-for hour when reconciliation between earth and heaven was complete.
10058{_}2866{_}3.
10058{_}2867{_}To beg from God light and grace, especially towards the end of life, for the day's decline in the afternoon is a figure of the waning of spiritual and corporal life.
10058{_}2868{_}The hymn for None expresses this:--       "O God, unchangeable and true,       Of all the light and power,       Dispensing light in silence through       Each successive hour;       Lord, brighten our declining day,       That it may never wane       Till death, when all things round decay,       Brings back the morn again.
10058{_}2869{_}This grace on Thy redeemed confer,       Father, Co-equal Son,       And Holy Ghost, the Comforter,       Eternal Three in One--Amen."
10058{_}2870{_}(St. Ambrose's hymn, translated by Cardinal        Newman).
10058{_}2871{_}TEXTS AND INTENTIONS TO AID THE PIOUS RECITATION OF NONE.
10058{_}2872{_}1.
10058{_}2873{_}"Come down from the cross" (St. Matthew, c. 27).
10058{_}2874{_}2.
10058{_}2875{_}"Lord, remember me when Thou shalt come into Thy Kingdom" (St. Matthew, c. 23).
10058{_}2876{_}3.
10058{_}2877{_}"My God, my God, why has Thou forsaken me?"
10058{_}2878{_}(St. Matthew, c. 27).
10058{_}2879{_}_General Intentions_.
10058{_}2880{_}All the intentions of the Sacred Heart; the conversion of Britain; the Church in America.
10058{_}2881{_}_Personal Intentions_.
10058{_}2882{_}Fervour in preparation for Mass; fervour in thanksgiving after Mass; fidelity to professional duties and studies.
10058{_}2883{_}_Special Intentions_.
10058{_}2884{_}The temporal welfare of Ireland; to beg a blessing on her priests; to beg a blessing on her Church students; to beg a blessing on her Catholic laity; to beg a blessing on her elementary schools.
10058{_}2885{_}CHAPTER IV.
10058{_}2886{_}VESPERS AND COMPLINE.
10058{_}2887{_}_Etymology_.
10058{_}2888{_}The word _vespers_ comes directly from the Latin _Vesper_; _Vespera_ or _Espera_ was a name given to the star Venus, which rising in the evening was a call to prayer.
10058{_}2889{_}This Hour is recited after None and before Compline.
10058{_}2890{_}In structure, it resembles Lauds, Pater Noster, Ave, Gloria, Five Psalms with antiphons, Capitulum, Hymn, Versicle, antiphon, Magnificat, antiphon and collect.
10058{_}2891{_}It had several synonymous names.
10058{_}2892{_}It was called _Duodecima Hora_ (Antiphonary of Bangor), because it was said at the twelfth hour of the day, six o'clock, or, perhaps, the name came from the twelve psalms which made up the Hour in some churches.
10058{_}2893{_}It was known, too, by the names _Lucernarium, hora lucernalis_, the hour of the candles; because at this hour a number of candles were lighted, not only to shed light but for symbolic purposes.
10058{_}2894{_}It was sometimes referred to as _hora incensi_, from the custom of burning incense at this evening service, and sometimes it is called _gratiarum actio_ (St. Isidore), because it gives thanks to God for the graces given during the day.
10058{_}2895{_}It came to mean not the evening Hour, but the sunset Hour.
10058{_}2896{_}And in the sixth century it was celebrated before daylight had gone and before there was any need for artificial light.
10058{_}2897{_}In the fourth century it was recited by torchlight.
10058{_}2898{_}_Antiquity_.
10058{_}2899{_}The Jews honoured God by special and solemn evening service.
10058{_}2900{_}Their feasts by God's command began in the evening.
10058{_}2901{_}"From evening unto evening you shall celebrate your sabbaths" (Lev.
10058{_}2902{_}xxiii, 32).
10058{_}2903{_}And David sang "Evening and morning and at noon I will speak and declare" (Psalm 54:32).
10058{_}2904{_}The eariy Christians faithfully followed the practice.
10058{_}2905{_}"In the sixth century, the order of Psalms, etc., in Vespers differed little from the Vespers in our modern Breviaries.
10058{_}2906{_}Long before the sixth century there were evening Offices in various forms.
10058{_}2907{_}Its existence in the fourth century is also confirmed by St. Augustine, St. Ambrose, St.
10058{_}2908{_}Basil, St. Ephraem ... Before the fourth century we find allusions to the evening prayer in the early Fathers, Clement I. of Rome, St. Ignatius, St. Clement of Alexandria, Tertullian, Origen, the Canons of St. Hippolytus, St. Cyprian (for texts see Baumer-Biron; 1. c.t.
10058{_}2909{_}20 seq.
10058{_}2910{_}73-74, 76, 78)"--(Dorn Cabrol, _Cath.
10058{_}2911{_}Ency._, art "Vespers").
10058{_}2912{_}Why do we offer up public prayer in the evening?
10058{_}2913{_}The old liturgists reply:--  1.
10058{_}2914{_}To imitate the devout Christians of apostolic times.
10058{_}2915{_}2.
10058{_}2916{_}To honour Jesus, the true Sun of the world, Who hid Himself at His Incarnation, and in His life, and Whose glory was hidden in His Passion.
10058{_}2917{_}3.
10058{_}2918{_}To thank Christ for the Eucharist, which He instituted in the evening of His earthly life, ... "and they prepared the Pasch.
10058{_}2919{_}But when it was evening (vespere autem) He sat down with His twelve disciples" (St. Matthew, xxvi.
10058{_}2920{_}20).
10058{_}2921{_}At this vesper meeting He gave to priests the power to offer the sacrifice of the Mass, to change bread and wine into His body and blood.
10058{_}2922{_}At this vesper service, too, Christ and His apostles celebrated the divine praises, "Hymno dicto" (St. Matthew xxvi.
10058{_}2923{_}30).
10058{_}2924{_}4.
10058{_}2925{_}In the evening our Lord's body was taken down from the cross.
10058{_}2926{_}5.
10058{_}2927{_}At the approach of evening Christ appeared to His disciples at Emmaus and revealed to them His divinity.
10058{_}2928{_}"Stay with us because it is towards evening (_advesperascit_) and He went in with them.
10058{_}2929{_}He took bread and blessed and brake and gave it to them and their eyes were opened and they knew Him" (St. Luke xxiv.
10058{_}2930{_}29-30).
10058{_}2931{_}At Vespers we thank God for the Eucharist.
10058{_}2932{_}The hymns at Vespers date for the most part from the sixth century.
10058{_}2933{_}They are of great beauty and have the peculiar characteristic of telling of the days of creation.
10058{_}2934{_}Thus St. Gregory's (?)
10058{_}2935{_}fine hymn, _Lucis Creator optime_, in Sunday's Vespers, refers to the creation of light; Monday's hymn, _Immense coeli Creator_, refers to the separation of land and water; Wednesday's hymn (written probably by St. Ambrose), _Coeli Deus sanctissime_, refers to the creation of the sun and moon; the hymns for Thursday's vespers, _Magnae Deus potentiae_, refers to the creation of fish and birds; Friday's hymn, _Hominis superne conditor_ (St. Gregory), refers to the creation of the beasts of the earth; Saturday's hymn (St. Ambrose) is an exception, as it refers to the Trinity.
10058{_}2936{_}All these hymns have been beautifully translated into English and the text and translations repay study.
10058{_}2937{_}Sunday's hymn, _Lucis Creator optime_, stands thus in translation:--       "O blest Creator of the light,       Who makest the day with radiance bright,       And o'er the forming world didst call       The light from chaos first of all.
10058{_}2938{_}Whose wisdom joined in sweet array       The morn and eve and named them day,       Night comes with all its darkening fears;       Regard Thy people's prayers and tears,        Lest sunk in sin, and whelmed with strife,       They lose the gift of endless life;       While thinking--but the thoughts of time,       They weave new chains of woe and crime.
10058{_}2939{_}But grant them grace that they may strain       The heavenly gate and prize to gain;       Each harmful lure aside to cast,       And purge away each error past.
10058{_}2940{_}O Father, that we ask be done,       Through Jesus Christ, Thine only Son;       Who, with the Holy Ghost and Thee,       Doth live and reign eternally.
10058{_}2941{_}Amen."
10058{_}2942{_}(Translation by Dr. J.M.
10058{_}2943{_}Neale).
10058{_}2944{_}_Structure._ Vespers, in structure, resembles Lauds and consists of five Psalms.
10058{_}2945{_}It begins with Pater Noster, Ave (said silently), Deus in adjutorium,... Domine ad adjuvandum.... Gloria Patri.... Sicut erat.
10058{_}2946{_}Alleluia or Laus tibi.... Antiphon begun only if the feast be not double; if feast be a double the antiphon is said in full before and after each psalm.
10058{_}2947{_}If feast be a semi-double or simple the antiphon is intoned at the beginning and is said in full at end of each psalm and then only.
10058{_}2948{_}Then are said Capitulum, Deo gratias, Hymn, versicle and response, antiphon to Magnificat, the canticle Magnificat, Gloria Patri.... Sicut erat.... Dominus vobiscum....
10058{_}2949{_}Et cum spiritu tuo, Oremus, collect, commemoration if any made by versicle and response and antiphon of Magnificat proper to commemoration with collect, Dominus vobiscum, Et cum.... Benedicamus Domino; Deo gratias, Fidelium animae.... Amen.
10058{_}2950{_}If Compline be not said immediately after Vespers, Pater Noster is added.
10058{_}2951{_}At the opening words of the _Magnificat_, _Nunc Dimittis_ and _Benedictus_, it is a practice with many priests to make the sign of the cross from forehead to breast, as at _Deus in adjutorium_ (_cf._ Ceremoniale Epis.
10058{_}2952{_}lib.
10058{_}2953{_}II.
10058{_}2954{_}i.
10058{_}2955{_}14).
10058{_}2956{_}This custom, where it exists, should be preserved (S.R.C., April, 1867).
10058{_}2957{_}Writers on liturgy tell us that the number of Psalms in Vespers have a symbolic meaning, typifying the five wounds of the Saviour, the last of which, the wound in the side, was inflicted on the evening of Good Friday, and the others, as the Church says in the hymn _Vergente mundi vespere_, at the waning of the day of the Old Law, before the dawn of salvation (Honorius of Autun, circa 1130).
10058{_}2958{_}Other writers say that these five psalms should produce acts of contrition for the sins committed during the day, by the five senses; and that they should be for us, morally, what the five lighted lamps were for the wise virgins in the Gospel parable (Amalare of Metz, circa 850).
10058{_}2959{_}_Magnificat._ Author.
10058{_}2960{_}The Blessed Virgin Mary is the author of this canticle.
10058{_}2961{_}"The witness of the codices and of the Fathers is practically unanimous for the Vulgate reading: 'Et ait Maria,' but apart from this, the attribution of the _Magnificat_ to Elizabeth would in St. Luke's context be highly abnormal" (Dr. H. T. Henry, _Cath.
10058{_}2962{_}Encyc_., word, _Magnificat_)--The Roman Breviary entitles it _Canticum Beatae Marine Virginis_.
10058{_}2963{_}It is divided by commentators into three parts (St. Luke 1, vv.
10058{_}2964{_}46-49; 50-53; 54-55).
10058{_}2965{_}It "is in many places very similar in thought and phrase to the Canticle of Anna (I. Kings ii.
10058{_}2966{_}1-10) and to various psalms (Ps.
10058{_}2967{_}33, vv.
10058{_}2968{_}3-4; Ps.
10058{_}2969{_}39, v. 9; Ps.
10058{_}2970{_}70, v. 9; Ps.
10058{_}2971{_}125, vv.
10058{_}2972{_}2-3; Ps.
10058{_}2973{_}110, v. 9; Ps.
10058{_}2974{_}97, v. 1; Ps.
10058{_}2975{_}117, v. 16; Ps.
10058{_}2976{_}32, v. 10; Ps.
10058{_}2977{_}92, v. 7; Ps.
10058{_}2978{_}33, v. 11; Ps.
10058{_}2979{_}97, v-3; Ps.
10058{_}2980{_}131, v. 11).
10058{_}2981{_}Similarities are found in Hab.
10058{_}2982{_}c. III.
10058{_}2983{_}v. 18; Mal.
10058{_}2984{_}c. III.
10058{_}2985{_}v. 12; Job.
10058{_}2986{_}c. 5, v. 11; Is, c. 41, v. 8; Is.
10058{_}2987{_}c. 149, v. 3, and Gen. c. 17, v. 19.
10058{_}2988{_}Steeped thus in scriptural thought and Phraseology, summing up in its inspired ecstasy the economy of God with His chosen people, indicating the fulfilment of olden prophecy, and prophesying anew until end of time, the Magnificat is the crown of the Old Testament singing, the last canticle of the Old and the first of the New Testament.
10058{_}2989{_}It is an ecstasy of praise for the inestimable favour bestowed by God on the Virgin, for the mercies shown to Israel, and for the fulfilment of the promises made to Abraham and the patriarchs" (Dr. Henry, _loc.
10058{_}2990{_}cit_.).
10058{_}2991{_}It is found universally in the ancient liturgies and affords a proof of the apostolic and universal praise of the Blessed Virgin.
10058{_}2992{_}Durandus (thirteenth century) gives some reasons for the assignment of the Magnificat to Vespers.
10058{_}2993{_}Because Vespers is the grandest liturgical Hour; because Mary probably arrived at the house of Elizabeth in the evening; because it was in the moral evening of the world that Mary consented to be the Mother of God; because she is the star of the sea, etc.
10058{_}2994{_}The following interesting reason for the use of the Magnificat at Vespers is given by St. Bede (works 5, 306).
10058{_}2995{_}"It comes to pass, by the bounty of the Lord, that if we were at all times to meditate upon the acts and sayings of the Blessed Virgin, the observance of chastity and the works of virtue will always continue with us.
10058{_}2996{_}For, the excellent and salutary custom has grown up in Holy Church that all shall sing her hymn (the Magnificat) every day with the Vesper Psalms, in order that the recalling of the Lord's incarnation, by this means, may the oftener incite the souls of the faithful to devotion and that the consideration of the example set by His Mother may confirm them in the stability of virtue.
10058{_}2997{_}And it is meet that this should be done at Vespers, so that the mind wearied in the course of the day, and distracted by various opinions, may, at the approach of the season of quiet, collect itself in oneness of meditation and through the wholesome reminder may hasten to cleanse itself, by the prayers and tears of the night, from everything useless or harmful which it had contracted by the business of the day."
10058{_}2998{_}_Suffrages of the Saints_.
10058{_}2999{_}(Title XXXV.)
10058{_}3000{_}In Sec.2 of rubrics of the new Breviary we read, "Deinceps, quando facienda erunt suffragia sanctorum, unum fiet suffragium, juxta formulam propositam in Ordinario novi Psalterii."
10058{_}3001{_}Thus were abolished the old formulae of suffrages and a new one inserted.
10058{_}3002{_}Antiphon Beata Dei Genitrix.... V. Mitificavit .... R. Et exaudivit.... Oremus, A cunctis....
10058{_}3003{_}This will be said at Lauds and Vespers outside Paschal time (1) on all Sundays and ferias, (2) on semi-doubles and simples, except (_a_) in Advent and Passiontide, (_b_) when there is a commemoration of a double, a day within an octave.
10058{_}3004{_}In Paschal time the Commemoration is of the Cross.
10058{_}3005{_}In this prayer the names of the Holy Angels and of St. John the Baptist, if they be titulars, are inserted before the name of St. Joseph.
10058{_}3006{_}At the letter N. in the prayer, the name of the titular saint of the particular church should be inserted; but churches dedicated by the title of a mystery (e.g., the Ascension) are not to be named in this prayer (S.R.C., March, 1912).
10058{_}3007{_}TEXTS AND INTENTIONS TO AID THE PIOUS RECITATION OF VESPERS.
10058{_}3008{_}1.
10058{_}3009{_}"Woman, behold thy Son; Behold Thy mother" (St. John, c. 19),  2.
10058{_}3010{_}"I thirst" (St. John, c. 19).
10058{_}3011{_}3.
10058{_}3012{_}"And they, putting a sponge full of vinegar about hyssop, put it to His mouth" (St. John, c. 19).
10058{_}3013{_}_General Intentions_.
10058{_}3014{_}The conversion of sinners; the wants of the Church; those in death agony; spread of Eucharistic devotion; daily Communion; priest adorers; reparation for bad Communions; reparation for impieties and irreverences towards the Eucharist.
10058{_}3015{_}_Personal Intentions_.
10058{_}3016{_}Regularity in visits to Blessed Sacrament; Fervour in Mass and in administering Holy Communion; a happy death; true and deep devotion to Mary.
10058{_}3017{_}_Special Intentions_.
10058{_}3018{_}The Irish Daily Mass Crusade; Total Abstinence; devotion to the Passion; devotion to the agonising Heart of Jesus.
10058{_}3019{_}COMPLINE.
10058{_}3020{_}_Etymology and synonym_.
10058{_}3021{_}The word compline comes from the Latin word _complere_, to complete, to finish, because this Hour completes or finishes the day Hours of the Office.
10058{_}3022{_}It bore several names, _Completa_ (St. Isidore), _Initium noctis_ (St. Columbanus), _Prima noctis hora_ (St. Fructeux).
10058{_}3023{_}_Antiquity_.
10058{_}3024{_}The origin of this Hour has given rise to a great deal of controversy.
10058{_}3025{_}Both Baumer and Battifol in their histories of the Breviary attribute the origin of this Hour to St. Benedict (480-543).
10058{_}3026{_}Other scholars attribute its origin to St.
10058{_}3027{_}Basil, and hence date it from the fourth century.
10058{_}3028{_}It is admitted that before the time of St.
10058{_}3029{_}Basil, Bishop of Caesarea (370-379) this Hour was in existence.
10058{_}3030{_}Some hold that St.
10058{_}3031{_}Basil established the Hour in the East and St. Benedict in the West.
10058{_}3032{_}The latter certainly invested the Hour with the liturgical character and arrangement which were preserved by the Benedictines and adapted by the Roman Church.
10058{_}3033{_}The Compline of the Roman Church is more ornate and solemn than the liturgy assigned to this Hour by St. Benedict, which was very simple.
10058{_}3034{_}The addition of the response _In manus tuas Domine_, the _Nunc dimittis_ and its anthem of the Blessed Virgin make this Hour one of great beauty.
10058{_}3035{_}_Structure_, The structure of the Hour seems to point to its monastic origin, "The reader begins, 'Pray, Father, a blessing' (jube, domne benedicere); the blessing, 'The Lord Almighty grant us a quiet night and a perfect end.
10058{_}3036{_}Amen.'
10058{_}3037{_}'Noctem quietam....' Then follows a short lesson, which the Father Abbot gave to his monks.
10058{_}3038{_}'Brethren, be sober and watch; because your adversary, the devil, as a roaring lion, goeth about, seeking whom he may devour, whom resist ye, strong in faith.
10058{_}3039{_}But Thou, O Lord, have mercy on us.'
10058{_}3040{_}And the monks answer 'Thanks be to God.'
10058{_}3041{_}'Fratres sobrii estote et vigilate....' Then the _Pater Noster_ (silently), and the presiding priest, who was the Abbot or his deputy, said the confiteor and the choir answered _Misereatur_.... 'May Almighty God have mercy upon thee and forgive thee thy sins, and bring thee to life everlasting.'
10058{_}3042{_}The choir then repeats the Confiteor and the priest replies 'Misereatur vestri....' 'May Almighty God have mercy upon you, forgive you your sins and bring you to life everlasting.'"
10058{_}3043{_}Of course, in private recitation, or where two or three recite the Office, these prayers are said only once, and in the Confiteor, _tibi pater_ and _te pater_ are omitted, and _nostri, nostris, nos, nostrorum, nobis_, are said in the Misereatur and Indulgentiam.
10058{_}3044{_}Then the _Converte nos Deus.... At averte iram tuam.... Deus in adjutorium.... Domine ad adjuvandum.... Gloria Patri_.... Antiphon (begun only) and three psalms, which vary, are said, _Gloria Patri_.... _Sicut erat_... being said at the end of each.
10058{_}3045{_}_In manus tuas_... is said twice.
10058{_}3046{_}_Redemisti nos_.
10058{_}3047{_}... _Commendo spiritum meum_; _Custodi nos_ ... _sub umbra_.... _Salva nos_; _Nunc dimittis_.... _Gloria Patri, Salva nos Domine vigilantes, custodi nos_... _pace_.
10058{_}3048{_}(Preces are said here if rubric orders; i.e., _Kyrie eleison, Christie eleison_... _ad te veniat_); _Dominus vobiscum, Et cum_.... _Benedicamus Domino, Deo gratias_; _Benedicat et custodiat nos omnipotens_.
10058{_}3049{_}Amen; then the anthem of the Blessed Virgin, _Alma Redemptoris Mater_ (from Saturday before first Sunday of Advent to the feast of the Purification, inclusive) with its antiphon; in Advent, _Angelus Domini_, response, _Et concepit_, Oremus and prayer, _Gratiam tuam_, or with antiphon (after Advent) _Post partum_... and response, _Dei genetrix, Oremus, Deus qui salutis_.
10058{_}3050{_}After the Purification, until Holy Thursday the anthem is _Ave regina coelorum_, with versicle _Dignare me_ ..., _Da mihi_, Oremus, _Concedemisericors_.
10058{_}3051{_}From Holy Saturday until Saturday after Pentecost, the anthem is _Regina coeli_ with versicle, _Gaude_... and response, _Quia surrexit_.... _Oremus_ and prayer, _Deus qui per resurrectionem_.
10058{_}3052{_}From Holy Trinity Sunday to the Saturday before Advent, the antiphon is _Salve Regina_ with versicle, _Ora pro nobis_... response, _Ut digni, Oremus_ and prayer, _Omnipotens semipeterne Deus_.
10058{_}3053{_}Then the versicle _Divinum auxilium_.... Amen.
10058{_}3054{_}_Pater Noster, Ave, Credo_, in silence, are said.
10058{_}3055{_}The _Sacro-sanctae_ is added (see pp.
10058{_}3056{_}133-135).
10058{_}3057{_}The study of the component parts of this Hour are of great interest.
10058{_}3058{_}After the Abbot had given his blessing and begged of God to grant the two-fold favour of a quiet night and a good death, a monk read from Holy Scripture, and when a suitable portion was read, or at the end of a Scripture chapter or theme, the Abbot said, "Tu autem," and the reader "Tu autem, Domine, miserere nobis."
10058{_}3059{_}This was to ask God to pardon faults both of reader in his reading and of monks, who, perhaps, were drowsy and inattentive.
10058{_}3060{_}The Abbot terminated the exercise by the _Adjutorium nostrum_ (the _Pater Noster_ is of more recent introduction).
10058{_}3061{_}Monks who were absent substituted for the Scripture lesson which they had missed, the pithy extract from St. Peter, "Fratres; sobrii estote," which we now read.
10058{_}3062{_}The whole company of monks and their abbot then proceeded to the chapel where each made his examination of conscience, and at a sign from the abbot, the monks, two by two, in a subdued tone of voice, said the _Confiteor, Misereatur, Indulgentiam_ and _Converte nos_.
10058{_}3063{_}Gavantus and Merati hold that the _Converte nos_ does not belong to this introductory matter, but formed part of Compline proper.
10058{_}3064{_}This prayer is very beautiful: "Convert us, O God, our Saviour.
10058{_}3065{_}And turn away Thine anger from us.
10058{_}3066{_}Incline unto my aid, O God; O Lord, make haste to help us.
10058{_}3067{_}Glory be to the Father,...
10058{_}3068{_}Praise be to God."
10058{_}3069{_}The new arrangement of the Psalter did not retain the old traditional psalms, 4, 90, 133, in Compline, except for Sundays and solemn feasts.
10058{_}3070{_}But the selection of psalms accords well with the idea of the hour--night prayer--and with the other prayers, which go to make up the close of the Office of the day.
10058{_}3071{_}The hymn, _Te lucis_, so chastely simple, has ever been admired.
10058{_}3072{_}Its ideas suit so admirably for the prayer before sleep and for reminding us of sleep and her sister death and the solemn petition made to God to be our guardian and defence in the solemn hour of death, are simply and solemnly set out in this daily hymn.
10058{_}3073{_}How beautiful it reads in Father Caswall's translation:--       "Now with the fast departing light,       Maker of all, we ask of Thee       Of Thy great mercy, through the night,       Our guardian and defence to be.
10058{_}3074{_}Far off let idle visions fly,       No phantom of the night molest:       Curb Thou our raging enemy,       That we in chaste repose may rest.
10058{_}3075{_}Father of mercies!
10058{_}3076{_}hear our cry;       Hear us, O sole-begotten Son!
10058{_}3077{_}Who, with the Holy Ghost most high,       Reignest while endless ages run."
10058{_}3078{_}In Passiontide, the Breviary gives us the last verse, Deo Patri, and the translation renders it:--       "To Thee, Who dead again dost live,       All glory, Jesus, ever be,       Praise to the Father, infinite,       And Holy Ghost eternally."
10058{_}3079{_}_Little Chapter_.
10058{_}3080{_}This is a beautiful call to our Lord to remind Him, as it were, that we are His own, that we bear His name.
10058{_}3081{_}In this invocation we express our confidence in Him and ask Him not to abandon us, but to dwell with us.
10058{_}3082{_}"But Thou, O Lord, art among us, and Thy holy name is invoked upon us; forsake us not, O Lord our God"; and for past protection the Church adds to their invocation, taken from the prophet Jeremias, the words of gratitude, "Thanks be to God."
10058{_}3083{_}_The Response_.
10058{_}3084{_}"In manus tuas, Domine, commendo spiritum meum...
10058{_}3085{_}nos."
10058{_}3086{_}"Into Thy hands, O Lord, I commend my spirit.
10058{_}3087{_}Into Thy hands I commend my spirit.
10058{_}3088{_}For Thou hast redeemed us, O Lord God of Truth.
10058{_}3089{_}I commend my spirit.
10058{_}3090{_}Glory be to the Father and to the Son and to the Holy Ghost.
10058{_}3091{_}Into Thy hands, O Lord, I commend my spirit.
10058{_}3092{_}Keep us, O Lord, as the apple of Thine eye.
10058{_}3093{_}Protect us under the shadow of Thy wings."
10058{_}3094{_}No more sublime prayer exists in the liturgy than this response, which the Church orders us to say nightly.
10058{_}3095{_}She wishes, in its daily recital, to prepare us for death, by reminding us of the sentiments and words of our dying Lord on the cross, "Into Thy hands I commend my spirit" (Ps.
10058{_}3096{_}30, v. 6), and by asking Him Who redeemed us on the bitter tree, to keep us safe as the apple of His eye and to protect us "under the shadow of His wings" (Ps.
10058{_}3097{_}40, v, 6).
10058{_}3098{_}These solemn words of our dying Saviour have been, in all ages, and in all lands, the death prayer of many of those whom He redeemed, with the great price.
10058{_}3099{_}St. Stephen, the proto-martyr, prayed "Lord Jesus receive my spirit."
10058{_}3100{_}"Into Thy hands I commend my spirit," prayed St.
10058{_}3101{_}Basil in his death agony.
10058{_}3102{_}"Into Thy hands I commend my spirit," prayed thousands of God's servants, heroes and heroines, e.g., Savanarola, Columbus, Father Southwell, the martyr Mary, Queen of Scots, and countless other servants of God.
10058{_}3103{_}_Nunc Dimittis_.
10058{_}3104{_}The canticle _Nunc dimittis_ is the last in historical sequence of the three great canticles of the New Testament.
10058{_}3105{_}It was spoken at the presentation of Christ, by Simeon, "This man was just and devout, waiting for the consolation of Israel; and the Holy Ghost was in him.
10058{_}3106{_}And he had received an answer from the Holy Ghost, that he should not see death before he had seen the Christ of the Lord.
10058{_}3107{_}And he came by the spirit into the temple.
10058{_}3108{_}And when His parents brought the child Jesus to do for him according to the custom of the law.
10058{_}3109{_}He also took Him in his arms and blessed God and said 'Now thou dost dismiss thy servant, O Lord, according to thy word in peace....'" (St. Luke ii.
10058{_}3110{_}29-33).
10058{_}3111{_}This sublime canticle uttered by the holy old man at the close of his days is placed fittingly in the priest's Office at the close of the day.
10058{_}3112{_}It breathes his thanks, expresses his love and his wish to die, having seen the Saviour.
10058{_}3113{_}Before the canticle are said the opening words of the antiphon, "Salva nos"; and it is repeated in full at the end.
10058{_}3114{_}"Save us, O Lord, while we are awake, and guard us when we sleep, that we may watch with Christ and rest in peace."
10058{_}3115{_}The prayers, Kyrie eleison, Christie eleison, etc., are said always except when a double office or a day within an octave has been commemorated at Vespers.
10058{_}3116{_}The prayer, _Visita quaesumus_ is found in Breviaries of the thirteenth century and was introduced probably by the Friars Minor.
10058{_}3117{_}The words _habitationem istam_ are said to indicate that it is a prayer not only for the chapel of the friars, but for their dwellings on journeys.
10058{_}3118{_}It was said in choir by the abbot or presiding priest.
10058{_}3119{_}Like all prayers for Compline it begs God to drive far away the snares of the enemy; it begs Him to let His angels dwell in that house to keep the dwellers therein, in peace; and finally, it begs Him to "let Thy blessing be always upon us.
10058{_}3120{_}Through Jesus Christ, Thy Son, our Lord, Who liveth and reigneth with Thee in the unity of the Holy Ghost, God, world without end.
10058{_}3121{_}Amen."
10058{_}3122{_}After the Dominus vobiscum and its response, the abbot or presiding priest gave the solemn blessing "Benedicat et custodiet..., May the Almighty and merciful Lord, the Father, the Son and the Holy Ghost, bless and preserve us.
10058{_}3123{_}Amen."
10058{_}3124{_}Then one of the anthems of the Blessed Virgin Mary is said.
10058{_}3125{_}From the Saturday before Advent until the feast of the Purification, inclusive, is said the anthem "Alma Redemptoris Mater"; translated by Father Caswall, it reads:--       "Mother of Christ, hear Thou thy people's cry,       Star of the deep and portal of the sky,       Mother of Him who Thee from nothing made,       Sinking we strive and call to Thee for aid.
10058{_}3126{_}Oh, by that joy which Gabriel brought to Thee,       Thou Virgin first and last, let us Thy mercy see."
10058{_}3127{_}The Latin hexameters are attributed to Hermanus (circa 1054).
10058{_}3128{_}It has been translated by several poets great and small, and is well known in Newman's translation, "Kindly Mother of the Redeemer."
10058{_}3129{_}It was a popular hymn in Norman Ireland and in Catholic England, as we see in Chaucer's "Prioress's Tale."
10058{_}3130{_}After this anthem are said its versicle, response, and prayer _Oremus, Gratiam tuam quaesumus_.
10058{_}3131{_}From the first Vespers of the Nativity, the versicle, response and prayer said are "Post partum ...; Dei Genetrix.... Oremus, Deus qui salutis."
10058{_}3132{_}... From the end of Compline on February 2nd until Holy Thursday exclusive the antiphon is "Ave Regina coelorum."
10058{_}3133{_}It appears to be of monastic origin, and St. Jerome attributes it to St. Ephraem.
10058{_}3134{_}Its expressions are borrowed from the works of St. Ephraem, of St. Athanasius and of other doctors, and its theme is Mary, as Queen of Heaven, the dawn of our salvation, and an extolling of her beauty.
10058{_}3135{_}From Compline of Holy Saturday, inclusive, until None of the Saturday after the feast of Pentecost, inclusive, the "Regina coeli" is said.
10058{_}3136{_}It is a very old composition, but its author is unknown.
10058{_}3137{_}Some authors attribute it to St. Gregory the Great (590-604).
10058{_}3138{_}Others, following a venerable tradition, say that the three first lines were the composition of angels, and the fourth, Ora pro nobis Deum, alleluia, was added by Pope Gregory.
10058{_}3139{_}The legend tells us that when in the year 596 Rome was desolated by the plague, Pope Gregory the Great exhorted his people to penance and prayer, and carrying in his hands the picture of the Blessed Virgin, said to be painted by St. Luke, he led them in procession to the church, Afa Coeli, on Easter morn.
10058{_}3140{_}When the procession was passing Adrian's Mole, angel voices were heard chanting the Regina Coeli, and the Pope astonished and rejoiced added the words "Ora pro nobis Deum, Alleluia," and immediately a shining angel appeared and sheathed his sword, the plague ceased on that very day (Gueranger, _Liturgical Year_, "Paschal Time," Part I., p. iii; Duffy, Dublin).
10058{_}3141{_}Attempts at translation have been indifferent.
10058{_}3142{_}From the first Vespers of the feast of the Most Hoiy Trinity to the None of the Saturday before Advent, the Salve Regina is said.
10058{_}3143{_}The authorship was assigned to St. Bernard (1091-1153).
10058{_}3144{_}But scholars reject this theory.
10058{_}3145{_}It is assigned to Petrus de Monsoro (circa 1000) and to Adehemar, but the claims of both are doubtful.
10058{_}3146{_}In 1220 the general chapter of Cluny ordered its daily chanting before the high altar, after the Capitulum.
10058{_}3147{_}The use of the anthem at Compline was begun by the Dominicans about 1221 and the practice spread rapidly.
10058{_}3148{_}It was introduced into the "modernised."
10058{_}3149{_}Franciscan Breviary in the thirteenth century.
10058{_}3150{_}The Carthusians sing it daily at Vespers; the Cistercians sing it after Compline, and the Carmelites say it after every Hour of the Office.
10058{_}3151{_}It is said after every low Mass throughout the world.
10058{_}3152{_}It was especially obnoxious to Luther, who several times denounced it, as did the Jansenists also.
10058{_}3153{_}It is recorded in the lives of several saints that the Blessed Virgin, to show her love for this beautiful prayer, showed to them her Son, at the moment they said "Et Jesum ... nobis post hoc exilium ostende."
10058{_}3154{_}Speaking of these antiphons of the Blessed Virgin, Battifol, in his _History of the Roman Breviary_ (English ed.
10058{_}3155{_}), writes: "We owe a just debt of gratitude to those who gave us the antiphons of the Blessed Virgin ... four exquisite compositions, though in style enfeebled by sentimentality."
10058{_}3156{_}After the antiphon of the Blessed Virgin the versicle and response are said.
10058{_}3157{_}Then Oremus and prayer "Omnipotens sempiterne Deus ... Divinum auxilium ... Amen," are said.
10058{_}3158{_}Then the Pater Noster, Ave and Credo are said silently, and this finishes the Hour.
10058{_}3159{_}The prayer Sacro-sanctae et individuae.... V. Beata viscera ... R. Et beata ubera ... Pater Noster and Ave are generally added though not of obligation.
10058{_}3160{_}They are to be said kneeling.
10058{_}3161{_}The reading of this well-known and oft-repeated prayer, in its English translation, may bring fresh and fervent thoughts to priests, for it is a sublime prayer:--       "To the most holy and undivided Trinity, to the       humanity of our Lord Jesus Christ crucified, to the       fruitful virginity of the most glorious Mary ever a       Virgin, and to the company of all the saints, be       given by every creature, eternal praise, honour,       power and glory, and to us the remission of all our       sins.
10058{_}3162{_}Amen.
10058{_}3163{_}Blessed be the womb of the Virgin       Mary, which bore the Son of the Eternal Father.
10058{_}3164{_}And blessed be the breasts which gave suck to       Christ our Lord."
10058{_}3165{_}TEXT AND INTENTIONS TO AID THE PIOUS RECITATION OF COMPLINE.
10058{_}3166{_}1.
10058{_}3167{_}"Into Thy hands, O Lord, I commend my spirit."
10058{_}3168{_}2.
10058{_}3169{_}"It is finished."
10058{_}3170{_}3.
10058{_}3171{_}"For this Thou hast redeemed us, O God of truth."
10058{_}3172{_}_General Intentions._ The spread of the faith; the Pope; the Church in France and in Spain; for the Church in Australia.
10058{_}3173{_}_Personal Intentions._ A happy death; fervour in administering the last sacraments; devotion to St. Joseph, patron of a happy death.
10058{_}3174{_}_Special Intentions,_ For the sick poor of Ireland; for persons dying without the last sacraments; for those dying all alone; for dying sinners.
10058{_}3175{_}THE LITTLE OFFICE OF THE BLESSED VIRGIN, (TITLE XXXVII.).
10058{_}3176{_}_Origin._ This Office dates from the eighth century at least.
10058{_}3177{_}Pope Gregory II.
10058{_}3178{_}(715-731) and Pope Gregory III.
10058{_}3179{_}(731-741) ordered the monks to say this little Office in addition to their great Office.
10058{_}3180{_}The practice was observed by St. John Damascene (676-787) and by St. Peter Damien (1007-1072).This usage was confined to monasteries only.
10058{_}3181{_}At the end of the eleventh century the practice became almost universal.
10058{_}3182{_}Pope Urban II.
10058{_}3183{_}(1088-1099) besought the special aid of the Blessed Virgin in his crusade against the Turks and recommended all clerics to recite the little Office.
10058{_}3184{_}Provincial councils prescribed its use and some canonists held it to be obligatory.
10058{_}3185{_}However, the Bull _Quod a nobis_ of Pope Pius V. (9 July, 1568) removed all obligation of the private recital of this Office, but he exhorted all to continue the practice and granted indulgences for its recitation.
10058{_}3186{_}PART IV.
10058{_}3187{_}NOTES ON SOME FEASTS.
10058{_}3188{_}CHAPTER I.
10058{_}3189{_}PROPER OF THE TIME.
10058{_}3190{_}ADVENT.
10058{_}3191{_}_Advent_ (Latin, _advenire_, to come to) is a period beginning with the Sunday nearest to the Feast of St. Andrew (November 30) and embracing four Sundays.
10058{_}3192{_}In the early Church there was a divergence of date and practice in Advent celebration.
10058{_}3193{_}Thus, in France it began on St. Martin's Day (11 November) and ended with Christmas, France kept Advent with tri-weekly fasts.
10058{_}3194{_}Rome did not, in very early days, observe the Advent fasts, but maintained the shorter period, containing only four Sundays.
10058{_}3195{_}(Father Thurston, _The Month_, No.
10058{_}3196{_}498).
10058{_}3197{_}Several authors stated that this period of preparation for the celebration of Christ's birthday was instituted by Gregory the Great.
10058{_}3198{_}It is now traceable to the fourth century in France; in Rome it was of later date.
10058{_}3199{_}The Church, as is seen in the Advent Offices in the Breviary, instituted this part of the liturgical year to honour and to recall the two comings of Christ--His first coming in human form at Bethlehem, as Saviour; and His second coming, as Judge of all mankind.
10058{_}3200{_}In her liturgy she expresses repeatedly both sentiments, a sentiment of joy and a sentiment of sorrow.
10058{_}3201{_}The former she expresses by her _alleluias_ and the latter by her omission of the _Te Deum_ and by her recital of the ferial prayers, the prayers of tears and grief.
10058{_}3202{_}In the Advent Offices are many phrases which were fulfilled at the Incarnation: "Rorate coeli desuper et nubes pluant Justum; O Adonai, veni ad redimendum nos; Emitte Agnum, Domine, Dominatorum terrae; Orietur sicut sol Salvator mundi et descendet in uterum Virginis."
10058{_}3203{_}Centuries have passed since the Saviour came, and yet the Church wishes us to repeat the sublime prayers and prophecies which associate themselves with the coming of the Word made Flesh, and by our repetition to be animated with the ardent longings of olden days; and that by them we may awaken our faith, our hope, our charity, and obtain and augment God's grace in our souls.
10058{_}3204{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}3205{_}The first Sunday of Advent has the invitatory hymn and the rest of the Office proper.
10058{_}3206{_}The lessons are from Isaias, the prophet of the Incarnation.
10058{_}3207{_}The first response to the lesson is unique in the Breviary for it has three verses (see p. 164).
10058{_}3208{_}These three verses are spoken in the names of the holy people who lived before the law, during the law, and after the law.
10058{_}3209{_}The Gloria Patri is added to honour the Holy Trinity, who has at length sent the long-watched-for Messias (Durandus).
10058{_}3210{_}And the response is repeated from the beginning because the second coming of Christ is watched for, by His faithful (Honorius d'Autun).
10058{_}3211{_}The _Te Deum_ is not said, in order thereby to mark the sad thought of the second coming of Christ, then our judge.
10058{_}3212{_}_Lessons_.
10058{_}3213{_}From the first Sunday of Advent until the first Sunday of August the lessons of the first and second nocturns are given in the Breviary in the Proprium de Tempore, after the Psaltery.
10058{_}3214{_}The lessons of third nocturn for same period are given after those of second nocturn.
10058{_}3215{_}The suffrages are not said in Advent.
10058{_}3216{_}In Advent the lectio brevis is "Domine miserere."
10058{_}3217{_}In Sunday Matins special versicles are given.
10058{_}3218{_}The preces are said at Lauds and Vespers in ferias of Advent and at the small Hours; preces are said, too, if they be said at Lauds.
10058{_}3219{_}The great antiphons are the antiphons of the Magnificat which begin on the 17th December.
10058{_}3220{_}They are sometimes called the great O's, or the O antiphons, as each begins with this letter.
10058{_}3221{_}They begin "O Sapientia, quae ex ore Altissimi prodiisti ..." and continue "O Adonai, O radix Jesse," etc....
10058{_}3222{_}They are the most beautiful antiphons in the liturgy, expressing the prayers and ardent hopes for the coming Saviour.
10058{_}3223{_}They have formed the subjects of study for poets, scholars and liturgists, ancient and modern.
10058{_}3224{_}It is asked why these antiphons introduce the Magnificat and not the Benedictus.
10058{_}3225{_}And liturgists reply: Because the Incarnation was of Mary, and hence these heralds of the Infant King more appropriately introduce Mary's canticle rather than that of Zachary.
10058{_}3226{_}And the old liturgists add that these antiphons are said at Vespers, the evening Hour, because the Messias was expected and watched for in the world's evening.
10058{_}3227{_}They tell us, too, why there are seven great antiphons.
10058{_}3228{_}They are to excite our piety during this octave preparatory to the birthday of Christ.
10058{_}3229{_}This number seven typifies the seven gifts of the Holy Ghost; it represents the seven miseries of mankind, ignorance, eternal punishment, the slavery of the devil, sin, gloom and exile from our fatherland, which is Heaven.
10058{_}3230{_}And those wonderful men of mediaeval days tell us why we have need of a Teacher, O Sapientia; of a Redeemer, O Adonai; of a Liberator, O Radix Jesse; of a Guardian, O Clavis David; of a brilliant Instructor, O Oriens; of a Saviour to bring us, Gentiles, back to our Great Father, God; O Rex gentium; a Herald to the Jews.
10058{_}3231{_}Honorius of Autun tells that these antiphons refer to the seven gifts of the Holy Ghost and are arranged in the well-known order in which these gifts are always arranged in works of piety.
10058{_}3232{_}He says that Christ came in the Spirit of Wisdom, O Sapientia, that in the word "Adonai" is indicated that Christ redeemed us in the Spirit of Understanding.
10058{_}3233{_}He says, too, that the antiphon "O Radix" signifies the sign of the cross, and that Christ redeemed us in the Spirit of Counsel.
10058{_}3234{_}"O Clavis" indicates that Christ opened Heaven and closed Hell in the Spirit of Strength or Fortitude.
10058{_}3235{_}"O Orient" shows forth Christ enlightening us in the Spirit of Knowledge.
10058{_}3236{_}"Rex gentiam" points out the holy King who saved men by the Spirit of Piety.
10058{_}3237{_}"O Emanuel" refers to Christ coming in the Spirit of Fear, but giving us also the Law of Love.
10058{_}3238{_}These antiphons have formed the theme of the oldest Christian poem in Europe--Cynewulf's "Christ," a work which is the admiration of modern scholars.
10058{_}3239{_}They were celebrated with great pomp and joy in monastic life, the monks carrying their congruous symbolism into their recitation.
10058{_}3240{_}For, to the gardener-monk was assigned, the chanting of "O Radix Jesse," and to the cellarer-monk, the "O clavis David"--typifying their work of root-growing and key keeping.
10058{_}3241{_}(See _The Month_, No.
10058{_}3242{_}489; _The Irish Ecclesiastical Record_, December, 1918).
10058{_}3243{_}_Christmas.
10058{_}3244{_}Antiquity._ "It was formerly taken for granted that Christ had actually been born on this day, and, accordingly, the learned were of opinion that the Church had observed it from the beginning, as the day of His birth.
10058{_}3245{_}Even at the present day it will be dfficult for many to give up this idea.
10058{_}3246{_}But there is no Christmas among the Christan feasts enumerated by Tertullian ([died] 220), Origen (185-254), and the recently published Testament of Jesus Christ.
10058{_}3247{_}On the contrary, there is clear proof that even in the fourth and fifth centuries it was unknown in some parts of the Church, where its introduction, at a later period, can be proved historically" (_vide_ Kellner, _op.
10058{_}3248{_}cit._, pp.
10058{_}3249{_}127-158).
10058{_}3250{_}Christmas is one of the great festivals.
10058{_}3251{_}In Rome there were two night Offices.
10058{_}3252{_}The first, celebrated at nightfall in the Papal chapel, begins with the antiphon of the first psalm in the nocturn.
10058{_}3253{_}It has nine lessons and the _Te Deum_.
10058{_}3254{_}About midnight a more solemn Office began, this time with the invitatory and psalm _Venite_.
10058{_}3255{_}The first of these Offices became the Office of the vigil.
10058{_}3256{_}In the Office of Christmas Day the lessons are read without the title of the book (Isaias) from which they are taken, because their author's name was so often repeated during the Advent that each one knew their source, or because at Christmas God speaks to us by His Son, rather than by His prophet.
10058{_}3257{_}In the first response the Gloria Patri is said, to thank God for the great favour He has bestowed on us--His Son, the Christ.
10058{_}3258{_}In the third nocturn, _Alleluia_ is added to the antiphons, because the third nocturn typifies the time of grace, in which we should express the joy that is ours in the birth of the Saviour.
10058{_}3259{_}In this nocturn, too, are given three Gospel extracts, corresponding with the Gospels in the Mass of Christmas.
10058{_}3260{_}Matins are separated from Lauds by the first Mass because, it is said at midnight, and Lauds is a day Office.
10058{_}3261{_}At Prime the versicle of the little response is _Qui natus est_.
10058{_}3262{_}_Rubrics_.
10058{_}3263{_}Christmas is a primary double of the First Class.
10058{_}3264{_}The third of the new _Tres Tabellae_ (S.C.R., January, 1912) in the new Breviaries gives the rules for concurrence of Vespers in the Octave of Christmas.
10058{_}3265{_}_Feast of St. Stephen_.
10058{_}3266{_}The worship of St. Stephen may be said to be as old as the Church herself, since St. Paul gave him the title of Martyr of Christ (Acts XXII.
10058{_}3267{_}20).
10058{_}3268{_}His name is to be found in the earliest liturgical sources, e.g., the Arian martyrology belonging to about 360 and in all calendars, ancient and modern, excepting the Coptic.
10058{_}3269{_}His cultus received great impulse from the discovery of his relics at Kaphar Gamala, on the shore of Lake Genesareth, and the wonderful miracles wrought by them, A basilica in his honour was erected, in Rome in the fourth century.
10058{_}3270{_}_St.
10058{_}3271{_}John the Apostle_.
10058{_}3272{_}The commemoration of St. John on the 27th December was formerly united with that of St. James the Less.
10058{_}3273{_}In time, St. John's feast only was celebrated on this date, and such was the case as early as the time of Bede.
10058{_}3274{_}_The Circumcision._ This festival was originally called _Octava Domini_, and hence it may be inferred that it was not an independent festival and passed unnoticed if it fell on a week day.
10058{_}3275{_}Thus, in the _Homilarium_ of Charlemagne (786) it is referred to by this name.
10058{_}3276{_}But very shortly after this, the name which we now use for the festival of the 1st January was used in Rome, and spread through the Church.
10058{_}3277{_}In the early days of Christianity the first day of the civil year was given over to rejoicings, dancing, feasting and rioting.
10058{_}3278{_}And these abuses lingered in France, though stripped of their pagan character, until the later middle ages.
10058{_}3279{_}A remnant of them is found in the so-called Feast of Fools, which was held in churches, and which mocked several religious customs and ceremonies.
10058{_}3280{_}These feasts lasted till the middle of the fifteenth century.
10058{_}3281{_}_Epiphany_.
10058{_}3282{_}The name is derived from a Greek verb employed to describe the dawn, and the adjective derived from the Greek verb was applied in classic Greek, to the appearances of the gods bringing help to men.
10058{_}3283{_}In Christian liturgy, the feast was instituted to celebrate the appearance, the manifestation of Christ, to the Gentiles, in the persons of the Magi.
10058{_}3284{_}In later times, there were added to this commemoration of Christ's manifestation to the Gentiles, two further commemorations of his wonderful showings of His divine mission, viz., His manifestation in His baptism in the Jordan, a manifestation to the Jews, and His miracle at Cana, a showing forth to His friends and disciples.
10058{_}3285{_}This feast is of early origin.
10058{_}3286{_}Suarez thinks it should be attributed to the Apostles (_De Relig_.
10058{_}3287{_}L.2.
10058{_}3288{_}ch.5, n.9); and Benedict XIV.
10058{_}3289{_}held that it was established by the infant Church at Rome to draw off the Christians from the profane and sinful revelry which marked the pagan feast of this date.
10058{_}3290{_}However, these statements are hardly accurate.
10058{_}3291{_}"With regard to the antiquity and spread of the feast, it was unknown in North Africa during the third century, for Tertullian makes no reference to it; and even in the time of St. Augustine, it was rejected by the Donatists as an oriental novelty.
